KASLOW, SERIES EDITOR \n\n"THE IMPORTANT THING IS NOT TO \n\nSTOP QUESTIONING." --Albert Einstein \n\nThe Art of the Question extends the range of cognitive-behavioral therapy by elaborating \n\non the ways that internal questions program thought, emotion, and behavior. Describing a groundbreaking, question-centered approach to therapy, the concepts \n\nand practices in this book are essential for facilitating successful counseling and ther- \n\napy. The Art of the Question contains everything therapists need add question- \n\ncentered methods to their therapeutic toolkits. \n\ntion-centered \n\n0 help clients \n\nces, and take \n\nng to solutions \n\nseativity, and lim- re often catalyzed \n\nThe question-driven nature of.Choice is one of the pillars of qu therapy. The Art of the Question offers practical psycho-educational to \n\ncreate better choices, be more effective in the ways they make c responsibility for their decisions. In this reflection on \n\nthe power of conversation and \n\ncollective wisdom Meg shares \n\nher perspective on the unique \n\ncontribution of the World Café \n\nto our common future. \n\nF O R E W O R D \n\nWe Can Be Wise Only Together By Margaret J. Wheatley \n\nIn this troubling time when many people are so disconnected \n\nfrom one another, I keep searching to find those ideas, \n\nprocesses, and behaviors that can restore hope for the future. The \n\nWorld Café does just that. The stories told in these pages by its \n\npractitioners from all over the world demonstrate that it is possi-\n\nble for people to find meaning, even joy, in working together. \n\nAnd that through our conversations, as we work together, we \n\ndiscover a greater wisdom that reveals our path forward. \n\nThe World Café reintroduces us to a world we have forgotten. \n\nThis is a world where people naturally congregate because we \n\nwant to be together. A world where we enjoy the age-old process \n\nof good conversation, where we're not afraid to talk about things \n\nthat matter most to us. A world where we're not separated, classi-\n\nfied, or stereotyped. A world of simple greeting, free from tech-\n\nnology and artificiality. A world that constantly surprises us with \n\nthe wisdom that exists not in any one of us but in all of us. And \n\na world where we learn that the wisdom we need to solve our \n\nproblems is available when we talk together. \n\nThis world has been forgotten by us, but it has never aban-\n\ndoned us. For several years, David Isaacs, co-originator of the Café \n\nprocess, has said that our work is to remember this world, that \n\nwe don't need to create it. From what I observe in many places, \n\nhowever, it appears that our memory of how to work together in \n\nhealthy, productive ways has been nearly extinguished by the \n\ncreeping complexity of group work, facilitation techniques, \n\nobscure analytic processes, and our own exhaustion. People are \n\nmore polarized, more overwhelmed, more impatient, more easily \n\ndisappointed in others, and more withdrawn than ever. We're \n\nfrustrated by the increasing number of problems that confront us \n\nand our impotence to resolve even the most simple ones. And no \n\nsane person wants to participate in yet another meeting or get\n\ninvolved with yet another problem-solving process, because these \n\nthings only increase our frustration and impotence. \n\nPerhaps the most pernicious consequence of this memory \n\nloss is our growing belief that humans are a difficult, self-serving \n\nspecies and that we cannot trust each other. As this negative belief \n\ngrows stronger, we remove ourselves and focus only on work that \n\nwe can do on our own. We pay attention to the work in front of \n\nus, and thus lose any appreciation of the whole system. Isolated \n\nand alone, we lose courage and capacity; our work loses meaning \n\nand we end up with unending fatigue and loneliness. \n\nThe World Café process reawakens our deep species memory of \n\ntwo fundamental beliefs about human life. First, we humans want to \n\ntalk together about things that matter to us. In fact, this is what gives \n\nsatisfaction and meaning to life. Second, as we talk together, we are \n\nable to access a greater wisdom that is found only in the collective. \n\nThe World Café in Action \n\nAs you read the stories and counsel in this book, you will see these two \n\nbeliefs brought to life in the Café process. In order to provoke your \n\nexploration of them, I'd like to underline some of the dimensions of \n\nthe Café process that bring these beliefs into vibrant, healthy reality. \n\nBelief in Everybody The World Café is a good, simple process for bringing people \n\ntogether around questions that matter. It is founded on the \n\nassumption that people have the capacity to work together, no \n\nmatter who they are. For me, this is a very important assumption. \n\nIt frees us from our current focus on personality types, learning \n\nstyles, emotional IQ--all the popular methods we currently use to \n\npre-identify and pre-judge people. Each of these typologies ends \n\nup separating and stereotyping people. This is not what was \n\nintended by their creators, but it is what has happened. \n\nThe Café process has been used in many different cultures, \n\namong many different age groups, for many different purposes, \n\nand in many different types of communities and organizations. \n\nIt doesn't matter who the people are--the process works. It works \n\nbecause people can work well together, can be creative and caring \n\nForeword ix \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nand insightful when they're actively engaged in meaningful con-\n\nversations around questions that count. I hope that these stories \n\ninspire us to move away from all the categories and stereotypes \n\nwe currently use about who should be involved, who should \n\nattend a meeting--all the careful but ill-founded analysis we put \n\ninto constructing the "right" group. We need to be focused on \n\ngathering the real diversity of the system, but that's quite different \n\nfrom being absorbed with these other sorting devices. \n\nDiversity It's important to notice the diversity of the places and purposes for \n\nwhich the World Café is used, and the diversity of participants \n\nwho are encouraged to attend World Café gatherings. These pages \n\ncontain a rich illustration of a value I live by: we need to depend \n\non diversity. Including diversity well is a survival skill these days, \n\nbecause there's no other way to get an accurate picture of any \n\ncomplex problem or system. We need many eyes and ears and \n\nhearts engaged in sharing perspectives. How can we create an accu-\n\nrate picture of the whole if we don't honor the fact that we each \n\nsee something different because of who we are and where we sit in \n\nthe system? Only when we have many different perspectives do we \n\nhave enough information to make good decisions. And exploring \n\nour differing perspectives always brings us closer together. One \n\nCafé member said it well: "You're moving among strangers, but it \n\nfeels as if you've known these people for a long time." \n\nInvitation In every World Café, there's a wonderful feeling of invitation. \n\nAttention is paid to creating hospitable space. But the hospitality \n\nruns much deeper. It is rooted in the host's awareness that every-\n\none is needed, that anyone might contribute something that \n\nsuddenly sparks a collective insight. Café facilitators are true \n\nhosts--creating a spirit of welcome that is missing from most of \n\nour processes. It's important to notice this in the stories here, and \n\nto contrast it with your own experience of setting up meetings \n\nand processes. What does it feel like to be truly wanted at an \n\nevent, to be greeted by meeting hosts who delight in your \n\npresence, to be welcomed in as a full contributor? \n\nx The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nListening When people are engaged in meaningful conversation, the whole \n\nroom reflects curiosity and delight. People move closer physically, \n\ntheir faces exhibit intense listening, and the air becomes charged \n\nwith their attention to each other. A loud, resonant quiet develops, \n\nbroken by occasional laughter. It becomes a challenge to call people \n\nback from these conversations (which I always take as a good sign). \n\nMovement In the World Café process, people generally move from table to \n\ntable. But it's much more than physical movement. As we move, we \n\nleave behind our roles, our preconceptions, our certainty. Each time \n\nwe move to a new table, we lose more of ourselves and become big-\n\nger--we now represent a conversation that happened among several \n\npeople. We move away from a confining sense of self and our small \n\ncertainties into a spaciousness where new ideas can reveal them-\n\nselves. As one participant describes it: "It's almost as if you don't \n\nknow where the thought came from because it has merged so many \n\ntimes that it has been molded and shaped and shifted with new \n\ndimensions. People are speaking for each other and using words \n\nthat started somewhere else that they hadn't thought of before." \n\nWe also move into a greater awareness as we look for connections \n\namongst the conversations, as we listen to voices other than our own. \n\nPatterns become apparent. Things we couldn't see from our own \n\nnarrow perspective suddenly become obvious to the entire group. \n\nGood Questions World Café dialogues, like all good conversations, succeed or fail \n\nbased on what we're talking about. Good questions--ones that \n\nwe care about and want to answer--call us outward and to each \n\nother. They are an invitation to explore, to venture out, to risk, to \n\nlisten, to abandon our positions. Good questions help us become \n\nboth curious and uncertain, and this is always the road that opens \n\nus to the surprise of new insight. \n\nEnergy I've never been in a World Café that was dull or boring. People \n\nbecome energized, inspired, excited, creative. Laughter is \n\ncommon, playfulness abounds even with the most serious of \n\nForeword xi \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nissues. For me this is proof positive of how much we relish being \n\ntogether, of how wonderful it is to rediscover the fact of human \n\ncommunity. As one host from a very formal culture says: "My \n\nfaith in people has been confirmed. Underneath all the formal \n\nways of the past, people really want to have significant conversa-\n\ntions. People everywhere truly love to talk with each other, learn \n\ntogether, and make a contribution to things they care about." \n\nDiscovering Collective Wisdom \n\nThese are some of the Café dimensions that bring out the best in \n\nus. But this is only half the story. World Café conversations take us \n\ninto a new realm, one that has been forgotten in modern, individ-\n\nualistic cultures. It is the realm of collective intelligence, of the wis-\n\ndom we possess as a group that is unavailable to us as individuals. \n\nThis wisdom emerges as we get more and more connected with \n\neach other, as we move from conversation to conversation, carry-\n\ning the ideas from one conversation to another, looking for pat-\n\nterns, suddenly surprised by an insight we all share. There's a good \n\nscientific explanation for this, because this is how all life works. As \n\nseparate ideas or entities become connected to each other, life sur-\n\nprises us with emergence--the sudden appearance of new capacity \n\nand intelligence. All living systems work in this way. We humans \n\ngot confused and lost sight of this remarkable process by which \n\nindividual actions, when connected, lead to much greater capacity. \n\nTo those of us raised in a linear world with our minds \n\nshrunken by detailed analyses, the sudden appearance of collec-\n\ntive wisdom always feels magical. I am fascinated by the descrip-\n\ntions given by Café participants of this emergence. Here are a few \n\nquotes from them. Notice how unusual these descriptions are: \n\n"The magic in the middle." \n\n"The voice in the center of the room." \n\n"The magic in experiencing our own and other people's humanity \n\naround whatever the content is." \n\n"Something coming to life in the middle of the table." \n\n"What joins us together--a larger whole that we always knew \n\nwas there, but never really appreciated." \n\nxii The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nFor me, the moments when collective wisdom appears are \n\nalways breathtaking. Even though I know such wisdom is bound to \n\nappear, I'm always stunned with delight when it enters the room. \n\nAnd the appearance of such wisdom is a huge relief. We actually do \n\nknow how to solve our problems! We can discover solutions that \n\nwork! We've just been looking in the wrong places--we've been \n\nlooking to experts, or external solutions, or to detailed, empty analy-\n\nses. And all this time, the wisdom has been waiting for us, waiting \n\nfor us to enter into meaningful conversations and deeper connec-\n\ntions, waiting for us to realize that we can be wise only together. \n\nOne last comment. One of the wonderful things about this \n\nbook is that it is designed to give an enticing taste of a World Café \n\nexperience; as much as is possible, it embodies what it describes. \n\nIn these pages, we are introduced to many strangers, diverse peo-\n\nple we don't know who may be doing work very different from \n\nour own. They relay stories of their many experiences in using the \n\nWorld Café. Their stories are compelling, and it's possible to feel \n\nas if we're sitting with them at an intimate café table, exchanging \n\ntales, learning from each other, moving closer. Then our gifted \n\nhost, Juanita, enters and warmly invites us to another level of \n\nlearning. She speaks in the World Café voice, inviting, curious, \n\ninquiring. With her guidance, we can see things that weren't clear, \n\nor discover concepts and tips that we can use in our own work. \n\nAnd as stories and learnings weave together, we can begin to \n\nnotice patterns and insights that weren't available to us before we \n\nopened the book. In the end, we too may experience broader \n\ninsight, wider wisdom, and the magic of collective thinking. \n\nI hope you will enjoy this book for all that it offers. I hope \n\nyou will read it, savor it, use it, and begin to host Café conversa-\n\ntions yourself. If enough of us do so, we can reintroduce many \n\npeople to a world where people enjoy working together, where \n\ncollaborative conversation yields true insight and new possibili-\n\nties for action, where work and life are revived with meaning and \n\npossibility. In this way, we truly can restore hope to the future. \n\nForeword xiii \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .\n\nThis page intentionally left blank \n\n1 I N T R O D U C T I O N \n\nBeginning the Conversation: An Invitation to the World Café \n\nIam a child of the sixties. During that time of social and politi-\n\ncal upheaval, many of us were determined to tell it like it is, to \n\nsee beneath the surface of things to what really mattered. That \n\ninner fire that fueled my early years as a social change activist is \n\nnow tempered by a compassion born of more than thirty years of \n\nworking intimately with the dilemmas and paradoxes of personal \n\nand institutional change in corporate settings. My self-righteous-\n\nness and certainty have slowly given way to a humility developed \n\nout of a growing sense that there are many ways to tell it like it \n\nis--that any story worth telling can be experienced from multiple \n\nperspectives. It is with this awareness that I share with you the \n\nstory of the learning journey from which the World Café has \n\nemerged and continues to evolve. \n\nWhen I was growing up in suburban South Miami, Florida, \n\nour living room and dinner table were always alive with conversa-\n\ntions. These weren't just any kind of conversations. They were \n\npassionate discussions about big questions--justice, democracy, \n\nand civil rights. From conversations like these in homes and \n\nchurches, the civil liberties movement in Florida was nurtured \n\nand grew into a force for decency and fairness at a time of great \n\nturmoil in the South. \n\nI remember, too, the spirited conversations we had at my \n\nadopted grandmother's home in southern Mexico when I was a \n\nteenager. Trudi Blom had been exiled from Europe during World \n\nWar II, and there, in the remote state of Chiapas, she founded a \n\nglobal center for dialogue and action on environmental issues-- \n\nmuch before it was fashionable to talk about sustainability. At her \n\nlong dining room table, anthropologists, writers, scientists, and \n\nlocal travelers joined together for delicious meals with Lacandon \n\nMaya rain forest people and Chamula highland Indian guests. The \n\ndiversity of the group always contributed to learning, discoveries, \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n2 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nand connections that never could have been anticipated. Today, \n\nhalf a century later, the Na-Bolom Center still serves as a place \n\nwhere diverse people and perspectives meet in dialogue around \n\nthe dining room table. \n\nDuring my early years as a community organizer with Cesar \n\nChavez and the farmworkers' movement, it was in the thousands \n\nof informal meetings--conversations among those seated on tat-\n\ntered couches in ramshackle homes and labor camps--that small \n\nmiracles occurred. Through dialogue and reflection, the underly-\n\ning assumptions that had kept farmworkers stuck for generations \n\nbegan to shift. As workers shared tortilla and bean suppers, they \n\nalso shared the if-onlys of their lives and imagined the impossible. \n\nWith practice, they began to ask the what-if questions. And from \n\nthe what-ifs came the why-nots! \n\nOver the last quarter-century, my life has taken me to large \n\ncorporations as a strategist and thinking partner with senior exec-\n\nutives as they struggle to embrace the challenges of the knowledge \n\nera. In this world, my language and descriptions have changed to \n\nthose of strategic dialogue and conversation as a core business \n\nprocess. My community-organizing emphasis has evolved to focus \n\non and embrace the informal communities of practice that are \n\nthe home for social processes of new learning and knowledge \n\ncreation. But the essential threads of my life remain unbroken. It \n\nis still my deepest belief that it is through conversations around \n\nquestions that matter that powerful capacities for evolving caring \n\ncommunity, collaborative learning, and committed action are \n\nengaged--at work, in communities, and at home. \n\nConversations That Matter \n\nThrough our conversations the stories and images of our future \n\nemerge, and never has this process been more critical. We now \n\nhave the capacity, through neglect of the planetary commons on \n\nwhich our lives depend, to make this precious earth, our home, \n\nuninhabitable. We now have the capacity, through escalating \n\nviolence and weapons of mass destruction, to make our precious \n\nhuman species, along with many others, extinct. Yet this is also a\n\nmoment of opportunity. We are connected as never before in \n\nwebs of communication and information-sharing through the \n\nInternet and other media that make our collective predicament \n\nvisible on a much larger scale than we could have imagined only \n\na few years ago. And for the first time, we now have the capacity \n\nfor engaging in connected global conversations and action about \n\nwhat is happening and how we choose to respond--conversations \n\nthat are not under the formal aegis of any one institution, govern-\n\nment, or corporation. It is time for us to engage in those conver-\n\nsations more intentionally. Our very survival as a human \n\ncommunity, both locally and globally, may rest on our creative \n\nresponses to the following questions: \n\n◗ How can we enhance our capacity to talk and think more \n\ndeeply together about the critical issues facing our communi-\n\nties, our organizations, our nations, and our planet? \n\n◗ How can we access the mutual intelligence and wisdom we \n\nneed to create innovative paths forward? \n\nThis book is the story of a personal and collective journey \n\nshaped by these questions. It is a story in which I have been an \n\nactive participant, along with my partner, David Isaacs, and a \n\nlively global community of inquiry and practice. It is the story \n\nof the discovery and evolution of the World Café, a simple yet \n\npowerful conversational process for fostering constructive dialogue, \n\naccessing collective intelligence, and creating innovative possibili-\n\nties for action, particularly in groups that are larger than most \n\ntraditional dialogue approaches are designed to accommodate. \n\nAnyone interested in creating conversations that matter can \n\nengage the World Café process, with its seven core design principles \n\nto improve people's collective capacity to share knowledge and \n\nshape the future together. World Café conversations simultane-\n\nously enable us to notice a deeper living pattern of connections at \n\nwork in our organizations and communities--the often invisible \n\nwebs of conversation and meaning-making through which we \n\nalready collectively shape the future, often in unintended ways. \n\nBeginning the Conversation 3 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n4 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nEngaging the World Café process, princi-\n\nples, and pattern in practical ways empowers \n\nleaders and others who work with groups \n\nto intentionally host World Café and other \n\ntypes of dialogue as well as to create \n\ndynamic networks of conversation and \n\nknowledge-sharing around an organiza-\n\ntion's real work and critical questions. \n\nHow Does a World Café Dialogue Work? \n\nCafé conversations are designed on the \n\nassumption that people already have within \n\nthem the wisdom and creativity to confront \n\neven the most difficult challenges. The \n\nprocess is simple, yet it can yield surprising \n\nresults. The innovative design of the World \n\nCafé enables groups--often numbering in \n\nthe hundreds of people--to participate \n\ntogether in evolving rounds of dialogue \n\nwith three or four others while at the same \n\ntime remaining part of a single, larger, con-\n\nnected conversation. Small, intimate conversations link and build \n\non each other as people move between groups, cross-pollinate \n\nideas, and discover new insights into questions or issues that really \n\nmatter in their life, work, or community. As the network of new \n\nconnections increases, knowledge-sharing grows. A sense of the \n\nwhole becomes increasingly strong. The collective wisdom of the \n\ngroup becomes more accessible, and innovative possibilities for \n\naction emerge. \n\nIn a Café gathering people often move rapidly from ordinary \n\nconversations--which keep us stuck in the past, are often divisive, \n\nand are generally superficial--toward conversations that matter, in \n\nwhich there is deeper collective understanding or forward move-\n\nment in relation to a situation that people really care about. The \n\nseven World Café design principles, when used in combination, \n\nalso create a kind of "conversational greenhouse," nurturing the\n\nconditions for the rapid propagation of actionable knowledge. \n\nThese design principles are not limited to a formal Café event. \n\nThey can also be used to focus and enhance the quality of other \n\nkey conversations--enabling you to draw on the talent and \n\nwisdom of your organization or community to a greater extent \n\nthan generally occurs with more traditional approaches. \n\nWorld Café conversations simultaneously create a lived \n\nexperience of how we naturally self-organize to think together, \n\nstrengthen community, share knowledge, and ignite innovation. \n\nThey allow us to see more clearly the importance of conversation \n\nas a living force so we can become more intentional about \n\nengaging its power. Café conversations demonstrate one \n\ninnovative way to put living systems theory into practice. \n\nThe World Café, both as a designed conversational process \n\nand as a deeper living systems pattern, has immediate, practical \n\nimplications for meeting and conference design, strategy forma-\n\ntion, knowledge creation, rapid innovation, stakeholder engage-\n\nment, and large-scale change. Experiencing a Café conversation \n\nin action also helps us make personal and professional choices \n\nabout more satisfying ways to participate in the ongoing \n\nconversations that help shape our lives. \n\nThe World Café Goes Global \n\nSince its inception in 1995, tens of thousands of people on six \n\ncontinents have participated in World Café dialogues in settings \n\nranging from crowded hotel ballrooms holding twelve hundred \n\npeople to cozy living rooms with just a dozen folks present. In a \n\nglobal consumer products company, executives from more than \n\nthirty nations used the Café process to integrate a new worldwide \n\nmarketing strategy. Mexican government and corporate leaders \n\nhave applied the World Café to scenario planning. Leaders from \n\nlocal communities representing more than sixty countries \n\nparticipated in Café dialogues during the Stockholm Challenge, \n\nwhich offers a Nobel-style prize for those creating technology for \n\nthe common good. \n\nBeginning the Conversation 5 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n6 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nFaculty members in the United States and Europe are creating \n\nvirtual, online Knowledge Cafés to conduct distance learning pro-\n\ngrams. In New Zealand and the United States, the World Café has \n\ninspired the creation of local venues for hosting Café conversa-\n\ntions on key issues related to business futures, \n\nsustainable development, and community collab-\n\noration. The World Café has supported Conversa-\n\ntion Cafés, Commonway Cafés, and Let's Talk \n\nAmerica, key citizen initiatives that invite diverse \n\ngroups to explore contemporary issues. Local \n\nchurches and schools have used the World Café \n\nprocess on a smaller scale to build community \n\nand access the wisdom of their members. \n\nWhether in business, government, health, \n\neducation, NGO, or community settings, the \n\nWorld Café can make a special contribution when \n\nthe goal is the focused use of dialogue to foster \n\nproductive relationships, collaborative learning, \n\nand collective insight around real-life challenges \n\nand key strategic questions. This is especially true when working \n\nwith groups that are larger than most traditional dialogue circles \n\nare designed to accommodate. \n\nA Community of Inquiry and Practice \n\nThe global World Café learning community--as well as this \n\nbook--have evolved as colleagues from around the world experi-\n\nment, document their work, share ideas, and learn from each \n\nother about the theory and practice embodied in the Café \n\nconversation approach. \n\nI will serve as the primary narrator and your host for weaving \n\ntogether the stories, reflections, and conversations among World \n\nCafé pioneers and others as we share with you our discoveries \n\nand the questions at the edge of our "not knowing." In the \n\nPerspectives & Observations sections I'll share my own personal \n\naha's and insights, and introduce you to others who are \n\ncontributing to our learning. \n\nThe World Café can make \n\na special contribution \n\nwhen the goal is the \n\nfocused use of dialogue \n\nto foster productive \n\nrelationships, collaborative \n\nlearning, and collective \n\ninsight \n\nAll pioneering ventures are incomplete, reflecting the particu-\n\nlar interests and ways of seeing of those who have been part of \n\nthe journey, and who make the initial maps of the territory. I am \n\nbut one among many colleagues who are furthering key aspects \n\nof this work. Yet I hope your travels with me through these pages \n\nwill provide glimpses that stimulate new conversations about \n\nwhere to focus special attention and care in your own organiza-\n\ntions and communities. \n\nThe research for this book and for my own earlier Ph.D. on \n\nthe World Café (Brown, 2001) was conducted in the spirit of \n\nAppreciative Inquiry, an approach to organizational learning and \n\ndevelopment originated by David Cooperrider and his colleagues \n\nat Case Western University (Cooperrider and Srivastva, 1987; \n\nCooperrider and others, 2003; Whitney and Trosten-Bloom, \n\n2003). Appreciative Inquiry deliberately focuses attention on \n\nwhat works, what brings life and vitality to an experience, and \n\nwhat's possible for its evolution. However, keep in mind that the \n\nsame challenges that come up in any group can arise in a World \n\nCafé conversation. At the same time, the World Café's focus on \n\nintimate exchange, disciplined inquiry, cross-pollination of ideas, \n\nand possibility thinking tends to create psychological safety and \n\nlessen inappropriate grandstanding and people's attachment to \n\ntheir own points of view. The very design of Café conversations \n\noften makes these common challenges easier to deal with than in \n\nmany group settings. \n\nWhat You'll Find Inside \n\nChapter 1 calls on insights from thought leaders across disciplines \n\nto reveal the critical but often invisible role of conversation in \n\nshaping our lives and our futures. Chapter 2 invites you to con-\n\nsider a new perspective on conversation as a core process--a \n\nfundamental means through which groups and organizations \n\nadapt to changing circumstances and co-create the knowledge \n\nnecessary for success. It also briefly introduces the seven core \n\ndesign principles that are central to understanding the World Café \n\napproach to dialogue. \n\nBeginning the Conversation 7 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n8 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nChapters 3 through 9 share the seven core World Café design \n\nprinciples, with each chapter focusing on one of them. The stories \n\nthat open these chapters reveal the creativity and imagination \n\nwith which Café hosts from around the world are using these \n\nprinciples to foster conversations that matter. These real life \n\n"learning stories," including the hosts' dilemmas and discoveries, \n\nform the heart of the book. More than any abstract treatise, teach-\n\ning, or training manual, they provide innovative ideas for how to \n\ncraft a World Café approach adapted to your unique situation. \n\nEach chapter then grounds these experiences in a discussion of \n\nboth the conceptual underpinnings of each design principle and \n\ntheir general application in a wide variety of settings. \n\nChapter 10 focuses on the practicalities of Café hosting that \n\nhave not been covered in detail in the earlier exploration of the \n\nseven guiding principles. This chapter is designed to stand alone \n\nas a World Café hosting guide to help you plan Café dialogues in \n\ndiverse settings. If you want an initial overview of the specifics of \n\nCafé hosting, this is the place to start. It provides the information \n\nneeded for someone with previous group experience to host a \n\nsuccessful Café, particularly if you have attended a World Café \n\ndialogue yourself. \n\nChapter 11 begins with several short stories that illustrate \n\nways leaders are using World Café approaches as part of their own \n\nconversational leadership--the capacity to engage the collabora-\n\ntive intelligence of their organizations and communities to meet \n\nreal-life challenges. These form the backdrop for our exploration \n\nof both the organizational infrastructures and personal capabili-\n\nties that conversational leaders can develop in order to nurture \n\ngreater business and social value using dialogue as a core process. \n\nChapter 12 highlights the societal implications and the prom-\n\nise inherent in embracing and acting on the insights and practical \n\nexperiences explored throughout the book. You are encouraged to \n\nbecome a part of the dialogue and deliberation community, shar-\n\ning your insights and discoveries as you make your own unique \n\ncontribution to creating a culture of dialogue wherever you may \n\nfind yourself. \n\nIn the epilogue, octogenarian Anne Dosher, Ph.D., the elder \n\nof the World Café, shares the questions that have informed her \n\nown life's journey and why she has committed her remaining \n\nyears to nurturing a culture of dialogue. Peter Senge, senior lecturer \n\nat the MIT Sloan School of Management and founding chair of \n\nthe Society for Organizational Learning, then offers an afterword \n\nbased on our experience together in hosting World Café gather-\n\nings with key global leaders. \n\nIf you want to learn more about other forms of dialogue, \n\nas well as key initiatives that are also making wonderful contribu-\n\ntions to the field of dialogue and deliberation, take a look at \n\n"Resources and Connections" at the back of the book. And \n\nalthough this book is not an academic treatise, enough reference \n\nmaterial has been included in the text to support you in "follow-\n\ning the trail" to the conceptual foundations of the World Café \n\nand related areas of interest. \n\nHow to Engage with What's Here \n\nHaving a common architecture as this book unfolded has allowed \n\ndiverse contributions in a shared framework, enabling you to \n\nengage with the material based on your own reading style and \n\npreferences. Each chapter begins with a quotation, \n\nan illustration, and a question that illuminate the \n\nessence of that chapter, so if you look only at the \n\nchapter openers you'll gain an overview of the \n\nbook's main themes. Each chapter's learning sto-\n\nries highlight the way the chapter's core ideas are \n\nbeing put into real-world practice. These stories, \n\nalthough simply "snapshots in time" that con-\n\ntinue to unfold, enable you to appreciate the \n\nmany ways you might introduce and engage Café \n\nconversations in your own life and work. In the \n\n"Perspectives & Observations" sections that follow, I, as your host, \n\nwill share multidisciplinary insights from leading edge thinkers as \n\nthey inform our exploration of dialogue and Café learning. At the \n\nend of each chapter, you'll find "Questions for Reflection," a \n\nBeginning the Conversation 9 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nEach chapter's learning \n\nstories highlight the way \n\nthe chapter's core ideas \n\nare being put into real-\n\nworld practice\n\n10 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nseries of questions to consider as you convene and host conversa-\n\ntions that matter. \n\nWe've purposely included multiple voices and modes of \n\nexpression as well as graphic illustrations to illuminate key ideas. \n\nWe've also used the following terms--World Café, Café conversa-\n\ntion, and Café dialogue--interchangeably throughout the text to \n\ndescribe the World Café process. In addition, you'll find Café \n\nnames like Knowledge Café, Leadership Café, Strategy Café, and \n\nothers that illuminate the many ways people are naming and \n\nadapting the basic World Café pattern and process in ways that \n\nmeet their unique needs and constituencies. \n\nAlthough this book is not a how-to manual or a detailed \n\nrecipe for creating a World Café event, you'll find both key ingre-\n\ndients and practical ideas for hosting conversations that matter in \n\nmany different organizational and community settings. We've dis-\n\ncovered that one of the strengths of the World Café approach is \n\nits simplicity and versatility. In fact, if you have experience leading \n\nor working with groups, a careful reading of the stories that begin \n\neach chapter along with a close review of chapter 10 will likely \n\ngive you enough information to get started. The seven World Café \n\ndesign principles and varied hosting practices you'll find here can \n\nbe helpful in convening conversations for many different pur-\n\nposes, whether you use the Café format or not. Even if you are \n\nnot planning to host Cafés personally, the book will provide you \n\nwith enough perspective to determine if this approach is right for \n\nyour own organization's meetings, conferences, or retreats. \n\nAs I mentioned earlier, "Questions for Reflection," posed at \n\nthe end of each chapter, encourage you to consider your own \n\nexperience and process of discovery about conversations that mat-\n\nter. Take a moment to ask yourself these questions now: \n\n◗ What drew me to this book? \n\n◗ If I think of this book as a personal conversation with the \n\nauthors, how will that affect how I approach what they have \n\nto share with me?\n\n◗ What question, if I explore it during my time with this book, \n\ncould make the most difference in my life and work? \n\nThere is ample room for noting your own thoughts and \n\nreflections. Imagine yourself in a Café dialogue and think of these \n\npages as Café tablecloths. Notice what connects to your personal \n\nexperience and your own process of discovery. Jot down your \n\ninsights about where to focus special attention and care as you \n\nengage conversations that matter in your own organization or \n\ncommunity. Consider your own questions. Add your voice to \n\nthe conversation. \n\nIn one of his wonderful poems, the Spanish poet Antonio \n\nMachado reminds us, "We make the path by walking on it." By \n\njoining us on the path that David and I, with the World Café \n\ncommunity, are walking, we hope you will find yourself as \n\nintrigued as we are by both the power of conversation and the \n\npromise of the World Café. We hope you'll find the value gener-\n\nated from Café conversations around the world as an encourag-\n\ning sign for the future. \n\nWelcome to the World Café! \n\nBeginning the Conversation 11 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n12 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nO N E \n\nIt's never enough just to tell people about some new insight. \n\nRather, you have to get them to experience it in a way \n\nthat evokes its power and possibility. Instead of pouring \n\nknowledge into people's heads, you need to help them grind \n\na new set of eyeglasses so they can see the world in a \n\nnew way. \n\n--John Seely Brown, Seeing Differently: Insights on Innovation \n\nSeeing the Invisible: Conversation Matters!\n\n13 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat i f humans are in conversat ion \n\nthe way f i sh are in water?\n\n14 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nDISCOVERING THE WORLD CAFÉ: THE INTELLECTUAL CAPITAL PIONEERS \n\nAs Told By \n\nDavid Isaacs \n\nJanuary 1995. It is a very rainy dawn at our home in Mill Valley, \n\nCalifornia. A thick mist hangs over Mt. Tamalpais as I look out \n\nbeyond the massive oak tree that borders the patio outside our living room. We have twenty-four people arriving in half an hour for the second day of a strategic dialogue on intellectual capital. Juanita and I are hosting the gathering in collaboration with Leif Edvinsson, vice president of intellectual capital for the Skandia Corporation in Sweden. This is the second in a series of conver-sations among the Intellectual Capital Pioneers--a group of cor-porate executives, researchers, and consultants from seven countries who are at the leading edge of this inquiry. \n\nThe field of intellectual capital and knowledge management is still in its infancy. No books have yet been written. No maps exist. We're making them as we go. Last evening we were in the midst of exploring the question: What is the role of leadership in maximizing the value of intellectual capital? \n\nJuanita is worried. As she sets out the breakfast and prepares the coffee, she's concerned about how we can create the right setting for the day's agenda if the pouring rain continues and no one can go outside on the patio to visit when they arrive. Then I have an idea. "Why don't we set up our TV tables in the living room and just have people get their coffee and visit around the tables while we're waiting for everyone to arrive? We'll then put away the tables and begin with our normal dialogue circle." \n\nJuanita breathes a sigh of relief. As we set out the small tables and white vinyl chairs, our interactive graphics specialist, Tomi Nagai-Rothe, arrives and adds, "Those look like café tables. I think they need some tablecloths!" She improvises, draping white sheets of easel paper over each of the paired TV tables. Now it's getting kind of playful. We've stopped wor-rying about the rain, which is coming down in sheets. Juanita decides we need flowers on the café tables, and goes for small vases downstairs. In the meantime, Tomi adds crayons to each of the tables, just like those in many neighborhood cafés. She makes a lovely sign for our front door--Welcome to the Homestead Café--playing off our address, Homestead Boulevard, which is actually a narrow road up the side of a mountain. \n\nJust as Juanita places the flowers on the tables, folks begin to arrive. They are delighted and amused. As people get their coffee and croissants, they gather in informal groups around \n\nDavid Isaacs, my partner in \n\nlife and work, is a co-origina-\n\ntor of the World Café. In this \n\nstory he shares the serendip-\n\nity surrounding the birth of \n\nthe World Café and its com-\n\nmunity of practice, along \n\nwith our early musings about \n\nwhat was at play in Café \n\ndialogues. Our initial Café \n\nexperience also set the stage \n\nfor unexpected discoveries \n\nabout the powerful role that \n\nconversation plays in shap-\n\ning our futures. \n\nS T O RY\n\nSeeing the Invisible 15 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nthe café tables and begin to talk about last night's question. People are really engaged. They begin to scribble on the tablecloths. Juanita and I have a quick huddle and decide that, rather than have a formal dialogue circle to open the gathering, we will simply encourage people to continue to share what's bubbling up from their conversations that could shed light on the essence of the relationship between leadership and intellectual capital. \n\nForty-five minutes pass and the conversation is still going strong. Charles Savage, one of our members, calls out, "I'd love to have a feel for what's happening in the other conversa-tions in the room. Why don't we leave one host at the table and have our other members travel to different tables, carrying the seed ideas from our conversation and connecting and linking with the threads that are being woven at other tables?" There's consensus that the suggestion seems like fun. After a few minutes of wrap-up, folks begin to move around the room. One host remains at each table, while the others each go to a different table to con-tinue the conversations. \n\nThis round lasts another hour. Now the room is really alive! People are excited and engaged, almost breathless. Another person speaks up. "Why don't we experiment by leav-ing a new host at the table, with the others traveling, continuing to share and link what we're discovering?" \n\nAnd so it continues. The rain falling, hard. People huddling around the TV tables, learn-ing together, testing ideas and assumptions together, building new knowledge together, adding to each other's diagrams and pictures and noting key words and ideas on the table-cloths. Juanita and I look up and realize that it is almost lunchtime. We have been partici-pating in the café conversations ourselves and the hours have passed as if they were minutes. \n\nThe energy in the room is palpable. It is as if the very air is shimmering. I ask the group to wrap up their conversations and gather around a large rolled-out piece of mural paper that Tomi has placed on the rug in the middle of the living room floor. It looks, in fact, like a large café tablecloth spread on the floor. We invite each small group to put their individ-ual tablecloths around the edges of the larger cloth and then take a "tour" to notice patterns, themes, and insights that are emerging in our midst. \n\nAs Juanita and I watch our collective discoveries and insights unfold visually on the large mural paper in the center of the group, we know something quite unusual has happened. We are bearing witness to something for which we have no language. It is as if the intelli-gence of a larger collective Self, beyond the individual selves in the room, had become visi-ble to us. It feels almost like "magic"--an exciting moment of recognition of what we are discovering together that's difficult to describe yet feels strangely familiar. The café process somehow enabled the group to access a form of collaborative intelligence that grew more potent as both ideas and people traveled from table to table, making new connections and cross-pollinating their diverse insights. \n\n. . .\n\nA \n\nfter that breakthrough meeting, David and I, along with \n\nFinn Voldtofte, a close colleague from Denmark who had \n\nparticipated in that initial gathering, spent the next day \n\ntrying to understand what had happened. We looked at each of \n\nthe components of the day, examining how it had contributed to \n\nthe living knowledge that emerged. We considered what had \n\noccurred when people entered the house and saw the colorful and \n\ninviting Homestead Café in our living room. Was there something \n\nabout the café itself as an archetype--a familiar cultural form \n\naround the world--that was able to evoke the immediate inti-\n\nmacy and collective engagement that we experienced? Did the \n\npositive associations that most people make with cafés support \n\nthe natural emergence of easy and authentic conversation that \n\nhad happened, despite the lack of formal guidelines or dialogue \n\ntraining among the participants? \n\nWe considered the role and use of questions to engage collab-\n\norative thinking. Was there something in the way we had framed \n\nthe conversation around a core question that participants cared \n\nabout--"What is the relationship between leadership and intellec-\n\ntual capital?"--that affected the quality and depth of collective \n\ninsight? Then there was the cross-pollination of ideas across \n\ngroups. Did carrying insights from one group to another enable \n\nthe emergence of an unexpected web of lively new connections \n\namong diverse perspectives? We mused on the function of people \n\nwriting on their tablecloths and later contributing their collective \n\ninsights to the common tablecloth as we explored our discoveries \n\ntogether. What was the importance, if any, that people could liter-\n\nally see each other's ideas on their tablecloths, similar to a hurried \n\nsketch or an idea scribbled on a napkin? \n\nAs we tried to illuminate our experience, we were reminded \n\nof how many new ideas and social innovations have historically \n\nbeen born and spread through informal conversations in cafés, \n\nsalons, churches, and living rooms. We realized that what we had \n\nexperienced in the café conversation in our living room was \n\n16 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nPerspectives & Observations\n\nSeeing the Invisible 17 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nperhaps a small-scale replica of a deeper living pattern of how \n\nknowledge-sharing, change, and innovation have always occurred \n\nin human societies. We recalled the salon movement that gave \n\nbirth to the French Revolution, as well as the sewing circles and \n\ncommittees of correspondence that foretold America's independ-\n\nence. Finn reminded us of the widespread network of study circles \n\nthat fostered the social and economic renaissance in Scandinavia \n\nduring the early twentieth century, and we realized that David's \n\nand my early experiences with social movements, including the \n\nfarmworkers, followed the same pattern of development. \n\nFounders of major change efforts often say, "Well, it all began \n\nwhen some friends and I started talking." \n\nThe evolving web of conversations in our liv-\n\ning room seemed to allow us to experience directly \n\nthe often invisible way that large-scale organiza-\n\ntional and societal change occurs--what we have \n\nsince come to call "nature's strategic planning \n\nprocess." Are we as human beings so immersed in \n\nconversation that, like fish in water, conversation is \n\nour medium for survival and we just can't see it? \n\nHad we somehow stumbled onto a set of princi-\n\nples that made it easier for larger groups to notice \n\nand access this natural process in order to develop \n\ncollaborative intelligence around critical questions and concerns? \n\nMight this awareness support leaders in becoming more inten-\n\ntional about fostering connected networks of conversation \n\nfocused on their organization's most important questions? \n\nOut of this conversation, the image of the World Café \n\nemerged as a central metaphor to guide our nascent exploration \n\ninto the possibilities that we had tapped into that rainy day. Many \n\nof us who were at that initial gathering began to experiment with \n\nthe simple process that we had discovered. We began to host \n\nWorld Café conversations in a variety of settings and to share our \n\nlearnings with each other as we went. \n\nAnd then from a completely unexpected source, it became \n\nclear to me just how much conversation matters. It matters a lot. \n\nAre we as human beings so \n\nimmersed in conversation \n\nthat, like fish in water, \n\nconversation is our \n\nmedium for survival and \n\nwe just can't see it?\n\nKnowing Together and Bringing Forth a World \n\nI was serving as co-faculty for a living systems seminar sponsored \n\nby the Berkana Institute, which supports new forms of leadership \n\naround the world. Fritjof Capra, the noted physicist and living \n\nsystems theorist, who was also on the faculty, was giving a talk \n\nabout the nature of knowledge. In his measured, professorial \n\nstyle, Fritjof began to share surprising ideas from the work of two \n\nChilean scientists--evolutionary biologist, Humberto Maturana, \n\nand cognitive scientist, Francisco Varela. I won't be able to do full \n\njustice to the range and subtlety of their groundbreaking research, \n\nbut I'd like to share one key aspect of it with you because I think \n\nit has direct relevance for how people see the world and how we \n\nchoose to live in it. \n\nMaturana and Varela's work reaffirms that as a species we \n\nhumans have evolved the unique capacity for talking together and \n\nfor making distinctions of meaning in language. This human gift \n\nfor living in the braided meanings and emotions that arise \n\nthrough our conversations is what enables us to share our ideas, \n\nimages, intentions, and discoveries with each other. Since our \n\nearliest ancestors gathered in circles around the warmth of a fire, \n\nconversation has been our primary means for discovering what \n\nwe care about, sharing knowledge, imagining the future, and \n\nacting together to both survive and thrive. \n\nPeople in small groups spread their insights to larger groups, \n\ncarrying the seed ideas for new conversations, creative possibili-\n\nties, and collective action. This systemic process is embodied in \n\nself-reinforcing, meaning-making networks that arise through the \n\ninteractions that conversation makes possible. Maturana and \n\nVarela point out that because we live in language--and in the \n\nsophisticated coordination of actions that language makes possi-\n\nble--we "bring forth a world" through the networks of conversa-\n\ntion in which we participate (1987, p. 234). We embody and \n\nshare our knowledge through conversation. From this perspective, \n\nconversations are action--the very lifeblood and heartbeat of \n\nsocial systems like organizations, communities, and societies. As \n\nnew meanings and the coordinated actions based on them begin \n\n18 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nto spread through wider networks, the future comes into being. \n\nHowever, these futures can take many alternative paths. In a \n\nprovocative seminar that Maturana later gave at the Society for \n\nOrganizational Learning, he brought this message home: \n\nAll that we humans do, we do in conversation. . . . As we live in conver-\n\nsation new kinds of objects continue to appear, and as we take these \n\nobjects and live with them, new domains of existence appear! So here we \n\nare now, living with these very funny kinds of objects called firms, com-\n\npanies, profit, incomes, and so on. And we are very attached to them. . . . \n\nJust the same, we are not necessarily stuck in any of the objects we cre-\n\nate. What is peculiar to human beings is that we can reflect and say, "Oh, \n\nI'm not interested in this any more," change our orientation, and begin \n\na new history. Other animals cannot reflect, as they do not live in lan-\n\nguage. We are the ones who make language and conversation our man-\n\nner of living. . . . We enjoy it, we caress each other in language. We can \n\nalso hurt each other in language. We can open spaces or restrict them in \n\nconversations. This is central to us. And we shape our own path, as do all \n\nliving systems (Maturana and Bunnell, 1999, p. 12). \n\nThus, from the perspective of human evolu-\n\ntion, conversation is not something trivial that we \n\nengage in among many other activities. Conversa-\n\ntion is the core process by which we humans \n\nthink and coordinate our actions together. The \n\nliving process of conversation lies at the heart of \n\ncollective learning and co-evolution in human \n\naffairs. Conversation is our human way of creating \n\nand sustaining--or transforming--the realities in \n\nwhich we live. \n\nVicki Robin, my dear friend and colleague, is \n\nthe founder of Conversation Cafés, an innovative \n\nsmall group dialogue approach that invites \n\ncitizens to gather in cafés and other public places \n\nto explore key societal questions. Recently, she shared with me a \n\nvivid reflection of how this often invisible process works in every-\n\nday life. \n\nSeeing the Invisible 19 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWe talk to ourselves in our minds about our past, present, and future. Out \n\nof this self-talk, we talk with others about our pasts, present, and futures, \n\ngenerating personal and shared possibilities though lively exchanges of \n\nideas and feelings. We each then carry the meanings \n\nand possibilities we've created into other conversations \n\nat home, at work, at church, in boardrooms, and bed-\n\nrooms, and halls of power everywhere. A daughter talks \n\nto her father about her concerns for the future . . . and \n\ncompany policy changes. A father talks to his daughter \n\nabout his concerns for her future . . . and a new life \n\npath unfolds, later impacting thousands. Line workers \n\ntalk with their bosses . . . and a plant is redesigned. Cit-\n\nizens testify at public hearings . . . and new priorities \n\nfor society emerge. We speak our world into being. A buzz develops--a \n\nkind of overtone of resonant ideas carried through multiple conversa-\n\ntions--that tells us what's on our collective mind. Some among us put \n\nwords on this buzz and new possibilities for us as a whole begin to enter \n\nour language, the vehicle of meaning-making. We see ourselves in their \n\ndescriptions--"Ah," we think, "I never saw that, but yes, that is true." We \n\ntalk about it . . . shaping again what we collectively imagine for ourselves. \n\nOur conversations shape the spirit and substance of our times. \n\nAnother colleague shared with me a wonderful story that \n\nprovides a powerful example of the way we can shape the future \n\nthrough conversation. The story began with a dinner conversation \n\namong four friends over steak and Chianti at the home of a \n\nyoung businesswoman in Munich and evolved, in just a few \n\nweeks, into one of the largest mass movements in Germany since \n\nthe end of World War II. \n\nOver dinner, the four friends decided it was time for them to \n\nstep out from the "silent majority" and show their repudiation of \n\nthe rising number of neo-Nazi attacks on foreigners. By the time \n\ndessert was over, each agreed to call several friends and colleagues \n\nand share the idea of creating a silent candlelight vigil to bear \n\nwitness to these injustices. Their first gathering drew one hundred \n\nfriends to a popular downtown bar, each of whom agreed to call \n\n20 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWe each then carry the \n\nmeanings and possibilities \n\nwe've created into other \n\nconversations\n\nten others to encourage a larger turnout for a second event. \n\nWithin days the "candlelight conversation" spread across the city \n\nthrough circles of acquaintances in businesses, schools, churches, \n\nand civic groups. The original group of friends--and the nation as \n\na whole--were stunned when four hundred thousand people \n\nturned out in Munich for the vigil. \n\nInspired by the Munich gathering, citizens in other cities held \n\nconversations and created vigils over the following weeks. Over five \n\nhundred thousand turned out in Hamburg, two hundred thousand \n\nin Berlin, and one hundred thousand or more in Frankfurt, Nurem-\n\nberg, and other cities. Many smaller towns joined in what became a \n\nnational dialogue on the acceptability of neo-Nazi behavior. The \n\nseemingly endless chains of flickering candlelight became a power-\n\nful symbol of the nation's collective commitment, born in conver-\n\nsation, to turn the tide against such behavior (Kinsler, 1992). And \n\nthis happened prior to widespread use of the Internet! \n\nConversation as a Generative Force \n\nI was excited to discover Maturana and Varela's work on the \n\npower of conversation to shape our future and to encounter vivid \n\nexamples of this power in action. For the first time, I could see \n\nthat leading edge thinkers with backgrounds completely different \n\nfrom our own were discovering what David and I had intuitively \n\nsensed from our lived experience with social movements as well \n\nas with our early World Café experiments. As we looked at the \n\npicture painted by both scholars and practitioners in other fields \n\nof endeavor, a fascinating pattern began to emerge. Although their \n\nperspectives on the importance of conversation may have made \n\nup only a single piece in the larger puzzle each was exploring in \n\nhis or her own work, when taken together these insights began to \n\nreveal the critical role of conversation in shaping our lives. I'd like \n\nto share a collage of these ideas to give you a flavor of what we \n\nnoticed as we literally put the pieces together on a large wall-\n\nboard. Please take a few minutes to read carefully, on the follow-\n\ning pages, what these thought leaders from different fields have to \n\nsay. I think you'll find their ideas illuminating. \n\nSeeing the Invisible 21 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n22 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nLearning Organizations True learning organizations are a space for generative conversations and concerted action which creates a field of alignment that produces tremendous power to invent new realities in conversation and to bring about these new realities in action. \n\nPolitics Democracy begins in human conversation. The simplest, least threatening investment any citizen can make in democratic renewal is to begin talking with other people, asking the questions and knowing that their answers matter. \n\nStrategy Strategizing depends on creating a rich and complex web of conversations that cuts across previously isolated knowledge sets and creates new and unexpected combinations of insight. \n\nThe Knowledge Economy Conversations are the way workers discover what they know, share it with their colleagues, and in the process create new knowledge for the organization. In the new economy, conversations are the most important form of work . . . so much so that the conversation is the organization. \n\nFamily Therapy Our capacity for change lies in "the circle of the unexpressed," in the capacity we have to be "in language" with each other and, in language to develop new themes, new narratives, and new stories. Through this process we co-create and co-develop our systemic realities. \n\nInformation Technology Technology is putting a sharper, more urgent point on the importance of conversations. Conversations are moving faster, touching more people, and bridging greater distances. These networked conversations are enabling powerful new forms of social organization and knowledge exchange to occur. \n\nEducation Within communities that foster human growth and development, change seems to be a natural result of constructing meaning and knowledge together--an outgrowth of our conversations about what matters. Leaders need to pose the questions and convene the conversations that invite others to become involved. . . . In social systems such as schools and districts, one good conversation can shift the direction of change forever. \n\nFred Kofman and Peter Senge "Communities of Commitment" Organizational Dynamics \n\nLinda Lambert and others The Constructivist Leader \n\nAlan Webber "What's So New About the New Economy?" Harvard Business Review \n\nHarlene Anderson and Harold Goolishian "Human Systems as Linguistic Systems" Family Process \n\nWilliam Greider Who Will Tell the People? \n\nGary Hamel "The Search for Strategy" Fortune \n\nRick Levine and others The Cluetrain Manifesto \n\nCONVERSATION MATTERS! \n\nSeeing the Invisible 23 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nLeadership Talk is key to the executive's work . . . the use of language to shape new possibilities, reframe old perspectives, and excite new commitments . . . the active process of dialogue, and the caring for relationships as the core foundation of any social system. \n\nCollective Intelligence Dialogue is the central aspect of co-intelligence. We can only generate higher levels of intelligence among us if we are doing some high quality talking with one another. \n\nConflict Resolution, Global Affairs The reality today is that we are all interdependent and have to coexist on this small planet. Therefore, the only sensible and intelligent way of resolving differences and clashes of interests, whether between individuals or nations, is through dialogue. \n\nFutures Research Conversation is the heart of the new inquiry. It is perhaps the core human skill for dealing with the tremendous challenges we face. The culture of conversation is a different culture, one that can make a difference to the future of the world. If we combine conversations that really matter . . . with the interactive reach of the Internet, we have a powerful force for change from the ground up. \n\nEvolutionary Biology Our human existence is one in which we can live whatever world we bring about in our conversations, even if it is a world that finally destroys us as the kind of being that we are. Indeed, this has been our history since our origins as languaging beings--namely, a history or creation of new domains of existence as different networks of conversation. \n\nConsciousness Studies I'm suggesting that there is the possibility for a transformation of the nature of consciousness, both individually and collectively, and that whether this can be solved culturally and socially depends on dialogue. That's what we're exploring. \n\nExecutive Development As the new business landscape continues to emerge, and new forms of organization take shape, our ability to lead will be dependent upon our ability to host and convene quality conversations. \n\nSuresh Srivastva and David Cooperrider Appreciative Management and Leadership \n\nRobert Lengel, Ph.D., Director Center for Professional Excellence University of Texas at San Antonio Executive MBA Program \n\nInstitute for the Future In Good Company: Innovation at the Intersection of Technology and SustainabilityTom Atlee, Co-Intelligence Institute \n\nThe Tao of Democracy \n\nHis Holiness the Fourteenth Dalai Lama "Forum 2000" Conference, Prague \n\nHumberto Maturana and Gerda Verden-Zöller The Origin of Humanness in the Biology of Love \n\nDavid Bohm, On Dialogue\n\n24 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat We View Determines What We Do \n\nWhat if you began to shift your lens to see the power and poten-\n\ntial of the conversations occurring in your own family, organiza-\n\ntion, community, or nation? What if you believed and acted as if \n\nyour conversations and those of others really mattered? What \n\ndifference would that make to your daily choices as a parent, \n\nteacher, line leader, meeting planner, organizational specialist, \n\ncommunity member, or diplomat? \n\nAs Maturana and Varela point out, we live inside the \n\nimages we hold of the world. It can be disturbing to "see dif-\n\nferently" and to contemplate the practical implications of \n\nchanging our lenses (Lakoff, 2003; Morgan, 1997). Yet as \n\nNoel Tichy, head of the University of Michigan's Global Lead-\n\nership Program, told me many years ago, "What we view \n\ndetermines what we do." How we view the world around us, and \n\nhow we act based on those images, can make all the difference. \n\nAs we enter a time in which the capacity for thinking together \n\nand creating innovative solutions is viewed as critical to creating \n\nboth business and social value, many of us still live with the idea \n\nthat "talk is cheap," that most people are "all talk and no action," \n\nand that we should "stop talking and get to work." Lynne Twist, \n\na social entrepreneur who has raised millions for improving life \n\nin developing nations, has a different perspective--one that you \n\nmight want to play with as you read the stories and reflections \n\nthat follow. "I believe," she said, "that we don't really live in the \n\nworld. We live in the conversation we have about the world. . . . \n\nAnd over that we have absolute, omnipotent power. We have the \n\nopportunity to shape that conversation, and in so doing, to shape \n\nhistory" (Toms, 2002, pp. 38-39). As your host I invite you, just \n\nfor the time you are reading this book, to put on a new set of \n\nglasses. See with new eyes the conversational landscape that has \n\nbeen here all along awaiting our personal attention and care. And \n\nbegin to live a different future. \n\nWe live inside \n\nthe images we hold \n\nof the world\n\nSeeing the Invisible 25 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nConsider the conversations you are currently having in your family, your organization, or your community. To what degree do they create frustration and fragment efforts or offer new insights and ways to work collectively? \n\nIf you were to accept the perspective that conversation really is a core process for accessing collective intelligence and co-evolving the future, what practical difference would that make in how you approached conversations, particu-larly in situations you care about? \n\nPick one upcoming conversation that matters in your own life or work. What one specific thing might you do or what one choice might you make that would improve the quality of that conversation? \n\nQuestions for Reflection\n\n26 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nT W O \n\nManaging in the new economy requires not just change \n\nprograms but a changed mindset. . . . Conversations are the \n\nway workers discover what they know, share it with their \n\ncolleagues, and in the process create new knowledge for the \n\norganization. In the new economy, conversations are the \n\nmost important form of work. \n\n--Alan Webber, "What's So New About the New Economy?" \n\nHarvard Business Review \n\nConversation as a Core Process: Co-Creating \n\nBusiness and Social Value\n\n27 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat i f conversat ion \n\ni s how things get done?\n\nS T O RY \n\n28 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nCREATING A CULTURE OF DIALOGUE: MUSEUM OF SCIENCE AND INDUSTRY, TAMPA BAY \n\nAs Told By \n\nWit Ostrenko and Fred Steier \n\nWe've designed MOSI to be a center for learning conversa-\n\ntions. We don't want it to be a one-way, don't-touch-any-\n\nthing science center, so we've struggled with what hands-on learning really means, both for our visitors and for ourselves. How do we learn together as families and as communities? For us, our best learning has been embedded in conversations--with the board, the staff, the larger community, and other science museums and associations. \n\nThe very nature of the museum is like a World Café, and each of our exhibits is like a Café table. Most people visit the exhibits in small groups, so there's already informal conversation and collec-tive discovery happening right there on the spot. Then, as people go from one exhibit to another, talking to others they don't even know, they become part of a lively web of conversations and share ideas, just like in an actual World Café event. And because we have many seniors as well as children visiting the museum, the conver-sations become intergenerational--they naturally cross-pollinate ideas by learning with and from each other. \n\nBut our use of the World Café is not only metaphorical. We use the Café process to engage with many of our key issues. We find the Café approach so useful with our internal staff, our pro-fessional community, and the public that the Café principles have \n\nbecome an integral part of our thinking about who we are as a community institution. The first Café we held was at a board retreat. People came with the expectation that it \n\nwould be another boring board meeting. But at the end, several people said, almost apolo-getically, that this was the first time they'd really talked with each other. The World Café built \n\nWit Ostrenko is the president \n\nof the Museum of Science \n\nand Industry (MOSI) of \n\nTampa Bay, Florida. Fred \n\nSteier, Ph.D., is professor of \n\ncommunication at the Uni-\n\nversity of South Florida and a \n\nresearch fellow at MOSI's \n\nCenter for Learning. MOSI is \n\na major regional science cen-\n\nter that receives eight hun-\n\ndred thousand visitors per \n\nyear. This is the story of how \n\nthe museum has been using \n\nWorld Café conversations \n\nand conversational leader-\n\nship as cornerstones in its \n\nefforts to redefine the rela-\n\ntionship between the institu-\n\ntion and its internal and \n\nexternal communities. \n\nThe following two stories offer real-life examples of how leaders in very \n\ndifferent settings are using the World Café to create business and social \n\nvalue through fostering conversations that matter.\n\nConversation as a Core Process 29 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nrelationships--not only between individual board members but also between the museum and the board as representatives of the community we serve. \n\nSo often boards are given the assignment simply to raise money. But the best fundrais-ing comes from talking together and understanding the reason for doing it. For example, it came out during our Board Café that there were no scholarships available to enable the com-munity's blue collar families to visit our museum. They're not poor, but spending $14.95 for adults and another $10.00 for kids is really cost-prohibitive, so they're not coming as often as we would like. It became a fundraising priority to help blue collar and other low-income families. As a result of ideas generated in that Board Café, we're halfway to raising a million-dollar endowment to provide full access to our museum to anyone who doesn't have the ability to pay. \n\nWe use our Café conversations to generate what we call "actionable knowledge." For us, this means that we intend for whatever emerges from a Café to be something that can be put into practice. For example, in the middle of 2003 we realized we weren't going to make our budget. We couldn't afford the staff we had, and we were going to have to make several cuts. So, we brought a group of about thirty staff people together and held a World Café to figure out what to do. \n\nThe objective of the Café was to generate new revenue-producing programs, with the goal of raising $175,000. We had no preconceived ideas of how to do this. During the Café, we came up with roughly $188,000 worth of new net revenue ideas. One of the wilder ideas was a Yu-Gi-Oh tournament. Nobody had ever heard of Yu-Gi-Oh except for a couple of the parents on our staff who knew how popular the game is because their kids watch it on tele-vision. They were the ones who encouraged the rest of the group to say yes to it. We put the stamp of approval on it, along with the other key ideas, right then and there. Do you know what? By putting our key ideas into practice, we ended fiscal year 2003 in the black by $267,000! \n\nSo where is the leadership in that kind of situation? The leaders at that Café weren't those with formal management titles, but rather anyone with great ideas for how to produce the most net revenue in a manner that is consistent with our mission and values. There were some real heroes that day! The Café conversations allowed people to contribute whom oth-ers don't normally see because they work nights and weekends or lead trips. All of a sudden one of these "invisible" people would put forth a great idea that everyone rallied around. It was very gratifying, and has helped build the feeling of MOSI as a collaborative community that includes our board and staff. \n\nIt's starting to become a habit with us to recognize when there is an issue that we need to work through together in a more dialogic manner. We've also noticed that our people get better and better results as they get more experience in using the Café process. We've discov-ered that we can learn to be better Café contributors. As we get more practice, we generate more actionable knowledge and collective wisdom. For example, our Cafés are helping us\n\n30 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\ngenerate creative program ideas that we anticipate will bring us $750,000 in net income this fiscal year. That's unheard of for a public institution in an uncertain economy. \n\nThe Café is also enabling us to explore the relationship between play and learning that is at the core of MOSI's mission. That kind of creativity and playing with ideas is now hap-pening in other settings, so even when we are not in a Café setting, people still behave in "Café fashion." For example, in a particularly innovative use of the World Café, we've held a number of Café conversations to engage visitors or potential visitors--including senior citi-zens, members of our minority communities, and children--in codesigning exhibit experi-ences. This use of the World Café really builds community, and the groups that help in the design process have a greater sense of ownership--the exhibits are truly "theirs." Thus, instead of MOSI being a kind of dictator of community values around science, it is becom-ing more of a partner with community members in discovering the collective knowledge and interests in science that people didn't even realize counted as knowledge. \n\nWe're now reaching out to the associations we belong to beyond our local area. For example, Wit hosted a large four-hundred-person Café with members of the Giant Screen Theater Association, and another with the international board of the Association of Science and Technology Centers. In both cases, World Café conversations helped us identify and pri-oritize key issues and what actionable steps we're going to take to address those issues. Café conversations are like a positive virus spreading out way beyond where we began! \n\n. . .\n\nConversation as a Core Process 31 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nIT'S WIN-WIN ALL THE WAY: SANOFI-SYNTHELABO \n\nAs Told By \n\nYvon Bastien \n\nIwas looking for a way to engage all of our people in thinking \n\nabout the future of our business. I'd tried several different \n\napproaches, but they just weren't bringing out our people's best thinking. I was searching for something that connected the heart, the mind, and the bottom line. It seemed to me that Café con-versations combined those three elements, and I decided to experiment with them. \n\nWe were in the midst of planning a celebration of our first $100 million in sales, a big achievement!
