I asked David Isaacs to come to Toronto to host a Café conversation with our sixteen-per-son design team in order to plan for our January strategy event. We wanted to create both a celebration of our success and a strategic conversation about the future of our business with all of our Cana-dian employees. At the end of the Design Team Café, David asked the group, "Well, what do you think?" And they replied, "Oh, it's great. Café conversations are powerful for a small group of 10 or 20, but they will never work with 250 people." David assured them that a Café dialogue could work even better with a lot of people, so we decided to try it, although there were some who were still skeptical. \n\nAs it turned out, the January Strategy Café Conversation and Celebration was extremely successful in generating key ideas for moving the business ahead. Something about the World Café approach helped people drill through the formal layers of their professional titles and bring more of their whole person to the conversation. I could see it in people's eyes and body language at the small tables. Afterwards, many people were excited by what had occurred at the January Café, and they began to go out and host Café conversations them-selves on all kinds of business questions. We counted seventeen Café dialogues in the months following that first experience! It was a bit clumsy at first, since we were learning as we went--it was a real challenge to craft the questions, use reflection in a more disciplined way, and give space for real dialogue. But several of us sensed that we were on to something, and I used my "executive privilege" as the president to encourage it to continue. \n\nThen I decided to push the envelope a little bit further and ask, "What if we were to do our long-range plan using Café conversations?" David was instrumental at that point in encouraging us to explore this possibility. We invited twenty or thirty people--the whole senior team, as well as younger leaders who had not been asked to contribute in this way \n\nYvon Bastien has served as \n\nthe president and general \n\nmanager of Sanofi-Synthe-\n\nlabo, Canada, a Paris-based \n\nglobal pharmaceutical com-\n\npany focused on the \n\nresearch, discovery, and \n\ndevelopment of innovative \n\nmedicines to treat many dis-\n\neases. Sanofi-Synthelabo \n\naffiliates operate in over one \n\nhundred countries world-\n\nwide. This is the story of \n\nhow Café conversations \n\nhave helped Sanofi-Synthe-\n\nlabo, Canada, create sus-\n\ntainable business value. \n\nS T O RY\n\n32 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nbefore--and convened a number of Café conversations focused on the long-range plan. We took our time, but we did it. And it was a success! Our Café work generated innovative but realistic action ideas for the strategic plan, as well as the collective commitment to make them happen. \n\nNext I said, "We did a three-year plan using Café conversations--what if we used it for next year's budget?" At that point, some people thought I was taking this Café idea too far! But others, including the director of finance, said, "Well, let's try it." And so we based the following year's budget on the selection of priority action ideas from our Café conversa-tions, which we then grounded in our projected return on investment and return on effort. To our surprise, we discovered that the Café conversation process was quite well-suited to this purpose. You see, in a way, the structure of the World Café obliges you to create a clean slate--a zero-based mentality--that's not encumbered by preconceived ideas. You can then select the ideas that have the most promise and link them to the hard-nosed account-ing requirements. \n\nTo me, it was critical that our Café conversations be directly focused on business needs and results. But the business is linked to the other worlds we live in--not just the organiza-tion but also our families and our communities. I saw our Café conversations as the glue that could hold these aspects together. So we began to experiment with Café conversations in areas where we had important stakeholder relationships. For example, our VP of sales and marketing hosted Café conversations with our business partners. And we did another long-range plan using Café dialogues where we pushed the envelope even further by inviting other external stakeholders--physicians, pharmacists, representatives from the Stroke Recovery Association, and even patients. \n\nThese Café conversations have helped us recognize that we don't work for ourselves alone. A special moment occurred during the whole-group conversation at the end of one of our Strategy Cafés when a person from the Stroke Recovery Association said, "You have a great product that prevents strokes. Why can't a part of your larger mission be to stand for no more strokes in all of Canada?" The room just lit up. It was like a volcano. All of a sudden, there was a feeling of "Oh, yes, that's it!" Now we're seeing patients, physicians, and hospi-tals as directly linked to our contribution to society. So, beginning to engage corporate social responsibility in a more focused way is an important outcome from this work. \n\nFor the last several years, we've been in hypergrowth mode, with double-digit increases. We're consistently surpassing the expectations of the corporation. I can't say if that is the direct result of our Café conversations or the result of other things we've also been doing, many of which have come from our Café work. But here's what I can say: the World Café has been very essential, very unique, because it's the only process I've found that consistently connects intellect and emotion to a business frame of reference. That's a key strategic busi-ness advantage.\n\nConversation as a Core Process 33 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nFor a businessperson, the numbers are the measure of success. If we don't have the num-bers, that's the end of the conversations. But if we don't have the conversations, that's the end of the numbers. It's a paradox. The numbers are only the outcome of actions that you've taken upstream, where the pulse of the organization lives. You need to look at how alive the organization is, how people interact and talk with each other--their relationships. That is a key part of the value-producing capacity of the organization. It's very difficult for people to measure that, since the only tool they have to measure is the numbers. \n\nBut there are other indicators. I know we're succeeding when I see people more engaged in our business decisions, taking risks, getting the issues on the table, not being afraid to speak and to take action. For example, if you look at the first Café conversation versus the tenth one, you can see a huge evolution in confidence--a knowledge that whatever the issue is, we're going to solve it. \n\nOf course, there are always challenges. I think there's great value in the kind of thinking and knowledge that Café conversations generate. But at the same time, we need better ways to move between the discovery part of a Café conversation and the action-planning part. We need to make sure when moving into the implementation phase that we leaders don't go back to our old control mode of doing things. \n\nFor me, Café conversations are an act of respect for our people and their capacity to con-tribute. A Café dialogue is like the on-ramp to a freeway--you enter into the flow of the traf-fic by contributing to the conversation and suddenly you're on the highway of the natural evolution of the business. Deep down, I believe that Café conversations are a carrier of life. I like to see people coming to life--and me coming to life at the same time--all around our business. It serves the individual, it serves the community, and it serves the shareholders. It's win-win all the way. \n\n. . .\n\nW \n\nhen Tom Johnson first walked in the door of our \n\nhome, I must admit I'd stereotyped him. I assumed \n\nhe'd be focused only on the numbers. After all, he was \n\na famous professor of accounting history and had written a semi-\n\nnal book on management accounting, Relevance Lost, which had \n\nan impact on measurement systems around the world (1991). \n\nHowever, when he began to speak as part of the opening circle at \n\nthe intellectual capital dialogue where the World Café was born, I \n\nwas dumbfounded. He was searching, he said, for a new way to \n\nthink about performance, measurement, value creation, and \n\nresults. Tom was concerned that leaders were using his writings \n\non activity-based costing as one more management tool to drive \n\nshort-term financial targets rather than to nurture sustainable \n\nbusiness and social value. \n\nTom challenged the group with the following set of ques-\n\ntions: Is it the financial and performance targets or the relationships \n\namong people and their patterns of working and thinking together that \n\nproduce the outcomes we value? If organizations are living systems, \n\nthen what core processes can an organization utilize that mirror \n\nnature's patterns? Where do you as a leader place intention and atten-\n\ntion in a living system? These powerful questions struck a chord \n\nwith the other participants at that initial World Café and have \n\ncontinued to resonate in our thinking and research into the role \n\nof conversation as a core process. \n\nOn Means and Ends \n\nWith his colleague Anders Bröms of Sweden, Tom later wrote \n\nanother seminal book, Profit Beyond Measure, focused on these \n\nquestions (2000). Based on extensive research, it encouraged lead-\n\ners to shift their attention from a focus on management exclu-\n\nsively by results (MBR) toward "management by means" \n\n(MBM)--the relationships and processes that shape the organiza-\n\ntion's capacity to learn, adapt to changing circumstances, and cre-\n\nate the knowledge necessary for its long-term performance. Tom \n\nand Anders point out that because we've separated the ends \n\n34 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nPerspectives & Observations\n\n(financial targets and performance objectives) from the means \n\n(the processes and practices used to create them), ends have come \n\nto seem more concrete, more "real," and therefore, more valuable \n\nthan means. In contrast, Tom and Anders show how means and \n\nends co-evolve simultaneously. "The task of managers," they argue, \n\n"is to stop treating results as a target one reaches by aiming better. \n\nInstead, results are an outcome that emerges spontaneously from \n\nmastering practices that harmonize with the patterns inherent in \n\nthe system itself. In other words, manage the means, not the \n\nresults. Means are ends in the making" (2000, p. 50, italics mine). \n\nTom Johnson's work has greatly informed our thinking about \n\nthe role of conversation as a core process, a fundamental "means" \n\nby which relationships are built, knowledge is shared, and value \n\nis created. As we explored this thinking with Tom and his wife, \n\nprominent educator Elaine Johnson, we began to see how the \n\nWorld Café can help make this natural process more tangible and \n\nreal, and therefore more actionable. In Elaine's words, "What \n\nwe're saying is that conversation is the essential, fundamental, \n\nand indispensable means. But how these conver-\n\nsations are viewed and structured will lead to \n\ndifferent outcomes." Tom adds, "If conversation \n\nis seen as a core means for creating organiza-\n\ntional performance, then how leadership works \n\nwith conversation will be a key factor in deter-\n\nmining how well the organization does. If people \n\nsay, 'Conversation means shut up and don't \n\nspeak until you're spoken to, or don't talk until \n\nthe boss authorizes you to,' that will lead to one \n\nset of results, and if the conversations occur more \n\nalong the lines of the World Café principles, that \n\nwill lead to a different set of results." \n\nWe've developed a simple picture to depict \n\none way of visualizing this core process of value \n\ncreation at work. \n\nAs our living tree, A Core Process for Value Cre-\n\nation, illustrates, questions that matter stimulate \n\nConversation as a Core Process 35 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nlearning conversations. These conversations, in turn, strengthen the \n\nnetworks of relationships and communities of practice through \n\nwhich an organization creates the fruits of its labors. The main chal-\n\nlenge is that this core process is generally invisible, unfocused, and \n\nrarely used intentionally by leaders as a means to create sustainable \n\nbusiness and social value. \n\nStart Talking and Get to Work \n\nFor most leaders, accepting the centrality of human conversation \n\nas a key organizational means for achieving desired results entails \n\na profound shift of mind--from seeing conversation as a periph-\n\neral activity to seeing conversation as one of the organization's \n\nmost valued assets. Experiencing a World Café conversation \n\nmakes it easier to visualize your whole organization or commu-\n\nnity as a dynamic, naturally occurring "Café," so you can work \n\nmore intentionally with the often invisible conversational and \n\nsocial networks underlying organizational performance. \n\nStop for a moment. Pause, as Peter Senge of MIT and I have \n\nin Café gatherings with key executives, to consider the practical \n\nimplications of the following question: If critical organizational \n\nknowledge really does get created through networks of conversation and \n\npersonal relationships, what does that mean in practical terms for strat-\n\negy evolution, for training and development, for technology infrastruc-\n\ntures, for the physical design of workplaces and spaces, and for your \n\nown action choices as an organizational member or leader? \n\nI'll never forget the booming voice of a six-foot-four Texan, \n\nthe head of global operations for a major multinational auto \n\nmanufacturer who was responsible for more than fifty thousand \n\nemployees. As he contemplated the implications of this shift in \n\npoint of view, his voice rang out, "Damn! Do you know what I've \n\ngone and done?" All eyes turned in his direction. I held my \n\nbreath. "I've just gone and reorganized my entire global opera-\n\ntion. I've broken up the learning communities and ongoing con-\n\nversations that have gotten built up over the years. It's going to \n\ntake us a long time to recover from this!" His heartfelt comment \n\nstimulated a lively conversation about what leaders could do to \n\n36 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nwork more effectively with conversation as a core process in their \n\nown organizations. \n\nThe Relationship Between Talk and Action \n\nOne of the most important shifts required in this new way of \n\nviewing conversation is to re-evaluate our traditional view that talk \n\nand action are separate activities. We had a fascinating conversa-\n\ntion with Café hosts in Denmark that explored this question. One \n\nparticipant suggested that we consider revising our traditional \n\nviews of talk and action, seeing them as a single integrated whole \n\nrather than as separate activities. What if, when conversations are \n\nhighly energized and relevant, you are already in the action phase? \n\nWhat if it's not talking and discovery followed by action planning and \n\nimplementation in the linear way we in the West think about it? \n\nConversation as a Core Process 37 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nTHE RELATIONSHIP BETWEEN TALK AND ACTION\n\nAs he suggested, perhaps the whole process is part of a single \n\naction cycle--reflection/insight/harvesting/action planning/imple-\n\nmentation/feedback--in which conversation is a lively core \n\nprocess every step of the way. We're discovering that when people \n\ncare about the questions they are working on and when their con-\n\nversations are truly alive, participants naturally want to organize \n\nthemselves to do whatever has to be done, discovering who cares \n\nabout what and who will take accountability for next steps. Per-\n\nhaps my eighty-four-year-old mom said it best when she shared \n\na fundamental insight from her lifetime of organizational work. \n\n"You see," she mused, "conversation is action. You can think \n\nthings and you can feel things but it doesn't become 'real' until \n\nyou express it. Then it begins to germinate. Other people hear it, \n\nother people begin to feel it, you share ideas together--and if it's \n\nimportant enough, relevant action becomes just a natural thing \n\nthat happens." \n\nThe World Café is designed primarily to generate collective \n\nknowledge-sharing, webs of personal relationships, and new pos-\n\nsibilities for action. A Café dialogue sets the stage for \n\nmore traditional forms of action planning, which often \n\noccur during the same session but at a later point in \n\ntime. Yvon Bastien, of Sanofi-Synthelabo, Canada, raises \n\nan important question for leaders who are reconceiving \n\nthe dichotomy between talk and action and are harness-\n\ning conversation as a core process: "How can we invent \n\nconversational processes for ongoing action planning \n\nand implementation that generate the same energy and \n\nspirit as the World Café generates for strategic thinking and col-\n\nlective insight?" This is a question that those who believe we \n\nneed to "start talking and get to work" can profitably explore as \n\nwe consider how to develop innovative conversational processes \n\nthat are alive throughout the whole action cycle--from initial \n\nexploration through implementation. \n\n38 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nA Café dialogue sets \n\nthe stage for more \n\ntraditional forms of \n\naction planning\n\nDoorways to Dialogue: Entering a Common Courtyard \n\nThe World Café is not the only doorway to the type of dialogue \n\nthat enables us to tap into the power of conversation as a core \n\nprocess for generating innovative possibilities and bringing forth \n\nnew futures. The image I hold of this rich territory comes from \n\nmy experience as a teenager living with my adopted grandmother \n\nin a colonial town in Chiapas, Mexico. When you entered the \n\nhome through the large carved wooden door, the first thing you \n\nsaw was a large central courtyard with vivid bougainvillea, lush \n\nflowers, and verdant trees in big clay pots with a large fountain in \n\nthe center. You could enter the central courtyard by going through \n\nany of the multiple arched entryways that surrounded this open, \n\nflower-filled space in the middle of the house. \n\nFor me, entering the space of authentic dialogue is like enter-\n\ning this central courtyard in the spacious home of our common \n\nhumanity. The World Café is only one valuable doorway into this \n\ncentral courtyard of collective possibility. Strategic dialogues, \n\nindigenous councils, salons, wisdom circles, citizens' deliberative \n\ncouncils, women's circles, study circles, Bohmian dialogue groups, \n\nAppreciative Inquiry, Open Space, Future Search, public delibera-\n\ntion models, and other conversational modalities from many \n\ncultures and historical periods have contributed to and draw on \n\nthis life-affirming experience. Information on many of these is \n\nlisted in "Resources and Connections," which you'll find at the \n\nback of this book. I encourage you to seek out a variety of dia-\n\nlogue approaches. Find the doorways to dialogue that best con-\n\nnect to your personal life experiences, your needs, and your own \n\nunique path of contribution. Then use your creativity and imagi-\n\nnation to become a conversation host in your own life and work. \n\nFor the rest of our time together, we'll be focusing on the the-\n\nory and practice behind the World Café. The seven World Café \n\ndesign principles, which we introduce on the following page and \n\nexplore in the chapters that follow, provide one easy-to-use \n\napproach to engaging the power of conversations that matter, \n\nwhether or not you ever decide to host a formal World Café event. \n\nConversation as a Core Process 39 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nCultivating Conversation as a Core Process \n\nThe following set of seven integrated World Café design principles \n\nhas been developed over the years as a means of intentionally \n\nharnessing the power of conversation for business and social \n\nvalue. Here's a quick overview of each principle: \n\nSet the context: Clarify the purpose and broad parameters within which the dialogue will unfold. \n\nCreate hospitable space: Ensure the welcoming environment and psycho-logical safety that nurtures personal comfort and mutual respect. \n\nExplore questions that matter: Focus collective attention on powerful ques-tions that attract collaborative engage-ment. \n\nEncourage everyone's contribution: Enliven the relationship between the "me" and the "we" by inviting full par-ticipation and mutual giving. \n\nCross-pollinate and connect diverse perspectives: Use the living-system dynamics of emergence through inten-\n\ntionally increasing the diversity and density of connections among perspectives while retaining a common focus on core questions. \n\nListen together for patterns, insights, and deeper questions: Focus shared attention in ways that nurture coherence of thought without losing individual contributions. \n\nHarvest and share collective discoveries: Make collective knowl-edge and insight visible and actionable. \n\nThese simple principles, when used in combination, provide \n\nuseful guidance for anyone seeking creative ways to foster authen-\n\ntic dialogue in which the goal is thinking together and creating \n\nactionable knowledge. \n\n40 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nthe World Café \n\nprinciples for hosting \n\nconversations that matter \n\nConnectdiverse PERSPECTIVES \n\nLIS TEN \n\ntogether \n\nfor insights \n\nEx pl \n\nor e \n\nQ U \n\nES TI \n\nO N \n\nS th \n\nat m \n\nat te \n\nr \n\nCreate hospitable \n\nSPACE \n\nSet the CONTEXT \n\nShare colle \n\nctive \n\nDIS COVERIE \n\nS \n\nEncourage \n\neveryone's \n\nCONTRIBUTIONS\n\nThink about the conversations taking place in your organi-zation or community. To what extent do members actively consider and work with conversation as a core process for creating business or social value? \n\nIf you began to consider conversation a fundamental means for co-creating value in your organization, what specific implications would it have for how you do your own work? \n\nWhat are some practical ways you can imagine to raise people's awareness, in your family, your organization, or your community, about the power of conversation as a key means for creating valued outcomes--whether those be tangible outcomes, like new ideas, or intangible ones, like trust, respect, and a feeling of inclusion? \n\nConversation as a Core Process 41 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nQuestions for Reflection\n\n42 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nT H R E E \n\nAll thinking and learning takes place in a context. The \n\nmind always seeks context in order to create meaning. \n\n--Ed Clarke, Ph.D., educator and author of \n\nDesigning and Implementing an Integrated Curriculum \n\nPRINCIPLE 1 \n\nSet the Context\n\n43 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat i f context i s l ike the \n\nbanks of a r iver through which \n\ncol lec t ive meaning f lows?\n\n44 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nDE CONGRESO A CONVERSACIÓN (FROM CONFERENCE TO \n\nCONVERSATION): MEXICO'S NATIONAL FUND FOR SOCIAL ENTERPRISE \n\nAs Told By \n\nCarlos Mota Margain \n\nI'm happy to share my experience with you because it was a pos-\n\nitive outcome from what seemed, at the beginning, to be a big \n\nchallenge. I was contacted by Laura Certucha, director of planning for the National Fund for Social Enterprise (FONAES), part of our Ministry of Economy. FONAES is a key player helping to create the emerging "social economy" in Mexico--that is, an economy that considers social and community needs as well as purely economic factors for improving the lives of our people, especially those who are most disadvantaged. Such factors include microenterprises, cooperatives, and other local development efforts. \n\nLaura was planning an official congreso, a major conference, on the emerging social economy. The original purpose of the confer-ence was to bring leading experts from around the world together to share their research and experience on the social economy with key government officials, mainly from the Ministry of Economy and the Ministry of Social Development. I've attended many con-gresos in Mexico. They are mostly very formal and highly structured official events with keynote speakers or a panel of experts. Some-times there are questions and answers, but it is generally a one-way information-sharing opportunity with little real interchange between speakers and members of the audience or among the par-ticipants themselves. \n\nDuring my conversations with Laura about how we could understand the deeper oppor-tunities for the social economy at this key moment in Mexico's history, we came up with an idea. Why not shift the context of this event from presentation to exploration? Why not move it from being a congreso, a formal conference, toward becoming a foro, a more interactive forum, where we could be in real learning conversations--everyone together? Yes, new ideas could come from international experts. But we could then use the wisdom and experience of all of the three hundred participants to consider our unique Mexican reality and context. Everyone could imagine together what kind of innovations could happen to create a new economy that includes social as well as economic interests. \n\nIn my heart I wanted to help create an event that was truly alive--alive in itself, not just in the transmission of ideas and knowledge from the few to the many. Of course, we had to \n\nCarlos Mota Margain, a long-\n\ntime colleague, has intro-\n\nduced Café approaches to a \n\nvariety of corporate, non-\n\nprofit, university, and govern-\n\nment settings in Mexico on \n\nissues as diverse as scenario \n\nplanning, visioning, youth \n\ndevelopment, and multi-\n\nstakeholder engagement. \n\nSetting the appropriate con-\n\ntext within which the Café's \n\npurpose and process will \n\nunfold enables members to \n\njoin together within a com-\n\nmon frame of reference. This \n\nis the tale of Carlos's collabo-\n\nration in creating the context \n\nfor a major government \n\nforum on social and eco-\n\nnomic development. \n\nS T O RY\n\nSet the Context 45 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nget the approval for this shift from the key directors of FONAES. To our delight, they said yes! However, I think people were still quite concerned because it had never been done that way before. We needed to find a way for key people to experience this new "Café way" of being together. So I suggested to Laura that we first host a mini-World Café with the twelve top FONAES executives. In this three-round mini-Café, we focused on the best possibilities for the overall future of FONAES that this foro could help achieve. Well, many interesting ideas were brought forward in that mini-Café. We documented the key insights and circulated them to the twelve senior executives. That raised the overall energy and excitement for the foro at the most senior level. We had built a good working relationship with the senior leaders of the event, but there was still a lot of detailed work to do! \n\nWe learned that it takes careful planning--and time--to meet the challenge of changing from traditional ways of doing things. Our core design team met twice a week over a four-month period. We didn't mind the hard work, though. It was fun and exciting to try this new way of being together at an official gathering. \n\nThe first thing was to develop the invitation list. Once we had shifted our thinking away from the original concept of one-way information-sharing by experts, it changed our think-ing about appropriate participants. In order to have the quality of rich conversation and cre-ative thinking we wanted, we would need to invite not only the key stakeholders from government but also businesspeople, farmers, representatives of NGOs, researchers, educa-tors--a microcosm of the whole society! That was important because we realized that no sin-gle stakeholder group by itself can bring forth innovative solutions to complex social issues. \n\nWe also had to create a unique invitation that would signal that this was going to be a different kind of meeting--a special gathering where everyone was going to contribute their unique perspectives on the possibilities for the social economy of Mexico. We sent a very per-sonal and warm invitation to each potential guest. In the invitation we shared the forum's purpose: "To create a space for reflection, for the exchange of national and international experiences, and for the generation of knowledge that will promote and focus efforts for the social economy both in Mexico and around the world." \n\nWe also created a special symbol to represent the different kind of process we hoped to use at the gathering. The symbol was designed to show that the knowledge could come from many sources, but that the sparks of innovation and wisdom would be discovered in the middle of the conversation as a whole. \n\nWe were surprised at how rapidly people responded. Often in Mexico an officially delivered invitation is expected for these types of gatherings, but our personal and warm approach, even in the e-mailed invitations, seemed to be attractive to many people, including key representatives from the World Bank, the Mon-dragon Cooperatives from Spain, and PlaNet Finance from France. \n\nKnowledge from Many Sources\n\n46 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nAnother big thing we did was to set the unique context of the gathering in advance for the experts, so they understood they were coming not just to give their speeches and leave but as learners and contributors for the entire meeting. I think this surprised many, in a good way. \n\nThen we needed to negotiate the parameters of the event. I suggested that we run the entire forum in a Café format. The director of FONAES felt that might be too great a depar-ture from how things were normally done. The compromise was to have the keynote speeches in the mornings in a normal auditorium-style setting. We'd then arrange the speak-ers from the morning keynotes in a small dialogue circle with a moderator and have them engage, in front of the audience, around what they'd learned from each other. That dialogue circle among the experts would be the bridge to the afternoon Café conversations among all the participants. After lunch we'd move to a different nearby room--our World Café--where everybody together would explore key questions based on the morning's input. \n\nThe questions we used at the Café were also very important for helping people under-stand the context in which we were working together. We actually had a short workshop for the core planning team on framing powerful questions. That was important internally at FONAES in order to reshape the context from traditional expert presentations to a focus on questions that would enable all participants to contribute their knowledge and experience. For example, one of the questions was, Beyond academic or technical definitions, what is the true meaning, for you, of a social economy? We sent the Café questions to keynote speakers and other presenters in advance. This helped clarify the framework in which they would share their own reflections in their morning keynote addresses as well as in the circle dialogues among key speakers and presenters prior to each afternoon's Café. \n\nWe had some real challenges in making the physical environment suitable for this kind of conversational gathering. First we had to change hotels, since we needed flexible space-- both for the Café conversations and for hosting the learning exhibits of various social econ-omy projects, which were designed to provide food for thought and stimulate more informal conversations. Second, hotels in Mexico only have banquet tables that seat ten, so we had to rent special small tables. Then there were other details, like getting the colored markers and the small flower vases and the flowers. These may seem like little things, but you have to understand that we were dealing with a Mexican government institution. The FONAES \n\nfinance department had never heard of a conference with small tables, little vases with flow-ers, special white-paper tablecloths, and cups with markers for hundreds of people! It was an ongoing negotiation, but they were great and in the end most everything was approved. \n\nThe day of the event arrived. What we said to open the Café was as important, I think, as all the advance work we had done to set a different context. We found that it's necessary to help people know what's the appropriate "Café way" of being and learning together. Oth-erwise, everyone will revert to their traditional ways of doing things. I reminded people about the invitation they had received--that they had been invited to participate in a conversation, not just to listen to presentations. I also told them that in a Café dialogue everyone is equal,\n\nSet the Context 47 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nno one is higher or lower in status, and every idea is a valid contribution. I said that every person had something valuable from his or her experience to share--even though there might be rural farmers sitting next to very well-known government officials and key business executives. After I gave the explanation of how we were going to work together, I asked every-one to move to a new table so we could make sure we had a good mix of life experience and perspectives at each table. FONAES representatives served as the initial hosts to welcome peo-ple to their tables and to make everyone feel comfortable. \n\nI think people were a little nervous in the beginning. The first round of the Café started out a bit slowly and hesitantly . . . but only for a few minutes. Soon people started to engage very openly, and a lively exchange of ideas began. When it was time to move into the second round, you could feel that people were very curious about what would happen next. They liked moving around the room and meeting new people. \n\nAfter three rounds of conversation, we opened a plenary session in which we invited the members to share not only their findings but also their feelings--what the conversation had meant to them personally. There were many heartfelt expressions, particularly from rural people who were surprised they could talk so easily with top officials. And the more senior officials from government and business said they learned so much from people they nor-mally would not have the chance to be in that kind of conversation with. Changing the con-text so that everyone was equal without authority or rank really worked! \n\nBy the end of the second day's World Café, it was as if we were cultivating one big gar-den, with the ideas from participants and experts as the nutrients we were putting into the soil and the seeds we were planting. By the third afternoon's Café, a change seemed to come over the group. They became much more reflective than before. There was an unusual feel-ing of aliveness, momentum, and depth. For example, the whole room would fall silent as people took in what one member of the group was saying. The mood changed from just being, "Oh, wow, this is fun and exciting," to a deeper contemplation of the real meaning of what each person was saying and the possibilities it might offer for the future. Those were the moments of magic for me. \n\nWhat were the outcomes of taking the risk and shifting the context of this gathering from the traditional way? First of all, it created a very different atmosphere from that of most con-gresos. It was very warming to my heart to see the honest way people appreciated this oppor-tunity to develop new ideas and insights that they could use to improve their own distinctive endeavors and to collaborate for the common good of Mexico. I could imagine the power-ful impact of these three hundred people each taking just one key discovery about the emerg-ing social economy into their own networks of relationships and institutions. \n\nIn terms of practical outcomes, the director of FONAES told the group that the insights from these conversations were going to help create the agenda and focus for the organiza-tion's efforts over the coming year. In addition, a second international foro has been sched-uled to build on our original findings and insights. One of the experts who attended, Marcos\n\nI \n\nt was Eric Vogt, a pioneer in developing online learning com-\n\nmunities, who first showed David and me the importance of \n\ncontext in providing the framework in which individual and \n\ncollective meaning-making unfolds. A number of years ago, Eric \n\ncalled me from his home in Cambridge, Massachusetts. He had \n\nrecently read an article that I had written with David on building \n\norganizations as learning communities (Brown and Isaacs, 1995). \n\nHe excitedly told me, "We have to meet and visit. Our values and \n\nthinking are so complementary!" Only later did we both realize \n\nthat we'd already met and visited together--as teenagers more \n\nthan thirty years earlier, dancing in the courtyard of my adopted \n\ngrandmother's home in Chiapas, Mexico, where Eric's father was \n\nhead of the Harvard Anthropology Project. It's a small world! \n\nEric had recently written an article on organizational learning \n\nentitled "Learning Out of Context" (1995). It included a very sim-\n\nple but key idea (left) that allowed David and me to visualize and \n\nillustrate the role of context in holding, surrounding, and inform-\n\ning both the content and the process of a great conversation. \n\nThe Role of Context \n\nContext is the situation, frame of reference, and surrounding fac-\n\ntors that, in combination, help shape the ways we make meaning \n\nof our experiences. Most of us are not used to thinking consciously \n\n48 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nde Castro Sanz, president of the Confederation for the Social Economy of Spain, said this was such a different and useful learning experience that he'd like to bring the Café format to his Spanish colleagues for engaging this kind of multisector thinking and dialogue on other key development issues. \n\nMy key discovery from this World Café experience? It's amazing what can happen when you shift the context to provide simple interactive formats for diverse stakeholders to really talk together as equals across traditional boundaries. It felt like a big challenge at first, but it was worth the risk! Seeing people from such different social and economic backgrounds cre-ating ideas together for Mexico's future was deeply rewarding. \n\n. . . \n\nPerspectives & Observations\n\nabout context even though its presence is critical for our minds to \n\ncreate patterns of understanding (Johnson, 2001). We become con-\n\nfused and uncomfortable when the relation between the larger \n\ncontext and the content we are exploring or the process that's \n\nbeing used is unclear. \n\nSetting the context involves intentionally creating the flexible \n\nboundaries in which the group's collaborative learning unfolds. I \n\noften like to imagine context as the banks of a river that help chan-\n\nnel the flow of meaning without controlling it. In planning for a \n\nconversation that matters, the Café host and planning team play a \n\nkey role as architects of context by helping to focus (but not con-\n\ntrol) the content as well as providing support to shape the process of \n\nthe dialogue--not only in advance of but also during the gathering. \n\nThe nested-bowl illustration on the previous page has helped \n\nus evolve our thinking about the elements of contextual architec-\n\nture that are involved in shaping an effective Café conversation. \n\nWe are finding that setting an appropriate context for Café con-\n\nversations requires paying attention to three key elements: purpose, \n\nparticipants, parameters. At first glance, these three elements may \n\nseem rather simple. However, each of these contextual elements \n\nbuilds on and relates to the others, creating a whole system that \n\nsurrounds and informs the conversation. They are an essential \n\npart of bringing "coherence without control" to the conversa-\n\ntional experience. \n\nThe amount of time and attention Café hosts pay to these \n\ncontextual elements is usually proportional to the size and com-\n\nplexity of the gathering. Carlos's Mexican foro was a large event \n\nrequiring considerable up-front planning as well as context setting \n\nat the event itself. For a smaller Café gathering, all three elements \n\nmight be addressed in a single design conversation. Hosts have \n\nfound, however, that it's important to consider each of these ele-\n\nments in planning a Café gathering, even if only briefly. Aspects \n\nof these elements can then be introduced as part of the contextual \n\nframework at the event itself. \n\nOnce you've hosted a number of Cafés or other collaborative \n\nconversations, each of these aspects of setting the context becomes \n\nSet the Context 49 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nELEMENTS OF CONTEXT\n\nmore intuitive as you call on learnings from previous gatherings. \n\nIn addition, the next two chapters, "Create Hospitable Space" \n\n(chapter 4) and "Explore Questions That Matter" (chapter 5), \n\nintroduce two complementary Café principles that build on and \n\nenhance the effectiveness of the initial contextual architecture. \n\nBecause context can be an amorphous concept, I'll use Car-\n\nlos's Mexican foro as the example throughout this chapter to \n\nground our exploration of contextual architecture. Where appro-\n\npriate, I've illuminated key points in small exhibits that compare \n\nthe original congreso concept with the foro approach that Carlos \n\nand his colleagues actually used. Both Carlos and another World \n\nCafé colleague, Arian Ward, have helped develop simple yet prac-\n\ntical ways to shape the context for a Café conversation in order \n\nto increase the likelihood of accessing living knowledge and \n\nbreakthrough thinking. David and I are grateful for their insight, \n\ncommitment, and collaboration. \n\nClarify the Purpose \n\nClarifying the purpose includes several elements. \n\nUnderstand the Current Situation Clarifying the relevant aspects of the larger situation in which you \n\nare hosting a learning conversation is a powerful initial step in \n\ndefining the overall purpose of the Café. There may be social, eco-\n\nnomic, political, organizational, community, or even interpersonal \n\nfactors. Ask yourself, What is the real-life situation or need that makes \n\nthis conversation relevant, and why is it important? For Carlos and the \n\nforo planning team, the complex realities of Mexico's current \n\nsocioeconomic situation, coupled with the need to seek the broad-\n\nest range of creative new thinking for the nation's social economy, \n\nhelped guide the design of the event. When they assessed Mexico's \n\ncurrent realities and needs, it revealed the value of engaging the \n\nknowledge and experience not only of traditional experts but also \n\nof multisector stakeholders who could provide a rich set of per-\n\nspectives. The resulting shift--from presentation-based congreso to \n\nconversational foro--reflected this understanding of the current \n\nreality and larger needs that made the event important. \n\n50 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nExplore Your Own Design Assumptions In addition to articulating your beliefs or assumptions about the \n\nnature of the current situation, it is useful to explore your \n\nassumptions about how people create knowledge together. This \n\ncan make a big difference in how you articulate the purpose of \n\nthe event, determine if a Café is the appropriate approach, and \n\nframe the invitation to the participants. For example, in the Mexi-\n\ncan case, although all agreed that Mexico was experiencing com-\n\nplex socioeconomic challenges that required a thoughtful \n\nresponse, the design team tested their traditional beliefs about the \n\nrole of experts in shaping that response. The conference planners \n\nbegan to see knowledge creation as a social and community \n\nendeavor--not the sole purview of experts--and one in which \n\nconversation is a core process. The event, therefore, was crafted \n\nwith this assumption at its core. \n\nThe World Café is grounded in a growing body of research \n\nabout the social nature of learning (Allee, 2002; Lambert and \n\nothers, 1995; Sandow and Allen, 2004; Wenger, 1998; Wenger \n\nand others, 2002). As the planning team's design partner, Carlos \n\nhelped the team see the creative opportunities in exploring their \n\nearly assumptions in order to determine the appropriateness of \n\na Café approach for accessing the collective intelligence of the \n\nentire conference community. Chapter 10 gives additional infor-\n\nmation on when (and when not) to choose the World Café. \n\nSet the Context 51 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n◗ Mexico is facing complex socioeconomic challenges. \n\n◗ Formal presentations of a situation analysis by Mexican and international experts to government officials can provide the most relevant data for effective planning and decision making. \n\n◗ The role of the nonpresenting participants is to become more informed about the best thinking in the field. \n\n◗ Mexico is facing complex socioeconomic challenges. \n\n◗ Diverse voices create a richer situation analysis by bringing new insight to planning and decision making around complex issues. \n\n◗ People want to contribute their knowledge, learn together, and make a difference. \n\n◗ Each participant in the conversation has expertise and can make a unique contribution. \n\nDESIGN ASSUMPTIONS \n\nCongreso Foro\n\nArticulate the "Big Why" A clear purpose provides a kind of North Star--the deeper inten-\n\ntion that guides the design of the Café. It is what determines your \n\ncriteria for success. One way to consider the purpose of the Café is \n\nsimply to ask, Why are we bringing people together? What need (or \n\nneeds) will this conversation fulfill? At times, creating a special name \n\nfor your World Café dialogue can help signal its \n\nbroad purpose--for example, Leadership Café, \n\nStrategy Café, Product Development Café, or simply \n\nDiscovery Café. In Carlos's story, the purpose \n\nevolved from providing expert information on the \n\nsocial economy to creating a collaborative space for \n\nexchanging national and international experiences \n\nand for generating knowledge that could focus \n\nlarge-scale efforts on behalf of the social economy, \n\nboth in Mexico and beyond. \n\nClarify Possible Outcomes A Café is purposely designed to avoid predetermined outcomes. \n\nHowever, illuminating the most exciting possibilities and consid-\n\nering the possible outcomes or success criteria that matter most \n\nfor achieving your purpose can be important aspects of shaping a \n\nCafé conversation. For example, tangible outcomes might include \n\ndiscovering new strategic directions, considering innovative pro-\n\ngram or policy options, or illuminating new business opportuni-\n\nties for a specific product. \n\nHowever, Café conversations are generally not focused, at least \n\ninitially, on finding an immediate answer or a solution. Often the \n\nmost powerful Café outcome is discovering the right questions to \n\nask in relation to a critical issue, or simply creating the opportu-\n\nnity to think and explore your situation together with others for \n\nthe first time. These and other intangible outcomes--like building \n\nnew relationships, sharing knowledge, and providing opportuni-\n\nties for contribution by those traditionally excluded from decision \n\nmaking--often have great payoffs that create long-term value. \n\n52 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nA clear purpose provides \n\na kind of North Star-- \n\nthe deeper intention \n\nthat guides the design \n\nof the Café\n\nDetermine the Right Participants \n\nDiversity of thought and experience is perhaps the single most important \n\ncriterion for gaining new insight and accessing collective wisdom. For \n\nthis reason, the participants you invite are a key part of setting the con-\n\ntext in which innovative possibilities will unfold. Often in designing a \n\nCafé, the participant group is already defined from the outset. However, \n\nit is always useful to ask, Who else do we want to join this conversation in \n\norder to help us achieve our purpose? What additional perspectives might con-\n\ntribute valuable insights? Who could receive real benefit by being a part of the \n\nconversation? We often neglect to invite participants who will (a) likely be \n\naffected by the outcomes of the conversation or (b) have unique or dif-\n\nferent perspectives to offer. These are key voices to include in any Café \n\nconversation, and are useful to consider when exploring who should be \n\ninvited. For example, in hosting a Café on the future of education in a \n\nlocal community, one Café design team decided to invite not only teach-\n\ners, parents, and school administrators but also students of all ages who \n\nwould be affected by their decisions. \n\nSet the Context 53 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n◗ FONAES is positioned as a key actor in the global exchange on Mexico's new social economy. \n\n◗ State-of-the-art knowledge is brought to Mexico by international experts. \n\n◗ Government representatives are provided with relevant data for future planning. \n\n◗ FONAES is positioned as a global convener and host, creating a space for reflection, exploration, exchange of experiences, and generation of knowl-edge to promote and focus efforts on behalf of the social economy in Mexico and beyond. \n\n◗ Expertise is used to stimulate multistakeholder dialogue and access collective wisdom for future decision making. \n\n◗ All participants become co-learners and contributors. \n\nPOSSIBLE OUTCOMES \n\nCongreso Foro \n\n◗ Presenters and panelists: international experts. \n\n◗ Audience: Mexican government officials from key government ministries. \n\n◗ Everyone is a participant; international experts act as conversation catalysts and learning partners. \n\n◗ Representatives of a cross section of Mexican soci-ety participate, including government officials, NGOs, social-cooperative enterprises, businesses of varied sizes (rural and urban), legislators, educators, researchers, and students. \n\nSTAKEHOLDERS/PARTICIPANTS \n\nCongreso Foro\n\nBe Creative with Parameters \n\nFinally, parameters are the third element of contextual architecture. \n\nClarify Your Learning Approach Traditional meeting approaches focused on PowerPoint presenta-\n\ntions and speeches are often ill-suited for enabling people to \n\nthink together intelligently about complex challenges and ques-\n\ntions. Yet as Carlos found out, with courage and creativity it's \n\noften possible to design a powerful new learning context from \n\nquite traditional beginnings. The Café format is adaptable enough \n\nthat you can often integrate it into more traditional meeting for-\n\nmats and environments. But it is important to consider how far \n\nyou can push the design parameters. In Carlos's case, the plan-\n\nning group's willingness to stretch the parameters of a traditional \n\nconference model resulted in a very different and innovative \n\nframework for eliciting individual and collective meaning. At the \n\nsame time, it still retained many traditional meeting structures, \n\nsuch as the keynote presentations in the morning. Determining \n\nthe overall learning and conversational approach, as well as clari-\n\nfying the ways a Café conversation can contribute, helps you set \n\nthe context by framing the major design elements of the gathering \n\nyou are hosting. \n\n54 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n◗ Auditorium style. \n\n◗ Formal keynote presentations. \n\n◗ Expert panels. \n\n◗ Written questions from audience with verbal responses by presenters or panelists. \n\n◗ Auditorium-style formal presentations. \n\n◗ Dialogue circles among presenters with moderator. \n\n◗ Café conversations focused on core questions elicit-ing everyone's contribution, including experts as participants and FONAES execs as table hosts. \n\n◗ Each day's Café insights are fed into next day's conversations via overnight summaries, shared by Economic Ministry representative. \n\n◗ Daily Cafés create an evolving/deepening collabora-tive dialogue around key themes. \n\nLEARNING APPROACH \n\nCongreso Foro\n\nDetermine Pre-Event Activities Although the FONAES gathering involved very detailed advance \n\nplanning, even when hosting a two- or three-hour Café it's impor-\n\ntant to pay attention to key aspects that will make it a successful \n\nevent. For example, framing and issuing the invitation, whether \n\nvia mail, phone, e-mail, or fax, is a key aspect of setting the \n\nappropriate pre-event context for the gathering. It helps set the \n\nexpectation in advance that this will be a different kind of meet-\n\ning where everyone will have an active opportunity to contribute. \n\nCarlos's story details some of the other types of pre-event activi-\n\nties that proved essential to shaping the context in which the foro \n\nunfolded, including sending a personal invitation to each guest \n\nthat explained the forum's purpose, creating a special symbol to \n\nrepresent the different kind of process they hoped to use at the \n\ngathering, and clarifying to the experts what would be expected of \n\nthem. As a host, use your imagination to determine which pre-\n\nevent activities are needed for planners, presenters (if they will be \n\nincluded), and participants to enter the gathering with a common \n\nset of expectations and sense of positive anticipation regarding \n\nthe purpose and process. \n\nSet the Context 55 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n◗ Periodic planning meetings. \n\n◗ Formal invitations and presenter scheduling. \n\n◗ Formal meeting space is secured. \n\n◗ Core planning team is formed to do detailed design--reflecting diverse perspectives and based on agreements on how to interact. \n\n◗ A mini-Café is held with FONAES execs about best possibilities. \n\n◗ Personal invitations are sent to everyone, which explain the new conversational context. \n\n◗ Workshop is held on framing Café questions. \n\n◗ Early attention is paid to negotiating resources, venue, physical layout, Café setup, and supplies. \n\n◗ Presenters are briefed on Café questions and process, and their role as learning partners. \n\nPRE-EVENT ACTIVITIES \n\nCongreso Foro\n\nFind the Appropriate Location We'll talk in much more depth about creating hospitable spaces for \n\nauthentic conversation in the following chapter. We've found this \n\nto be one of the most essential parameters to work with early on \n\nbecause most conference and retreat venues, as well as meeting \n\nrooms in organizations, are poorly equipped to accommodate con-\n\nversation-based processes. In Carlos's case, simply finding a hotel \n\nballroom was not sufficient. He needed a hotel that could accom-\n\nmodate large auditorium presentations while simultaneously \n\nConsider Post-Event Follow-Up Often the form of the follow-up is determined at the Café event \n\nitself. However, thinking in advance about what kind of follow-up \n\nmight occur is an important parameter to consider in setting the \n\ncontext, because it can affect how you frame the invitation and set \n\nthe context during the gathering itself. For example, if you know \n\nthat participant insights will become part of a longer-term devel-\n\nopment process, it might be important to share that in your \n\ninitial invitation. Follow-up needs or plans can also determine the \n\nmanner in which you document the Café. In the case of the foro, \n\nalthough a number of key next steps emerged in the moment \n\nbased on the group's Café conversations, resources were dedicated \n\nin advance to create a follow-up government publication detailing \n\nthe presentations and outputs from the event. \n\n56 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n◗ Government creates documentation of presentations. \n\n◗ Possible follow-up congreso is considered. \n\n◗ Government publishes presentations, learnings from expert dialogue circles, and summary of Café insights. \n\n◗ Next steps emerge from Café dialogues, including possible regional Cafés. \n\n◗ Interim discoveries are scheduled to feed into a follow-up foro. \n\n◗ Virtual learning exchange among experts is facili-tated by FONAES. \n\nPOST-EVENT FOLLOW-UP \n\nCongreso Foro\n\nSet the Context 57 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nhaving enough flexible space to host both World Café conversa-\n\ntions and participant exhibitions in order to stimulate new \n\nthinking and breakthrough possibilities. \n\nSecure Needed Resources and Setup Important parameters include time, budget, equipment, furnish-\n\nings, and supplies. These might seem like obvious considerations, \n\nyet what appears simple often requires unexpected negotiations to \n\nstretch these parameters in order to create a conversational context \n\nwhere people can think together in new ways. As the following \n\nexhibit demonstrates, Carlos's foro required a quite different set of \n\nresources than the congreso that had been originally considered. \n\n◗ Setup is for for formal auditorium presentations and lectures. \n\n◗ Chairs are placed in rows. \n\n◗ Time is set for Q&A from audience at plenary sessions. \n\n◗ Setup is for formal auditorium presentations, dia-logue circles, and Café conversations. \n\n◗ Budget is allocated for rental of small tables, tablecloths, butcher paper, markers, and flowers on each table. \n\n◗ Appropriate time is set aside each afternoon for Café conversations. \n\nRESOURCES AND SETUP \n\nCongreso Foro \n\nSet the Context at the Event We'll talk in more depth about the role of the Café host, as well \n\nas specific Café etiquette, in chapter 10. We've found, however, \n\nthat since Café conversations are a departure from traditional \n\nmeeting experiences, it is important to reset the context during \n\nthe event--often at several junctures. This includes clarifying the \n\nsituation or issue that has brought the group together--the "Big \n\nWhy" of the Café. It also includes highlighting the interactive \n\nlearning orientation in which this new type of conversational \n\nprocess will take place. As you can see from Carlos's story, the ini-\n\ntial introduction of the Café process at the event can have a signif-\n\nicant impact on the way the conversational vessel is shaped for \n\nindividual and collective meaning to emerge.\n\nOften, in addition to sharing Café etiquette, as Carlos did, \n\nCafé hosts begin with a brief table conversation and whole-group \n\nsharing among participants focused on the following question: \n\nRecall a time when you had a great conversation where real learning or \n\nnew insight occurred--what enabled that to happen? In this way, \n\nmembers have the opportunity to participate in setting the emo-\n\ntional context and framework to support innovative thinking. \n\nSetting the context--at the planning stage, during the event \n\nitself, and in positioning whatever follow-up will occur (or not) \n\nas a result of the conversation--can make or break a collaborative \n\ninquiry. Becoming a creative "architect of context" at every stage \n\nof conversational design and implementation is an art, not only \n\nfor World Café hosts but for anyone seeking to create effective \n\nconditions for authentic dialogue. \n\n58 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n◗ Purpose is clear, but overall process context is not emphasized. \n\n◗ Participants are instructed to write questions after formal presentations for presenter response. \n\n◗ The purpose and process are reiterated throughout the event. \n\n◗ Participants are reminded that they are invited to participate in authentic and active conversation rather than be a passive audience. \n\n◗ The experts' analysis is framed as a resource to provide food for thought. \n\n◗ There is an invitation to engage in a different way of talking and listening with new people. \n\n◗ Everyone's experience and ideas are considered a valuable contribution to the whole. \n\n◗ Café etiquette is explained, and how everyone will work together. \n\n◗ FONAES reps act as table hosts, welcoming a diverse mix of sectors and participants to each table. \n\n◗ Café questions are shaped so that all can contribute. \n\nCONTEXT SETTING AT THE EVENT \n\nCongreso Foro\n\nThink about a meeting, conference, or gathering you've attended that went really well. What was it about how the context was set--either prior to or at the event itself--that contributed to its effectiveness? \n\nConsider an upcoming gathering you are planning. Based on the three key elements of context setting that we've explored in this chapter, what might you pay more atten-tion to in your planning that would help set a conversa-tional tone and invite a greater diversity of perspectives toward the outcomes you are seeking? \n\nHow might you set the context at the event itself to help peo-ple feel comfortable moving into this more collaborative and conversational way of learning together? \n\nSet the Context 59 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nQuestions for Reflection\n\n60 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nF O U R \n\nThe idea is to create a physical space that enables you to \n\nmove around in that space, and to create a social space that \n\nencourages you to swap and share and help each other. \n\nIf you can design the physical space, the social space, and \n\nthe information space together to enhance collaborative \n\nlearning, then that whole milieu turns into a learning \n\ntechnology. People just love working there and they start \n\nlearning with and from each other. \n\n--John Seely Brown, former chief scientist, Xerox Corporation \n\nPRINCIPLE 2 \n\nCreate Hospitable Space\n\n61 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat i f we create environments that \n\nnurture authent ic conversat ion?\n\n62 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nA SYSTEM THINKING TOGETHER: THE PEGASUS SYSTEMS THINKING IN ACTION CONFERENCE \n\nAs Told By \n\nDavid Isaacs \n\nThe annual Systems Thinking in Action (STA) conference, \n\nhosted by Pegasus Communications, focuses on innovative \n\napplications of systems thinking, organizational learning, and new forms of leadership. It attracts about one thousand partici-pants from around the world. The title of that year's conference was "Learning Communities: Building Enduring Capability." To our surprise, the conference coordinator, LeAnne Grillo, called us and asked, "How would you like to do a keynote Café with the whole conference in the hotel ballroom focused on collective processes of learning and knowledge creation?" \n\nWell, that phone conversation created quite a buzz in our office! Juanita, our colleague Nancy Margulies, and I could hardly imagine what it would be like to create a Café for 1,000 people \n\nfrom more than twenty countries. The largest Café we had done up to that point was for 250 people. How would we scale it up to more than four times that size, and still create a wel-coming, intimate Café learning environment for people from diverse professional back-grounds and cultures? \n\nWould it be possible, in the confines of a ninety-minute session, for so many people to explore core questions about the social nature of learning? Could we move beyond a focus on individual contributions and help people appreciate the phenomenon of collective intel-ligence--a "system thinking together"--in that large a group? Our challenge was to create the environment for this experience to happen. Nancy is a gifted graphic artist, illustrator, and designer. Juanita is great at conceptual stuff. And I've learned over the years how to make peo-ple feel comfortable together. So the three of us began to imagine and play with what might be possible when we combined our skills. \n\nHere's what actually happened when we got to the conference. We took one look at the large, sterile, hotel ballroom and thought about how we could transform it into a warm, friendly Café environment for a thousand people--all in the mere forty-five minutes we would have during the break between sessions. We arranged for the hotel to get small, round cocktail tables for four (no small feat in a banquet-oriented hotel). Conference volunteers rounded up red-checkered tablecloths, small vases, and fresh red-and-white carnations for each table. We put white paper sheets over the tablecloths, just like in many cafés, and left a small container of colored markers to use for doodling. \n\nDavid Isaacs, a co-originator \n\nof the World Café, has collab-\n\norated with corporate, com-\n\nmunity, government, health, \n\nand educational leaders to \n\ncraft strategic conversations \n\naround a wide variety of key \n\nissues affecting the future. In \n\nthis story he focuses on cre-\n\nating hospitable space for \n\ncollaborative dialogue in a \n\nvery large group setting. \n\nS T O RY\n\nCreate Hospitable Space 63 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhile we couldn't get rid of the raised stage, we did manage to have the speaker's podium removed. We replaced it with a round café table next to a special type of overhead projector that Nancy could use for prepared graphics as well as for real-time visual recording of the whole-group conversations. Her drawings of the audience's contributions would be projected onto huge video screens so that everyone's comments could be seen as well as heard. We brought in palm trees and other greenery to give the room a warmer, more natu-ral feel. And in a stroke of luck, we discovered an inexpensive way to project different col-ored designs on the huge blank walls. When the lights were dimmed, this gave the effect of walking into an intimate jazz café. \n\nBut we realized that creating a welcoming, hospitable environment for such a large group extended beyond the physical setup of our keynote Café. We wanted to create a dif-ferent feel for the conference as a whole. Our keynote session was on the afternoon of the second day. In order to get people into the Café mood ahead of time, we arranged to have the coffee break areas also set up with a Café theme: red-checkered cloths on the serving tables, as well as on café tables in the foyers nearby. Nancy made beautiful silk banners with different quotes on them, such as this one from Martin Buber: "All real living is meeting." These were hung in the break area, making it look like an art show rather than just a regular get-your-coffee-quick place. We tried to create many subtle signals that something different and interesting was happening here. \n\nWhen it came time to transform the ballroom, Nancy put colorful signs outside in the hallways that said No Entry (Yet!), Café Under Construction. We had a quick meeting with the volunteers, hotel crew, and technicians--everybody involved in the setup. We told them it was important that we all think of ourselves as the co-hosts for this gathering and that in the next forty-five minutes we were going to transform this traditional hotel ball-room into a welcoming World Café. Prior to our keynote, the ballroom had been divided into three formal meeting rooms, so we had our work cut out for us. We put upbeat jazz on the sound system and literally took the walls down. It was amazing to watch the trans-formation. I'll never forget it. \n\nNancy had created hand-drawn, whimsical overheads with quotes from well-known scientists and others that gave a flavor of the key ideas that underpinned our work: \n\n◗ Intelligence emerges as the system connects to itself. \n\n◗ In life, the issue is not control but dynamic interconnectedness. \n\n◗ The real voyage of discovery lies not in seeking new land-scapes but in having new eyes.\n\n64 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nNancy had also made a colorful Welcome to the World Café overhead that would be shown on the large video screen at the front of the room. \n\nWhen the transformation was complete, we opened all the ballroom doors simulta-neously. There to greet each guest was a volunteer host shaking hands and extending a wel-come. Posters at each entryway invited folks to sit with people they didn't know, to welcome each other, and to start getting acquainted. Michael Jones, a gifted musician, played mellow piano music, creating a warm neighborhood café atmosphere. We dimmed the ballroom lights slightly so that our projected designs could be seen all over the room, giving it a warm, intimate feeling even in such a huge space. The Welcome to the World Café sign appeared on one large video screen, while the scientists' quotes alternated on the other. As hundreds of people streamed into the ballroom, our Café was coming to life! \n\nIt was fascinating to notice the shift of collective energy from previous plenary sessions held in this very same room. We could feel the playfulness and curiosity as people began to gather at the small round tables. They immediately began talking with each other. The buzz of conversation filled the room as everyone got settled. \n\nIn an effort to reinforce our role as Café hosts rather than traditional presenters, I opened the gathering by welcoming this group from twenty-seven different countries with their own words for "welcome." I wanted people to see that we were, indeed, a World Café, representing \n\nalmost every continent on the globe. We were a microcosm of the whole system. Perhaps, if we were lucky that day, we would see and experi-ence ourselves as a system thinking together. \n\nPart of creating a hospitable space for every-one to contribute was clarifying how to treat each other well in a Café dialogue. Without doing any formal dialogue teaching or lengthy presentation of guidelines, Nancy briefly shared a few pointers (left) on Café etiquette. \n\nAs people began to explore the first question together, the room came alive! When it was time for the first twenty-minute round to come to a close, I decided to try an experiment. I quietly raised my hand and signaled to the tables closest to me to do the same. As people noticed what was happening, a wave of hands began to rise throughout the huge ballroom and in less than sixty seconds the entire room fell silent. We all laughed as I asked the participants to notice how they collectively already knew what to do.\n\nCreate Hospitable Space 65 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThen I asked people to begin the second round of conversation by moving to another Café table in their general vicinity. The host who remained at the table would share the essence of their conversations from the notes and drawings on their tablecloths, and travel-ers would link and connect ideas they were bringing from their own previous table conver-sations. It was absolutely astounding to watch the 750 travelers at 250 tables say goodbye to their table hosts, pick up their books, bags, and materials, and quickly move to surrounding tables where they were welcomed as guests by their new hosts. Self-organized hosting works! \n\nFor the second and final round, I encouraged people to notice the patterns, themes, and deeper questions that were emerging from their mutual listening and from the cross-polli-nation of new ideas being shared. Now there were four different conversations linked at each table, greatly increasing the number of connections and possible new insights. \n\nI put up my hand again as the round ended. As before, a wave of silence swept the room. Now the room seemed to be filled with a special kind of focused energy. It was palpable. I asked people to notice what it felt like to experience a whole system thinking together about questions it cared about. Instead of normal, small-group report-outs, Juanita and I stepped down into the audience for a "conversation of the whole." We gave a moment of silence and asked people, as they offered their key insights, to imagine themselves thoughtfully con-tributing to weaving a connected "knowledge web" together. During this entire period, Nancy was creating visual images of what was unfolding. As a way of ending, we asked peo-ple to turn to one other person and share a seed idea that they wanted to take home, plant, and allow to grow. \n\nAmazingly, all of this happened in just one and a half hours. When the session was over and people began to leave, volunteers with brightly wrapped boxes were at all the doors, giv-ing each person a gift--a large, colorful poster that Nancy, Juanita, and I had created. This Map of the Territory summarized the assumptions underlying the World Café and included key conceptual quotes, interdisciplinary contributions, simple guidelines for Café hosting, and ways to find additional resources. \n\nEven though the Café was over, it seemed that people didn't want to leave. The hotel staff kept trying to get people to move along because they had to redo the ballroom and put up the wall dividers again for the next session, but it was hard to get people to stop talking. \n\nWhat did I learn from this little experiment about creating hospitable space for large-group dialogue and learning? I discovered the importance of creating a welcoming and infor-mal environment, which included this: \n\n◗ Setting up the room to look like an actual café ◗ Naming the Café (in this case, Knowledge Café) to reflect our purpose ◗ Having the intimacy of small table conversations in a larger common space, and \n\nmaking sure each table felt connected to the larger whole ◗ Using art, music, and greenery\n\nB \n\nefore the birth of the World Café, I'd never thought much \n\nabout hosting. However, for Joy Anderson, a lively social \n\nentrepreneur who uses Café-style conversations to wel-\n\ncome new colleagues into her network, hosting is a way of life. As \n\nwe sat in our hilltop living room where the World Café was dis-\n\ncovered, Joy told me that as a young child she learned from her \n\nparents, two Lutheran ministers, that among many early peoples, \n\noffering food and shelter to travelers was a sacred obligation and \n\na matter of mutual survival. \n\nTravelers brought not only small gifts from their own lands but \n\nalso their personal stories, news from their travels, and novel ideas \n\nto the tables of their hosts. Joy offered a wonderful perspective that \n\nher father, Herbert Anderson, shared one Christmas on the spirit of \n\n66 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n◗ Having volunteers act as greeters and hosts ◗ Using the language of "hosts, travelers, and guests" during the Café rounds to \n\nencourage a spirit of mutual hospitality and friendship ◗ Encouraging living systems images through the use of metaphors, such as planting \n\nseeds and cross-pollinating ideas ◗ Going out into the audience to be with the participants during the conversation \n\nof the whole ◗ Wearing informal yet appropriate attire ◗ Using hand-drawn graphics rather than PowerPoint presentations ◗ Giving gifts to each participant \n\nThese may seem like simple things, but they are rarely done in the context of support-ing collaborative intelligence and knowledge evolution in organizations. Creating a safe, inviting, and informal space is one way of encouraging more generative conversations to emerge. There is something about the physical and social environment of a café that evokes more authentic conversations, even across cultures--conversations that are more creative, playful, curious, intimate, and honest than those in most formal business meetings and off-site retreats. We took a step in that direction in our World Café keynote session and we're learning more all the time. \n\n. . . \n\nPerspectives & Observations\n\nChristian hospitality. He described hospitality as a stance of open-\n\nness toward new people and ideas, a lens that reflects our experi-\n\nence in hosting Café conversations. "Hospitality," he said, "is the \n\nact of affirming gifts in others. It is entertaining ideas that enable us \n\nto see life in a new way.
