When we offer hospitality to the stranger \n\nor a guest, we welcome something new, unfamiliar, and unknown \n\ninto our lives that has the potential to expand our world." Our own \n\nemphasis on encouraging people to serve as Café table hosts who \n\nwelcome travelers as guests or "ambassadors of \n\nmeaning" for each progressive round of conversa-\n\ntion draws on the same spirit of welcome, inclusive-\n\nness, novelty, and mutual contribution that lies at \n\nthe heart of hosting and the creation of hospitable \n\nspace the world over. \n\nIn his wonderful book The Great Good Place (1989), sociolo-\n\ngist Ray Oldenburg focuses on the importance of informal gather-\n\ning places for the development of innovative ideas, democratic \n\npractice, and community life throughout history. His insights on \n\nwhat he calls "the third place" are critical to our understanding of \n\nCafé conversations and why they work. According to Oldenburg, \n\nthe third place is a locale that exists apart from the "womb" of \n\nprivate family space and the "rat race" of the workaday world. \n\nSuch places--including cafés--provide neutral ground where \n\npeople of diverse perspectives and backgrounds can come \n\ntogether in an inclusive way. They offer hospitality. They are \n\nupbeat, and at times, playful. They accept and welcome newcom-\n\ners and lively conversation, which helps maintain their vitality. In \n\nfact, it is the quality of the conversation itself that is the defining \n\ncharacteristic of third places. As Jaida N'ha Sandra puts it in The \n\nJoy of Conversation(1997), a fascinating history of salons and their \n\nrole in social innovation, the point of creating hospitable space is \n\nto encourage the quality of conversation for which salons have \n\nbecome famous as incubators of new thinking. \n\nFor most people, the idea of creating hospitable space is not \n\nactually foreign or new. But what is exciting and novel is the \n\nintentional creation of these environments for our conversations \n\nCreate Hospitable Space 67 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nHospitality is the act of \n\naffirming gifts in others\n\nat work. As one executive said to me, "You know, this is so obvi-\n\nous and so important. These are the everyday human things we \n\npay attention to when we're having a gathering or welcoming \n\nguests in our home, but we just don't think about how important \n\nthey are when we're thinking together with our colleagues about \n\nkey issues." Another added, "Our workplaces, and many hotels \n\nand conference centers, aren't designed to create the right \n\nenvironments for the kind of strategic thinking and quality con-\n\nversation that is so important for the future of our business. \n\nBoardrooms and conference rooms are so sterile and cold. And \n\nthose big tables are barriers too. It's something we're going to \n\nneed to pay a lot more attention to as leaders if we want to \n\nsupport knowledge-sharing and get the best results." \n\nTransforming Traditional Meeting Spaces \n\nWhat do these insights from our traditions of human community \n\nas well as our Café discoveries tell us about the value of creating \n\nhospitable space to catalyze collaborative conversation? In his \n\nprovocative work Shared Minds, Michael Schrage, a former \n\nresearch fellow at MIT's Media Lab, points out that \n\nthe physical setup of most meetings actually subverts \n\ncollaborative effort by focusing on deadening one-\n\nway presentations that "subsidize the excesses of indi-\n\nvidual communication at the expense of collaborative \n\ncommunity" (1990, p. 122). \n\nSchrage focuses on creative ways to make meeting \n\nenvironments more dynamic and interactive, includ-\n\ning creating informal meeting environments, intro-\n\nducing collaborative technology tools, and creating \n\ncommon spaces that enable people to see each other's \n\nideas visually in real time. (The paper tablecloths \n\nused for drawing key ideas provide this opportunity in Café con-\n\nversations.) Schrage concludes that "in many respects it's easier to \n\nget results by changing the meeting environment than by trying to \n\npersuade people to behave differently" (1990, p. 122). \n\n68 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThe physical setup of \n\nmost meetings actually \n\nsubverts collaborative \n\neffort by focusing on \n\ndeadening one-way \n\npresentations\n\nLike Schrage, Roslie Capper of New Zealand knows that envi-\n\nronments matter, and she put that knowledge into practice. While \n\nothers in the field were thinking about the process and the con-\n\ntent of the conversations they were hosting, Roslie was thinking \n\nabout the physical and psychological context that could nurture \n\nthese deeper conversations. "What really gave me pause was the \n\ntime I went to a conference on creating a sustainable future," \n\nRoslie told us. "The speakers were honoring the natural world in a \n\nhotel ballroom with no natural light, no plants, and no fresh air!" \n\nFirst introduced to the World Café at an International \n\nWomen's Dialogue, Roslie had a dream: "I wanted to design a \n\nvenue that would intentionally support the kind of thinking \n\ntogether that I had seen in Café conversations and in dialogue cir-\n\ncles," says Roslie. With seed funding from friends, family, and \n\nassociates, she created TOTEM, a business and meeting center on \n\nAuckland's waterfront. It was designed with curved walls and a \n\nhuge glass roof over the large circular reception area where people \n\ncould gather and mix informally. Art reflecting New Zealand's \n\nmulticultural heritage was placed throughout the building. Roslie \n\nhad comfortable furniture designed in natural, vibrant colors. The \n\nmeeting spaces were outfitted with lots of small round tables with \n\ncomfy chairs of the right size for supporting Café dialogues and \n\nother interactive processes. An Italian cappuccino machine in the \n\nbusiness lounge enabled people to enjoy serving themselves and \n\nnaturally begin conversations with people they hadn't met \n\nbefore--making new friends. "We adopted the motto Connections \n\nMade Easy," says Roslie, "which is very similar to what happens in \n\na Café conversation. For us, TOTEM has been the embodiment of \n\nthe Swedish term for business, närings liv, that I learned from \n\nDavid Isaacs. In English it means 'nourishment for life.' The goal \n\nof TOTEM? To nourish life while connecting people and ideas in \n\nunexpected ways." \n\nPeople began to recognize that something different was hap-\n\npening at TOTEM. Roslie shares that "little by little we began to \n\nprovide organizations with thinking partners to help them create \n\nstrategic conversations at TOTEM using what we might call the \n\nCreate Hospitable Space 69 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\ndeeper World Café philosophy and principles even when it wasn't \n\nthe full Café process itself." Rosalie's dream is to have TOTEM \n\nmeeting venues in every major city in the world to help demon-\n\nstrate the importance of paying attention to the subtle, sometimes \n\ninvisible, aspects of conversational environments in order to \n\nsupport new ways of thinking and being together in the service \n\nof things we care about. \n\nMichael Schrage's insights and Roslie's experiment with \n\nTOTEM mirror our own discoveries in creating interactive Café \n\nenvironments to support collaborative work. Edna Pasher, a Café \n\npioneer from Israel who has worked with key leaders there, points \n\nout, "You have to design the communications environment if you \n\nwant it to work. You have to be an architect of the knowledge \n\necology." It seems like common sense, doesn't it? But perhaps \n\nthat's what Café conversations help us do--remember common \n\nsense and put it into practice. \n\nCreative Approaches to Designing Café Learning Environments \n\nIn talking with Café hosts around the world, the power and \n\nimportance of creating a conversational environment that looks, \n\nto the degree possible, like an actual café is one of our most inter-\n\nesting discoveries. Yet the range of creativity among World Café \n\nhosts is amazing. There are many exciting ways to create hos-\n\npitable space and evoke those intangible human qualities that \n\nmake Café dialogues most effective. As a World Café host, you're \n\nlimited only by your imagination. \n\nFor example, Andrea Dyer, an organizational learning consult-\n\nant who has used Café methods in a range of settings, describes \n\nthe Café environment she created at a global strategy event for a \n\nmajor multinational corporation that involved representatives \n\nfrom over thirty countries. "We had people take pictures of sun-\n\nrises in their home countries and send them in advance," says \n\nAndrea. "And we had them take pictures of their families. We had \n\na gallery in the hallway coming into the room. We changed the \n\nartwork over the three days. We also put the graphic murals from \n\nthe whole-group conversations into the art gallery every day." \n\n(continued on p. 73) \n\n70 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nBUILDING ON TRADITIONS OF DIALOGUE: SAUDI ARAMCO \n\nAs Told By \n\nBronwyn Horvath, Ed.D. \n\nSaudi Aramco is a fully integrated oil company with opera-\n\ntions in exploration, production, refining, and marketing. \n\nWe have over fifty thousand employees and an additional one hundred thousand outside contractors, representing fifty-four different nationalities. Our operations include remote out-posts in the Rub-al-Khali desert and offshore platforms in the Arabian Gulf. We also build schools and roads, run hospitals, and support residential communities the size of small towns. We maintain a fleet of planes and helicopters that rival some national carriers. \n\nAs you can imagine, one of the greatest challenges for any company of this size is effective communication. We'd been strug-gling with the question of how to promote broad information-sharing, let alone meaningful dialogue, at this level of complexity. \n\nThen the senior vice president of Saudi Aramco's Engineer-ing and Operations Service (E&OS), Salim Al-Aydh, attended a Society for Organiza-tional Learning session with Peter Senge in Egypt. At that event, Al-Aydh experienced the World Café firsthand and saw the potential of Café dialogues to align people's aspira-tions with the company's strategic direction. Equally important, he saw the World Café as an extension of the type of dialogue and hospitality that was characteristic of the Arab culture itself. For hundreds of years, tribesmen had met in an open forum called a majlis to discuss the issues they confronted. \n\nIn a radical departure from business as usual, Salem Al-Aydh convened Café '03, a forum building on Saudi Arabia's long-standing tradition of dialogue. He introduced the Café idea as a creative approach for fostering the mutual contribution of over seven hun-dred members of his management team across the four major E&OS functional areas. The purpose of the gathering, he explained, was to "have conversations with each other about things that really matter," focused on the E&OS mission, values, and business plan. \n\nThe logistical challenges of designing Café '03 seemed overwhelming. But when the going gets tough, the tough get innovative! When the creative E&OS design team of Dan Walters, Alfred Hanner, and Jim Davidson discovered that no local hotel could handle the large number of participants, they converted the cavernous Saudi Aramco aviation \n\nBronwyn Horvath is the \n\nLeadership Forum facilitator \n\nfor Saudi Aramco, Saudi Ara-\n\nbia's national oil company. \n\nThe World Café has been \n\nused with groups of over \n\nseven hundred people to \n\nbridge organizational hierar-\n\nchies and build on Arab soci-\n\nety's historical traditions of \n\ndialogue. This story shows \n\nthat hospitable space can be \n\ncreated in a way that suits \n\nany culture. \n\nCreate Hospitable Space 71 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nS T O RY\n\nhangar into the largest Café on the Arabian Peninsula! They created new ways to meet the audiovisual and technical requirements in that unlikely setting, mounting numerous screens on the hangar walls so that all participants could see the speakers. They brought in hundreds of Arabian carpets to transform the hangar into an inviting, intimate Café, Middle Eastern style. The carpets also served to muffle the sound of seven hundred peo-ple in exuberant conversation. \n\nThe more organized Café conversations were complemented by a social lunch that encouraged informal exchange. All Café participants, as well as guests from the aviation department, sat on the floor around a hundred large platters of lamb and rice for a traditional Arabian meal--a kabsah. We used the occasion to host a ceremony honoring the outstanding contributions of individuals and teams during the previous year. It was a true cele-bration of people's capacity to give their best--a day of "serious fun" for leaders at all levels. \n\nIn March 2004, we hosted our most ambi-tious Café to date--the Shaybah Café--at our remote Rub-al-Khali desert facility. We brought together over two hundred members of Saudi Aramco's senior leadership with CEOs and business leaders from across the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia to share knowledge, forge new relationships, and contribute to building the nation's business community as a learning network. After the Café, we welcomed all two hundred participants to gather under the stars in the red sand dunes of one the most awe-inspiring deserts of the world. The group's sharing under the night sky--the culmination of our earlier Café dialogues-- was powerful and moving. It connected many people back to their desert heritage, and to their desire to contribute the essence of this rich heritage to future generations. I've dis-covered that the Café concept strikes a familiar chord, not only with Saudi Arabs but with the many other cultures that are represented in the company. Whether people are Egypt-ian, Turkish, Malaysian, American, or Korean, they all have cultural traditions of sitting down together in a social setting to discuss their hopes and dreams for the future. \n\nOur Cafés, by drawing on local traditions of hospitality, have made it possible for peo-ple to build wider personal networks of business relationships across traditional bound-aries. When you've had meaningful dialogue over time--you've shared ideas and spread the possibilities for new ways for people to contribute--change happens. The World Café is a simple, natural way to foster it. Maybe that's why I've seen Café conversations take root and grow and thrive at Saudi Aramco while many other programs have come and gone. \n\n. . . \n\n72 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nSaudi Aramco Café\n\nAs Andrea discovered, creating a personal connection is a key \n\nelement in creating hospitable space, as is the inclusion of art. \n\nThink of those elements that make a café or a living room invit-\n\ning--the pictures on the wall, groups of plants, vases of flowers, \n\nand other homey touches. In this setting, Andrea added the extra \n\npersonal element of having the artwork come from the partici-\n\npants' home countries. Not only were they able to bring some-\n\nthing of their personal lives into this work setting, but the art \n\ngallery also honored the diversity of cultures in the Café. \n\nBo Gyllenpalm, the former CEO of Phonogram, a Philips \n\nsubsidiary in Sweden, and himself a very experienced Café host, \n\ndescribes another innovative Café setting. It was an outdoor \n\nDinner Café that David and I hosted under a tent on the island \n\nof Lidingö, outside Stockholm. We invited fifty of our Swedish \n\nfriends, many of whom did not know each other, \n\nto be part of a World Café experience in which \n\nthey moved to a different table for each course of \n\nthe meal. At each turn we posed a different query \n\nto help people explore what they cared about and \n\nthe questions at the heart of their lives and work. \n\nWhen I asked Bo, "What do you think helped the \n\nDinner Café work well?" he smiled and said, "Well, the whole \n\natmosphere. The surroundings were very beautiful. It was the gar-\n\nden, the water, and the very relaxed atmosphere. It created an \n\nimmediate openness to what would happen there. It didn't force \n\nor push us into discussion, but I was surprised at how fast people \n\nwere able to speak from their heart." \n\nBringing nature to the Café--or the Café to nature, as we did \n\nin this case--serves as a reminder that the Café itself is a natural \n\nprocess, reflecting nature's deepest self-organizing principles. To \n\nthe degree that you, as a host, create a space that taps into this \n\nelement, you invite participants to engage more easily in authentic \n\nconversations and enjoy creative ways of being with one another. \n\nYou can invite members to experiment with having a different \n\nconversation in a number of ways. For example, Colonial Pipeline \n\nCorporation created a more inviting environment for strategic \n\nCreate Hospitable Space 73 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nCreating a personal con-\n\nnection is a key element in \n\ncreating hospitable space\n\nconversation by explicitly adopting the name "The Pipeline Pub" \n\nto signal a more informal tone for their Café dialogues. In \n\nanother business setting, Tammy Sicard, an organizational learn-\n\ning consultant, used an Italian café theme for participants who \n\nwere charged with crafting a global human resources strategy \n\nafter a major corporate merger. For Tammy, focusing on fun and \n\ncreativity in the Café was an essential part of both reducing the \n\nnatural tensions created by a post-merger situation, and for open-\n\ning the participants up to experimentation and innovation in \n\ndeveloping their new HR strategy. "First, we had HR people from \n\nall over the world sit on a panel and talk about the challenges \n\nand questions unique to their area," she said. "Then we taught \n\npeople how to do mind mapping (a visual recording process). \n\nAfter a break we had them walk into the Knowledge Café. It was \n\nset up like an Italian café--Italian opera music playing, Chianti, \n\nand red-and-white checkered tablecloths. The minute you walked \n\nin, it felt like a different place. People had both fun \n\nand rich discussions about topics they had a shared \n\ninterest in. They were using their mind-mapping \n\nskills right on the butcher paper on the tables. Peo-\n\nple hooked right into it." \n\nThe integration of mind-mapping practices and \n\nother visual elements in the Café brings right-brain \n\nprocessing into the conversation and allows for the \n\nintegration of different learning styles. It has often \n\nbeen our experience that one person at a table might \n\nnot say a word during a Café, but then he or she will \n\ndraw a picture that coalesces the whole conversation. \n\nWe're continuing to discover ways to help the Café environment feel \n\nsafe for people to bring their unique gifts and perspectives to the \n\nconversation in whatever way is most comfortable. \n\nPart of the challenge of creating powerful conversations-- \n\nespecially ones about questions that really matter to people--is \n\nthat there is often not enough trust to make those conversations \n\nsafe for people to share their deepest beliefs and most courageous \n\n74 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThe integration of mind-\n\nmapping practices and \n\nother visual elements in \n\nthe Café brings right-\n\nbrain processing into the \n\nconversation\n\nideas. That was clearly the challenge facing Sharif Abdullah, direc-\n\ntor of the Commonway Institute in Portland, Oregon. \n\nAfter being introduced to the World Café concept at a Fetzer \n\nInstitute meeting focused on "Peace Building for the Twenty-First \n\nCentury," Sharif decided to experiment with a Commons Café, \n\nbringing people of diverse backgrounds together to explore issues \n\nof race, class, ethnicity, values, and politics. A variety \n\nof local organizations were asked to send members of \n\ntheir constituencies. Sharif recalls, "Our goal was not \n\nsimply to have members feel good about each other. \n\nWe wanted people to shift their consciousness from \n\nthinking 'I am separate' to thinking 'we are one.'" \n\nSharif describes his choice of location as key to \n\nsuccess. "We wanted to have a relatively light and very \n\nsafe atmosphere. We didn't want anyone to feel, 'I'm \n\ngoing to get beat up physically, mentally, or emotionally if I go to \n\nthis.' On the other hand, we wanted it to be an adventure. So we \n\nheld it in a festival marketplace that had public café tables. The \n\ncoffee vendor was one of the sponsors and there were other \n\npatrons all around us. One of the incentives from the sponsoring \n\norganizations was that participants got $5.00 worth of lattes and \n\ndesserts for free." \n\nBalancing safety and adventure, as Sharif discovered, is at the \n\nheart of good Café hosting. It seems that having small café-style \n\ntables (or small circle groups of not more than four or five mem-\n\nbers) is one aspect of achieving this balance. Lloyd Fell, a research \n\nscientist who attended an initial Conversing Café hosted by Alan \n\nStewart to redesign a local cultural center in Australia, noticed a \n\n"tremendous wave of energy . . . right across the room. It was as if \n\nsomething had suddenly been unleashed by the invitation to \n\nspeak freely in the more intimate setting of the café tables." \n\nStefan Wängerstedt, who has introduced World Café conversa-\n\ntions to large banks and hospital systems in Sweden, provides \n\na provocative image of the way in which the World Café creates \n\na safe psychological space for the birth of new ideas. In sharing \n\nwith me his first experience of Café hosting with executives at a \n\nCreate Hospitable Space 75 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nBalancing safety and \n\nadventure . . . is at the \n\nheart of good Café \n\nhosting\n\nmajor Swedish bank, Stefan described the Café as a "sort of preg-\n\nnant place where you can explore all phases of the new life of ideas \n\nin your conversations. The World Café provides a safe place, nour-\n\nishment, and all of the things that an unborn and newborn life \n\nneeds. The tablecloth becomes a collective place where the birth of \n\nthe ideas can come out in the center." Safety, adventure, playfulness, \n\nintimacy, and inclusion all contribute to creating a space that is \n\nalive, inviting, and open to new ways of seeing and connecting. \n\nThe Quality That Has No Name \n\nAs I re-read these reflections from both interdisciplinary \n\nresearchers and from our learning conversations with Café hosts \n\naround the world, I am once again drawn to the ideas of Christo-\n\npher Alexander, the famed architect. In his early work, The Time-\n\nless Way of Building, Alexander describes the deep, life-affirming \n\npatterns embedded in the human species that, when honored and \n\nexpressed in physical form, allow what is natural and whole in us \n\nto emerge. In a beautiful passage, he speaks of the "quality that \n\nhas no name"--the quality embodied in places and spaces that \n\nhe describes as being comfortable, free, whole, and alive. "Places \n\nwhich have this quality, invite this quality to come to life in us. . . . \n\nIt is a self-supporting, self-maintaining, generating quality. It is \n\nthe quality of life. And we must seek it, for our own sakes, in our \n\nsurroundings, simply in order that we can, ourselves, become \n\nalive" (1979, p. 53). \n\nAnd so it is with intentional creation of hospitable space as \n\npart of the Café approach to catalyzing collaborative conversa-\n\ntion, shared learning, and collective insight. Our varied experi-\n\nences with the World Café across cultures affirm Christopher \n\nAlexander's fundamental insight. Perhaps it really is that simple \n\nand obvious after all. We are able to confront difficult questions, \n\nexplore underlying assumptions, and create what we care about \n\nmore readily in environments that evoke warmth, friendliness, \n\nauthenticity, and real conversation than in environments that are \n\nless hospitable to the human spirit. Discovering creative ways to \n\nevoke the quality that has no name is both our greatest challenge \n\nand our greatest opportunity as Café hosts. \n\n76 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThink about times when you participated in a really great conversation. What was it about the setting or the envi-ronment (physical or psychological) that contributed to its success? \n\nConsider a meeting or a conversation you have coming up. Think about the place where it will occur. What are the few small things that you can do to make the space more comfortable and inviting? What can you do personally to make people feel welcome? \n\nWhich of your colleagues do you think might find these ideas relevant? How might you engage them in exploring how to make your meeting and gathering spaces more hospitable to the human spirit? \n\nCreate Hospitable Space 77 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nQuestions for Reflection\n\n78 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nF I V E \n\nBecause questions are intrinsically related to action, they \n\nspark and direct attention, perception, energy, and effort, \n\nand so are at the heart of the evolving forms that our lives \n\nassume. . . . Creativity requires asking genuine questions, \n\nthose to which an answer is not already known. Questions \n\nfunction as open-handed invitations to creativity, calling \n\nforth that which doesn't yet exist. \n\n--Marilee Goldberg, The Art of the Question \n\nPRINCIPLE 3 \n\nExplore Questions That Matter\n\n79 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat i f the gold l ies in \n\ndiscover ing the big quest ions?\n\n80 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nA CAFÉ ON QUESTIONS \n\nAs Told By \n\nMembers of the World Café Community \n\nIn this imaginary Café, I include the perspectives of various World Café hosts who have con-\n\ntributed to our mutual learning about the power and potential of questions that matter--a defin-\n\ning feature of the World Café approach to dialogue. I also include the voices of colleagues who have both hosted Café dialogues and written about the "question of questions." As part of this synthe-sis, I've kept the spirit and essence of individual reflections while taking creative license by includ-ing ideas from other Café hosts who have contributed to our mutual discoveries. Listen in. The members of the conversation are just beginning to introduce themselves as they take their seats at the tables. . . . \n\nJuanita: Welcome to our Café on Questions. Everyone here has been part of other World Café conversations, but let me just share again how we'll be working together. We'll have three rounds of conversation. As your Café host, I'll let you know when each round is coming to a close. One person will stay at the table as a host to wel-come guests who have been a part of other conversations. They'll be bringing seed ideas from their tables into the next round. The important thing is to capture the essence of what's been said in drawings, symbols, and words on your tablecloths. Continue to link and build ideas. Notice patterns and common themes as well as the aha's. Perhaps we could play with the Café question I've put on the flip chart here: \n\nWhat are you learning about the use of questions for engaging collaborative learning and collective intelligence that might be of help to others? \n\nSome of you know each other, but others don't, so it would be great if you could introduce yourselves at your tables before jumping in. \n\n(A brief silence ensues, then the conversation at one of the tables begins. . . . ) \n\nDavid M.: Well, I'll start. I'm David Marsing. I worked as a senior executive with Intel for many years. I'm now developing new commercial as well as community ventures. I first encountered the World Café as part of the original Intellectual Capital Pioneers. \n\nVerna: Hi, I'm Verna Allee. I wasn't at the first Café but was invited to a later session of the Intellectual Capital Pioneers. I had just finished writing a book called The Knowledge \n\nRound 1: Beginning the Conversation \n\nS T O RY\n\nExplore Questions That Matter 81 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nEvolution: Expanding Organizational Intelligence. As you can imagine, the whole subject of questions fascinates me. \n\nToke: I'm Toke Møller. You can probably tell from my accent that I'm not from California. I'm Danish. My wife, Monica, and I have been convening seminars on the art of hosting in Europe, Canada, and the U.S. I'm interested in the World Café from the perspective of col-lective wisdom. \n\nDavid I.: Welcome, Toke. I'm Juanita's partner, David Isaacs. Why don't I just start? I think questions open doors to collective discovery. The word question comes from "quest"--to be on a journey, to search for something important. \n\nToke: For me, a good question opens that search into the realm of new possibilities. For example, I was hosting a Café in a small community in Denmark about the future of their local school. We went around and around to find the right Café question. Sometimes it's harder to find a good question than to actually host the Café! The question we finally came up with was not "How could the school be better?" or "How can we fix the problems in the school?" but "What could a good school also be?" The question had no judgment about what was already happening, and it didn't require immediate action. That framing of the question created more of an open invitation to explore creative options for the future. \n\nDavid I.: People often want to jump too quickly into action. But I've found that even begin-ning to explore the question allows people to deepen their collective understanding of each other's perspectives. People also begin to take responsibility for giving it their best thinking. That is a very important part of co-evolving effective action. \n\nToke: Right! Effective action often comes out of it if you are exploring a question that is really alive for people. The paradoxical thing is that action almost always does come out of a conversation that has life in it, but it doesn't have to be the primary stated goal. \n\nDavid I.: So the question you talked about in the Education Café in Denmark was more like an invitation to bring more of people's creativity to the exploration, rather than a mandate to go out immediately and do something or fix something. \n\nToke: Yes, the question has to catch people where they are, meet them where there is the most interest and relevance, and then use that collective energy to create forward movement. Actionable knowledge flows naturally from that. \n\nDavid M.: What kinds of questions do that? \n\nVerna: I've thought a lot about what kinds of questions have the most life. To me, the most energizing questions are those that engage people's values, hopes, and ideals--ques-tions that relate to something that's larger than themselves, to which they can connect and contribute. People don't have a lot of energy around questions that are about removing pain or fixing problems. \n\nDavid I.: But that comes up, the pain.\n\n82 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nVerna: Well, I don't mean that you can't address the pain. But you can set the context for the question to evoke collective possibility rather than pain. Here's an example. I recently worked with a large organization whose best people were walking out the door to work for a competitor. Most people would pose the question, "How can we keep from losing our best people?" That's an OK question to ask, but it's not an energizing question. It's about how to stop the pain. A better question might be, "How can we retain our best people?" That's a more positive framing, but it's still not a particularly energizing question. You need to find a question that will get people excited, energize them, and matter to them. \n\nDavid M.: So what would be an example in the case you're describing? \n\nVerna: I would work with key people who are going to be part of it to come up with a ques-tion that is really alive for them and holds an element of personal connection as well as col-lective possibility. For example, "What would this workplace be like if it were the kind of place I looked forward to getting up and coming to every morning?" Or, "What have been my best times here in this organization, the times when I've really looked forward to com-ing to work? What contributed to that?"--and then build your next question from there. \n\nDavid I.: Framing problems as questions is an important shift. I did a Futuring Café with the leadership team at a global leather goods company. At the end, when I asked the partic-ipants what had the most value for them, almost all of them said that the single most pow-erful learning for them was in converting a challenge, issue, or problem into a question. \n\nVerna: What changed for them when they did that? \n\nDavid I.: There's always a subtle feeling of disempowerment in a problem. "We've got a problem. . . . Oh, no! Another problem!" There's a weariness and stuckness about it. Sim-ply shifting the focus from problem to evocative inquiry helps people get unstuck and opens doors. \n\nDavid M.: One way I like to look at a good question is that it is like a seed crystal. A good question starts a conversation that begins to take on a more complex, richer form. It grows in amazing and unpredictable ways. The conditions have to be right for this, of course, but the seed itself, the original question, is very important. The crystal won't grow without that seed, or it won't grow as beautifully or fully. \n\nVerna: In addition to revealing new facets of a situation, I've found that a good question can simultaneously focus our minds in much the same way as meditation. A catalytic ques-tion magnetizes an intense quality of individual and collective attention, and we need to respect that. \n\nToke: I like how you give a powerful question such respect. In a Café conversation, the ques-tion seems to be an "attractor" for the collective intelligence and deeper wisdom of the group to reveal itself. It can bring more coherence. It's almost as if an invisible energy field forms around the question. \n\n(There is a pause in the conversation.)\n\nExplore Questions That Matter 83 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nDavid I.: We've touched already on several attributes of a powerful question: it is a question that matters, it attracts and generates energy, it opens up possibilities, it invites deeper explo-ration, and so on. Any other ideas? \n\nToke: Well, I would add that a good question is simple. If the question is too complex, impersonal, or abstract you miss what's at the heart of it. A simple question can invite in a variety of voices, like the question, "What could a good school also be?" Even young children from the school were able to help take responsibility for that question. We could then take it to the next step and ask everyone, "What do you want to say about the future of the school based on these ideas?" The young people had great contributions, like creating their own Ten Commandments outlining the responsibilities of the parents, the students, and the teach-ers--which completely surprised the adults! It all came from that first simple question. \n\nVerna: In my experience, a good question is one that creates a certain tension, a certain dis-sonance that pulls us forward in order to lessen the gap between our current knowledge and our new learning. \n\nDavid I.: And it might not be just one question; it could be a series of related questions that build on each other over the life of the Café. But you have to watch what's evolving so either you or the group can try to find the next question that will take the inquiry for-ward and deeper. \n\n(Juanita raises her hand quietly. Others see her doing this and they begin to raise their hands. The room becomes quiet as this round of conversation comes to a close.) \n\nJuanita: So, let's take it to the next phase. Please move now to a new Café table and con-tinue the conversation. One person should stay as the host to greet the guests who are com-ing from other conversations. Whoever is the host, could you make sure everyone does a quick introduction as you get started? Then show your new group what's on your tablecloth and let your guests know where your conversation has taken you so you can continue to link your thinking. And be sure to keep doodling on the tablecloths. In this second round, begin to listen even more deeply to see what patterns and connections are emerging. \n\nDavid M.: I'll stay as host. \n\n(Three others arrive and everyone shakes hands across the table as they get settled.) \n\nDavid M.: Welcome. I'm David Marsing, the host at this table. I was a line guy at Intel and took World Café conversations into my operational work as a leader. \n\nBarbara: I'm Barbara Waugh from Hewlett-Packard. For me, even though I've never conducted a formal Café learning event, the World Café pattern describes what I've done on my whole journey at HP: seeding conversations \n\nRound 2: Moving Between Tables\n\n84 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nwith good questions, sending people out to cross-pollinate and connect ideas, and letting things grow on their own. It's been quite a ride. \n\nEric: Hi, everyone. I'm Eric Vogt. I coordinate the International Corporate Learning Asso-ciation (InterClass) and was also a part of the early intellectual capital dialogues at Juanita and David's. \n\nSusan: And I'm Susan Skjei. I've developed an executive leadership program for the Marpa Center for Business and Economics at Naropa University that combines reflective practices with leadership skills. \n\nDavid M.: You can see our beautiful drawings and scribbles here on the tablecloth. . . . \n\n(Laughing, he shows the group the drawings, ideas, and scribbles from the first round. The new guests begin to add their ideas.) \n\nEric: (commenting on the drawings and notes) It's amazing! Some of the same things came out at our table. The one example you have on the tablecloth of making the questions more open-ended while still being directly connected to what people care about touched on some-thing we also explored in our group. Can I draw a little diagram here? (He takes a green pen and draws a little diagram with the words "construction," "scope," and "assumptions," on the table-cloth.) Now, this is a little rough, but here goes. \n\nThe construction part has to do with the actual architecture of the question: Is it con-structed as an open question that's more than a yes/no or multiple choice? For example, the question "Should we move the company to Albuquerque or not?" evokes a yes/no response rather than a more open question like "What are all the things we need to weigh in consid-ering if we should move to Albuquerque?" \n\nA scope question might be what you were talking about earlier at this table in relation to problem-solving or fix-it questions. If you ask, "What's the one biggest problem in the school?" the question has a narrower scope than "What could a good school also be?" \n\nThe assumptions aspect of a question is even subtler. Understanding and working more intentionally with the beliefs and assumptions that underlie the question can make a real difference to the outcome of an inquiry. That's as far as we got. Maybe you can help. \n\nBarbara: Eric, I really like that way of thinking about questions. It helps me see my own situation at HP in another light. I think I can add something to the part related to assump-tions. Can I give you a real-life example of a key question at HP and how it evolved? \n\n(Heads nod in encouragement around the table.) \n\nI was helping to mobilize a visioning effort at HP Labs around the question, "How can we be the best industrial research lab in the world?" We started a whole global network of interconnected conversations around that question. As I look back on it, we were hosting a World Café, even though the dialogues weren't happening in the same room. One day Lau-rie, one of our engineers, came into my office and said, "What would really energize me would be to ask the question, 'How can we be the best industrial research lab for the world?'"\n\nExplore Questions That Matter 85 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n(Barbara puts the two alternative HP visioning questions under the part of Eric's diagram that says "assumptions.") \n\nThat one word profoundly shifted the assumptions that underpinned our inquiry--how we could become the best for the world as the deeper context for becoming the best in the world. This shift mobilized a huge amount of collective energy, not only at HP Labs but also throughout the whole company. It was no longer just the lab's question; it became the ques-tion many others at HP began to ask themselves. As I thought about it, being the best in the world was based the assumption of competition. Being the best for the world was based on the assumption of contribution. \n\n(Barbara adds "competition" and "contribution" under her questions on the tablecloth.) \n\nOnce we got the core question, "How can we become the best for the world?" we could increase or decrease the scope depending on the situation. I call that scaling up or scaling down the question. For example, decreasing the scale would be, "What does 'HP for the World' mean for me? What does it mean in my life, in my own work?" Or we could scale it\n\n86 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nup: "What does 'HP for the World' mean for my work group? For my department? For HP as a company? And what might it mean for the world itself for HP to be for the world?" \n\n(Susan scribbles "scale up/scale down" on the tablecloth.) \n\nDavid M.: That's fantastic. Maybe we should be asking the "for the world" question more intentionally in all corporations! \n\nSusan: You know, David Isaacs shared with me that he sometimes finds it works well not even to have prepared a question in advance. He often likes to ask a Café group: "What core question, if explored, could make the most difference to the situation we're considering?" or "What do we not know, that if we did know, could transform this situation for the better?" That way, they can use their own knowledge to generate the questions. \n\nDavid M.: Isn't that interesting? I've been involved in meditation for a number of years. Intentionally engaging people's "not knowing" is actually what is called "beginner's mind" in meditation. In the Eastern tradition, it is considered the start of all wisdom. \n\n(The room is buzzing with energy. People are leaning forward, intent, focused. Juanita quietly puts up her hand again, and as people notice, they begin to raise their hands. The room quiets down.) \n\nJuanita: Now for the third round. Please return to wherever you started--your home table. Spend a few minutes sharing how the conversation has evolved and ask yourselves, "What remains at the edge of our 'not knowing' here? What deeper questions do we still have regarding how to use questions effectively for engaging collaborative learning and collective intelligence?" Get a sense of the two or three questions that, together, you think are really important. Put each question separately on the large hexagonal stickies that are on each table. When this round ends, we'll open up into a "conversation of the whole" to see what emerges in the middle. \n\n(People return to their original tables and begin to share the common themes and insights that have emerged from their multiple conversations. They begin to frame the few deeper questions they collectively feel need to be a part of the continuing explo-ration. Juanita then moves into the middle of the room. The graphic recorder, Gretchen Pisano, is in front of the room, working on a large wall mural that serves as the table-cloth for the whole.) \n\nJuanita: Let's hear from one table. Then, if another has a question that is connected to the first, speak up and we'll see what emerges. If others don't know who you are, please just say your first name and maybe a couple of brief words about yourself. \n\n(People from a number of the tables begin to speak up.) \n\nPaul: I'm Paul Borawski, executive director of the American Society for Quality. At my table we're still ringing with a question that relates to what David Marsing, sitting over there next to Verna, said about questions being the beginning of wisdom. I'm wondering: "How do we shape rich questions that evoke a deep and thoughtful response versus what I would call 'flat' ques-tions--ones that don't have that ring that evokes deep curiosity and creativity?"\n\nExplore Questions That Matter 87 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nMonica: I'm Monica Nissén from Denmark. Toke Møller and I have been focusing our work on the art of hosting over the last few years. Our table had a related question that I think gets to the heart of how to help Café conversations yield their greatest insights: "How can we make the question simple enough so that it really penetrates--gets through into the real heart of the mat-ter? How little is enough?" \n\nCarlos: That's a great question. I'm Carlos Mota and I've been using Cafés in Mexico for sce-narios and strategic planning. Once a friend told me about a time she was being interviewed. The interviewer said, "We're just going to ask you one question: What is the question we should be asking?" With that in mind, I'm wondering: "Is it realistic to think that we as Café hosts can discover and frame questions that really get to the essence of what matters--or can only the people themselves who are closest to it do that job? If it's the people themselves, then what does that mean for who we involve in planning for our Cafés?" \n\nBo: It's beautiful that you say that. I'm Bo Gyllenpalm from Sweden. I teach at the Fielding Graduate Institute, and I think our question is connected to yours. At our table we asked: "What are the ways we can begin to discover what questions are at the heart of things, even before the Café begins?" Perhaps it might mean including a request in the invitation for people to send in their own burning questions related to the Café topic ahead of time. Then you can post these all around the room for people to see as they come into the Café, or begin with a key question you've discovered in advance from the people themselves that seems really powerful and relevant. \n\nKen: I'm Ken Homer. I've helped to create the World Café Web site and am working with the development of the World Café community globally. Can we touch on a different area? \n\n(Heads nod around the room, and he continues.) \n\nAt our table we raised a larger-scale question. We were thinking about Barbara's exam-ple of how a small shift in the question enabled it to travel even more broadly throughout the company. We're wondering: "How do you discover the questions that will really 'travel well,' either throughout an individual Café gathering or, as importantly, beyond any one situation into a large-scale change effort like what happened at HP?" For example, what are the powerful ques-tions that can connect us as a community of Café hosts all around the world? \n\nJuanita: That's a great one to reflect on together. I'm sure we haven't gotten all of the key questions out, even in this whole-group conversation. So, as we go out for the break, I'd love for you to put up all the questions you've written on your hexagonal stickies on the large mural here at the front--on our Wall of Inquiry. Maybe as you come back from break you can take a gallery tour and informally begin to group the questions that seem to relate to each other. After the break, we'll take a few minutes to see what might be the really big ques-tions that we're holding together, which might take our understanding together to a deeper level. Thanks to all for being a part of this!\n\nI \n\nfirst learned about the relevance of paying disciplined attention \n\nto questions that matter from Mike Szymanczyk, the current \n\nchairman and CEO of Philip Morris USA. Mike is a brilliant \n\nsystemic thinker and an innovative architect of large-scale organiza-\n\ntional change. He and other company leaders are creating multiple \n\nopportunities for collaborative dialogue and engagement with both \n\ninternal and external stakeholders as they address the challenges of \n\nrealigning with societal expectations and reinventing the future of \n\ntheir company. Mike and I have worked together for almost two \n\ndecades on strategic change initiatives in several different corpora-\n\ntions. Over time, I began to notice that he consistently came up \n\nwith surprising business and organizational insights. Was Mike just \n\nsmarter than other people? Was it that he had more highly devel-\n\noped intuitive capacities? Or did he simply have some secret access \n\nto the Goddess of Strategy? (My favorite explanation!) \n\nI asked Mike if we could sit down with him to learn how he \n\ndeveloped strategic insight. Mike's reflections during that session \n\n88 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n(Mellow jazz plays in the background. Many in the group continue sharing in small clusters around the room once they have gotten their coffee. Little by little they meander over to the Wall of Inquiry--individually and in groups. By the time the twenty-minute break is over, folks have played around with the hexagons until several clusters emerge that reflect their own collective sense of the patterns of meaning among the questions. Even though they were not asked to do so, people have gotten larger hexagons from Gretchen and put one "big question" over each cluster that captures the core idea of that cluster. As they come back from the break, the group does not sit down, but gathers around the Wall of Inquiry to see what they have created together. \n\nNancy Margulies, one of the early Café innovators, who was rather quiet during earlier Café rounds, takes the lead in helping the group refine and clarify the big questions. After another ten minutes, it seems that folks have finished. They have added several other key questions that came up in the context of bringing this circle of exploration to a resting point. There is a peaceful silence as people take in their collective insights.) \n\n. . . \n\nPerspectives and Observations\n\nExplore Questions That Matter 89 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\ntransformed my thinking about the role and power of questions \n\nin co-evolving the future. His perspectives have greatly influenced \n\nthe way the World Café has come to place disciplined attention \n\non discovering and exploring questions that matter as one of its \n\ndefining features. Mike mused: \n\nFor me, developing strategy is like panning for gold. The gold lies in dis-\n\ncovering the "big questions"--the really strategic questions that can pull \n\npeople's energy and learning forward toward the future. But how can you \n\nfind the gold? First, you have to care about finding it and you have to be \n\ncurious. With that in mind, you head toward the general territory where \n\nyou think the gold may be located, armed with your best tools, your expe-\n\nrience, and your instincts. \n\nYou scan the horizon and contours of the land--the real-life situation you \n\nlive in. In my case that's our business, organizational, and social land-\n\nscape. You begin to notice the details of the terrain all around you, \n\nbecause, for all you know, the gold might be right at your feet. You take \n\nnote of the interesting formations as you travel, knowing that you may be \n\nbreaking new ground or making the path as you go. You begin to turn \n\nover rocks to see what shows up and to see if there are even small flecks \n\nof gold (issues and questions) that might point you to the places where \n\nthe gold nuggets (the big questions) might be found. As you look at your \n\nparticular situation, you scan the horizon looking for trends or other \n\nexternal signals. The purpose is to see where your curiosity and imagina-\n\ntion lead you in order to identify the big questions that your situation \n\nanalysis reveals. \n\nI should emphasize that I mean questions, not problems--questions \n\nstated in a way that ends with a question mark, not with a period or an \n\nexclamation point. Questions like, "How does A relate to C, and what \n\ndeeper question does that suggest?" or "If X were at play here, what ques-\n\ntion would we be asking?" "Given Y, how can we . . . ?" or "What's the \n\nreal question, the fundamental question here?" Framing your issues as \n\nquestions rather than problems is the hardest part, because we're so used \n\nto thinking in terms of problems. But something fundamental changes \n\nwhen people begin to ask questions together. The questions create more of \n\na learning conversation than the normal stale debate about problems.\n\nBased in large measure on Mike's insights about the impor-\n\ntance of discovering big questions, I began to focus on exploring \n\nquestions that matter as a key element in our early work with \n\nstrategic dialogue (Bennett and Brown, 1995). As we deepened \n\nour experience with Café learning, Café hosts around the world \n\nbegan to realize how central the emphasis on catalytic questions \n\nfor collaborative knowledge creation was going to be. \n\nWhy Questions Count \n\nConsider the possibility that everything we know today about our \n\nworld emerged because people were curious. They formulated a \n\nquestion or series of questions about something that sparked \n\ntheir interest or deeply concerned them, which led them to learn \n\nsomething new. Many Nobel laureates located their eureka! \n\nmoment of discovery in the instant when the right question \n\nfinally revealed itself--even if it took them considerable time to \n\ncome up with the final answer. For example, Einstein's theory of \n\nrelativity resulted from a question that he had wondered about as \n\na teenager: "What would the universe look like if I \n\nwere riding on a beam of light?" When asked what \n\naccounted for his success, another Nobel prize win-\n\nner, physicist Arno Penzia, replied: "I went for the \n\njugular question" (Vogt and others, 2003, p. 1). \n\nGenuine questions--ones for which we don't \n\nalready have answers--are open invitations to inno-\n\nvation, calling forth ideas and insights that don't yet \n\nexist. For every step into the future, someone or some \n\ngroup had to wonder what the possibilities might be \n\nfor changing or improving the current situation--if \n\nonly to ask themselves: "What would happen if we thought about this \n\ndifferently? What questions are we not asking, that if we did ask, might \n\nmake our situation better?" \n\nThe importance of carefully crafted questions for break-\n\nthrough thinking and effective action is slowly starting to reach \n\ngreater public awareness (Adams, 2004; Allee, 1997; Goldberg, \n\n1997; Leeds, 2000; Strachan, 2001). For example, in addressing \n\n90 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nGenuine questions-- \n\nones for which we don't \n\nalready have answers-- \n\nare open invitations \n\nto innovation\n\nExplore Questions That Matter 91 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nthe results of more than a decade of research and practice in the \n\narea of Appreciative Inquiry, David Cooperrider and Diana Whit-\n\nney state unequivocally that "the most important insight we have \n\nlearned with AI to date is that human systems grow toward what \n\nthey persistently ask questions about" (2000, p. 70). Laura \n\nChasin, a founder of the Public Conversations Project, which \n\nfosters constructive conversations around divisive public issues, \n\nreminds us that questions have an unsung power. They carry the \n\ncapacity to intensify conflict--"How can we get even?"--or \n\ndeepen mutual understanding--"How did we each come to the \n\nperspective we hold?" Laura encourages us, particularly in these \n\nuncertain times, to help co-evolve the future by having "conversa-\n\ntions focused on the right questions--the most constructive and \n\ncatalytic questions we can ask" (2001, pp. 1-3). If asking good \n\nquestions is so critical, how can we, as conversation hosts, become \n\nmore skillful in shaping questions that matter? \n\nThe Art and Architecture of Powerful Questions \n\nAlthough we've met only once, Fran Peavey, a pioneer in the use of \n\nstrategic questioning for social change, has greatly influenced my \n\nthinking about how to construct powerful questions. Fran observes \n\nthat "questions can be like a lever you use to pry open the stuck \n\nlid on a paint can. . . . If we have just a short lever, we can only \n\njust crack open the lid on the can. But if we have a longer lever, or \n\na more dynamic question, we can open that can up much wider \n\nand really stir things up" (1994, p. 91). For example, in a commu-\n\nnity development effort to clean up the Ganges River in India, a \n\n"short-lever" question--one that will elicit a narrow yes/no \n\nresponse--might be, "Have you thought about cleaning up the \n\npollution in the river?" "Longer-lever" questions might include \n\nthese: "What do you see when you look at the river? How do you \n\nfeel about the condition of the river? How do you explain the situ-\n\nation with the river to your children?" (1994, p. 105). These more \n\nopen questions encourage a more thoughtful response that opens \n\nthe door to further exploration and positive change.\n\nFran could have been sitting in any of our own learning con-\n\nversations about the World Café when she said, "One of the basic \n\nassumptions of the strategic questioning process is that knowl-\n\nedge resides and is alive in all people. . . . The point here is to ask \n\nquestions in such a way that it lets the ideas and energy come \n\nfrom the individual or the system itself" (1994, p. 100). \n\nThe art of framing strategic questions that call forth our \n\ninnate wisdom has important implications for focusing intention, \n\nattention, and energy, as well as for increasing our collective abil-\n\nity to generate insights that help shape the future. \n\nBy understanding what makes a powerful question, you can \n\nexperiment with increasing the power of your questions and see-\n\ning what impact they have on the conversations you host. For \n\nexample, in advance of an important conversation, spend a few \n\nminutes with a colleague and write down several questions that \n\nare relevant to the topic. Rate them in terms of their power. See if \n\nyou can spot why certain questions are more compelling than \n\nothers. Experiment with changing the construction and scope of \n\nthe question, as Eric Vogt (Vogt, 1994; Vogt and others, 2003) \n\nsuggested in the opening story for this chapter and as Barbara \n\nWaugh (2001) demonstrated in her HP for the World project. \n\nExamine the assumptions that may be embedded in your ques-\n\ntions and check to see if they will help or hinder your explo-\n\nration. Notice the impact of the questions you hear, read, or \n\nintroduce into your own conversations. Just a few practice ses-\n\nsions will greatly enhance your ability to engage in conversational \n\ninquiry stimulated by dynamic questions. \n\nLooking at questions that have worked for others is a useful way \n\nto stimulate your own creativity in framing powerful questions. In \n\ntheir Encyclopedia of Positive Questions (2002), Diana Whitney and \n\nDavid Cooperrider share hundreds of questions that have been used \n\nin their work with Appreciative Inquiry to elicit innovative ideas and \n\nnew possibilities in both organizations and communities. In chapter \n\n10, we've included a number of generic questions that World Café \n\nhosts have found helpful in a wide variety of Café and other dialogue \n\nsettings to focus collective attention, connect ideas, and create \n\n92 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nExplore Questions That Matter 93 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nQuestions Travel Through Networks \n\nforward movement. In organizations as well as communities, ques-\n\ntions that matter can enliven a generative and focused field of inquiry \n\ncharacterized by what we call "coherence without control," as grow-\n\ning networks of people share their own responses to questions at the \n\nheart of their common inquiry. Exploring your organization's or \n\ncommunity's big questions and seeding wider fields of conversation \n\nwith strategic questions that attract energy and "travel well" lie at the \n\nheart of cultivating the knowledge we need to thrive today, as well as \n\nthe wisdom we need to ensure a sustainable future. \n\nConsider an upcoming conversation you are hosting and ask \n\nyourself these questions: \n\nWhat question, if explored thoroughly, could provide the breakthrough possibilities we are seeking? \n\nIs the question relevant to the real life or real work of the people who will be exploring it? \n\nIs this a genuine question--a question to which I/we really don't know the answer? \n\nWhat work do I want this question to do? That is, what kind of conversation, meanings, and feelings do I imagine this question will evoke in those who will be exploring it? \n\nWhat assumptions or beliefs are embedded in the way this question is constructed? \n\nIs this question likely to generate hope, imagination, engage-ment, new thinking, and creative action, or is it likely to increase a focus on past problems and obstacles? \n\nDoes this question leave room for new and different ques-tions to be raised as the initial question is explored? \n\nQuestions adapted from Sallyann Roth, Public Conversations Project, copyright © 1998. \n\nQuestions for Reflection\n\n94 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nS I X \n\nThe idea of contribution is especially instructive because \n\nit lies in the area that unites the "I" and the "we." We \n\ncontribute because we are part of something larger than \n\nour own lives and efforts, but the form of our contribution \n\nis based on our uniqueness and our individuality. \n\n--Carol Ochs, Women and Spirituality \n\nPRINCIPLE 4 \n\nEncourage Everyone's Contribution\n\n95 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat i f your contr ibut ion \n\ni s a key ingredient?\n\nEVERY VOICE COUNTS: FOSTERING A CULTURE OF CONTRIBUTION: THE FINANCIAL PLANNING ASSOCIATION \n\nAs Told By \n\nJanet McCallen, chief executive officer; Elizabeth Jetton, president, board of \n\ndirectors, 2004; Kim Porto, director, Career and Community Development; Sean \n\nWalters, group director, Community and Knowledge Management \n\nAs part of our effort to create a greater sense of community \n\namong our members, the FPA board decided to hold our \n\nfirst World Café--which we called a Knowledge Café--at our annual conference beginning September 12, 2001, and to make postconference Café follow-up available online. When news of the attacks on New York and Washington came, we quickly decided to postpone the conference, along with our planned Knowledge Café. But we took one additional step that proved pivotal: we invited the chapters into a community conversation about what was happening as a result of the attacks. \n\nWe set up a conference call at 4:30 P.M. Pacific time on Sep-tember 11. Given the chaos of that day and the short notice for the call, we thought perhaps a dozen folks or so would participate. Imagine our surprise when 125 local chapter officers called in! They each wanted to reach out to help others as best they could. We immediately launched the online forum. Within a day, people who had never participated in an online dialogue were actively contributing to the conversation. A web of heartfelt relationships \n\nand authentic dialogue began to evolve that laid the foundation for building our FPA com-munity in a way we could never have anticipated. \n\nOver the following months, we began to engage our membership in face-to-face Café conversations focusing on their needs and the future of both the association and the finan-cial planning profession. In the early Cafés, we learned a lot about how to ensure that the association's past culture did not derail the process of mutual contribution. For example, after several rounds of great conversation and idea generation at individual Café tables, we would bring the full group back together for synthesis and someone would invariably say to the association leaders, "So, what are you going to do about this for us?" We have learned to frame the Café in the beginning in such a way that the members understand that they them-selves play a significant role in both raising issues and contributing to solutions. There are \n\nS T O RY \n\n96 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThe primary aim of the \n\nFinancial Planning Associa-\n\ntion (FPA) is to be the com-\n\nmunity that fosters the value \n\nof financial planning and \n\nadvances the financial plan-\n\nning profession. This jointly \n\ntold story, focusing on how \n\nCafé conversations evoke \n\nmutual contribution, illumi-\n\nnates how the FPA has used \n\nCafé conversations as a core \n\nprocess to engage a growing \n\nnumber of its twenty-eight \n\nthousand members in evolv-\n\ning its community and mak-\n\ning a difference to its future.\n\nEncourage Everyone's Contribution 97 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nways in which the association leadership can exert helpful influence, but we are less and less seen as the only ones responsible for "fixing" things. \n\nWe've now hosted dozens of Cafés at events ranging from our large annual convention of 4,000 participants to smaller gatherings for specific constituencies, such as our retreat for 275 advanced financial planners and our broker-dealer conference with over 500 financial product and service providers, as well as with our own board and staff. We've discovered from both our successes and our mistakes how important it is to clarify the purpose of each Café and spend adequate time designing the process to fit the desired outcomes. Why is this group gathering? What do they want the attendees to experience? Is it a homogeneous group who will all be exploring the same question, or are there multiple stakeholders who need to explore a common theme but with different questions? These are key questions to address in order to ensure that the Café process is successful and that each member's contribution can be put to good use. There are so many little details to attend to in order for the Café process to serve well. There are also lots of creative ways to innovate through using the basic Café principles. This is sometimes a surprise for new hosts. The process itself is very simple, but there's an art to it as well. \n\nWe've learned that you also need to be open to changing things if the Café isn't going as planned. A lot of our most interesting Café innovations have resulted from in-the-moment conversations beginning with, "Hmmm, that didn't work as we planned. What can we do to get back to the purpose we're here for?" One of the hard questions is asking ourselves, "Is this not working, or is it just uncomfortable?" Sometimes the uncomfortable is necessary to break through to new thinking and new knowledge. There are times when we come up against a personal fear or perceived limit and one of us will say, well, let's try this or try that. There are no hard-and-fast rules. \n\nOne of the most interesting things to observe as we host our FPA Knowledge Cafés is the way our members have responded to the Café format and our invitation to be active contributors. Newcomers sometimes arrive with hesitancy and skepticism. Yet in most Café gatherings, people thoroughly enjoy this way of working, even when they realize that, contrary to their initial expectations, there is no outside "expert." Instead, they are being asked to actively contribute their own expertise and knowledge for the betterment of the FPA community. \n\nThe World Café approach has had big payoffs in both networking and mutual contri-bution. For example, a Knowledge Café in Denver with seventy-five local FPA members focused on the future of the members' business by asking: "What do you need to take your business to the next level?" In the initial Café round, the members identified where they were in their careers. Over the course of the four Café rounds, as people moved between tables, the folks closer to retirement met newer planners looking for mentors and even possible can-didates to whom they could pass on their businesses. At the end of the Café, the newer plan-ners stood up and waved to the ones in the later stages of their careers and vice versa. Thanks\n\n98 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nto the World Café, they could now literally "see" each other and share the ways they might contribute to each other's success at each stage of their careers. \n\nThrough Café dialogues such as this one, we are putting more responsibility into the hands of our members. In the Café approach, they are responsible for offering the questions and ideas they feel are important to the future. They are the ones who create their own net-work of personal relationships in a Café. As a result, we are beginning to see a much higher level of member contribution and ownership. In fact, at a recent leadership conference with 250 chapter leaders, we provided participants with Café hosting materials and offered coach-ing support to help them begin to convene Café conversations themselves to foster active member contributions to the FPA community at the local level. \n\nThis overall process of engagement reflects the cultural shift we are engaged in at FPA. Our Café conversations have evolved from the members and staff simply giving input to the board into a much more collaborative focus on how everyone can actively contribute to the future of the organization and the profession. Board, staff, and membership are now more closely aligned around our best hopes for the future. \n\nWe think our next big frontier is deepening the notion of citizenship among our mem-bers. By definition, a citizen is a co-creator and a contributor--not just a passenger along for the ride. To be honest, we are not there yet. It's challenging for us as leaders to ask our con-stituencies to engage in truly meaningful conversation. Are we really willing to listen to them? How will they know they've been heard? Are we ready to share leadership and honor their contributions once we've encouraged them? Will our governance and decision-making approaches shift? These questions--and more--inevitably arise in this type of large-scale cul-tural evolution. The answers are not always easy or clear, but we're becoming more and more willing to engage with these questions as we see the fruits that this growing and evolving process is bringing--to us personally as leaders, and to our members. \n\n. . .\n\nD \n\nuring my years with Cesar Chavez and the farmworkers' \n\nmovement, I worked from the Pink House, a Spartan \n\nbungalow in a run-down, working-class neighborhood \n\nalongside Route 99 in Delano, California. The Pink House was \n\nalways buzzing with activity. People streamed in and out--young \n\nand old, Mexicans, Filipinos, African Americans, and Anglos. \n\nThere were farmworkers arriving straight from the fields. There \n\nwere students, clergy, and well-dressed urban types. Everyone \n\ncame offering what they could to help. A key emphasis in the \n\nmovement was on asking, "What small thing can each of us \n\ncontribute to the larger effort?" \n\nThat was a key word--contribute. There were ways for everyone \n\nto give, to volunteer, to be of service. I remember Cesar Chavez \n\nsaying to me, "If you go to someone's home and they offer you \n\ntheir food, they're giving something of themselves. Once people \n\ngive, then their heart is involved. Giving--making your contribu-\n\ntion--is what brings community alive." \n\nYears later, I was working on a corporate \n\nproject where we were experimenting with \n\nwhat might be possible if we were to look at \n\nthe organization as a community. As we \n\nshifted our thinking toward the idea of build-\n\ning community, we found that honoring and \n\nencouraging each person's unique contribution \n\nseemed more compelling than focusing on \n\neither participation or empowerment-- \n\nconcepts still dear to the hearts of many \n\norganizational change specialists. \n\nThe distinction is subtle but important. Contribution has a \n\ndifferent tone and feeling than individual participation. Impor-\n\ntant as it is, the focus on individual participation can lead to an \n\noveremphasis on the I: I'm voicing my opinions. I'm speaking up. \n\nI'm participating. In contrast, focusing on contribution creates a \n\nrelationship between the I and the we. Employees in the corporate \n\nEncourage Everyone's Contribution 99 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nPerspectives & Observations \n\nWe found that honoring and \n\nencouraging each person's \n\nunique contribution seemed \n\nmore compelling than focus-\n\ning on either participation \n\nor empowerment\n\ncommunity began to ask themselves, "What is my unique contri-\n\nbution to our larger mission as a company?" \n\nThe Role of Contribution in Café Settings \n\nThere was something about our early Café conversations that had \n\nthe same vitality and active sense of connection to the larger \n\nwhole that I had experienced in the farmworkers' movement and \n\nin our efforts to build the corporation as a community. A conver-\n\nsation with Patric Carlson helped shed light on what that special \n\nquality might be. Patric is a former student with the \n\nKaos Pilots, an innovative educational program in \n\nDenmark that has used Café learning extensively. \n\n"The orientation of the Café is toward contribution," \n\nhe explained. "It starts with somebody giving some-\n\nthing. The purpose of the Café is not to criticize, but \n\nto contribute. You can't blame anybody for giving. In \n\nthe Café you don't have to perform, only \n\ncontribute. And when you contribute, the knowledge grows." \n\nIn that moment, sitting with Patric at our kitchen table I \n\nrealized, "Aha, contribution! That's what's happening when a Café \n\nconversation is really cooking." I began to think of Café conver-\n\nsations as being similar to potluck dinners. Contributing your \n\nunique dish is what makes a potluck so interesting, fun, and \n\nnourishing. It's always a surprise. If you simply come to partake \n\nof the potluck meal without bringing your unique contribution \n\nto the party, how can the party happen? In a Café conversation, \n\neach member bringing his or her personal contribution to the \n\ncollective potluck of ideas and insights enriches the intelligence \n\nof the larger whole. \n\nThe philosopher John Dewey, in a poignant reflection more \n\nthan fifty years ago, pointed to the same belief in the power and \n\npotential of collaborative contribution that we now intentionally \n\nfoster in Café conversations. In a 1937 speech, "Democracy as a \n\nWay of Life," Dewey commented, "While what we call intelligence \n\ncan be distributed in unequal amounts, it is the democratic faith \n\nthat it is sufficiently general so that each individual has some-\n\n100 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThe purpose of the \n\nCafé is not to criticize, \n\nbut to contribute\n\nthing to contribute, whose value can be assessed only as it enters \n\ninto the final, pooled intelligence constituted by the contribu-\n\ntions of all" (Singer and Ammerman 1960, p. 276). \n\nAs Café hosts, we've become increasingly intentional about \n\nencouraging everyone's contribution as a core design and operat-\n\ning principle--whether that be the contribution of ideas and \n\ninsights or of concrete support related to critical operations. For \n\nexample, World Café etiquette, often shared at the beginning of a \n\nCafé dialogue (see chapter 10), focuses on inviting everyone's \n\ncontribution rather than simply assuring each person a voice or \n\nasking that everyone participate. \n\nFrances Baldwin, who has hosted Cafés among executive \n\nwomen, comments on the use of the language of contribution in \n\nCafé dialogues. "There's something about the word contribution \n\nthat's proactive," she says.
