"When you're offered the opportunity \n\nto actively contribute, it suggests having more responsibility and \n\nopportunity to make a difference than when you are simply asked \n\nto participate." A Café member in a dialogue hosted by Karen \n\nSpeerstra at Christ Church, a small parish in Vermont, affirms \n\nFrances's insight. "At first, I admit I felt stuck," she said, "but then \n\nI began realizing I was one of four people responsible for how \n\ninteresting this was going to be." \n\nAlong with Café etiquette, we've introduced other intentional \n\nstructures that encourage everyone's contribution. Bronwyn \n\nHorvath of the Saudi Aramco oil company, who has hosted Café \n\nconversations with multicultural groups as large as seven hun-\n\ndred, emphasizes both the intimate size of the Café groups and \n\nthe intentional use of small, café-style tables as supporting \n\nstructures for mutual contribution. "Even your most introverted \n\nperson usually feels comfortable speaking and contributing at \n\nthose little tables of four," she observes. In addition, Café hosts \n\noften introduce a talking piece--a stone or other object--which \n\nslows down the conversation and provides an intentional space for \n\neach person to make his or her unique contribution to what is \n\nemerging in the center of the table. (See description in chapter 10.) \n\nEncourage Everyone's Contribution 101 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .\n\nCafé conversations also provide the opportunity for members \n\nwho are more reflective, or who learn visually, to contribute \n\nthrough attentive listening, drawing on the tablecloths, or making \n\na verbal offering later in the conversation. Laura Peck, reflecting \n\non her use of the World Café to help redesign a California school \n\nprogram, observes, "There are people who listen, there are people \n\nwho see patterns, and there are people who think in images. The \n\nCafé honors and includes the diversity of how people process \n\ninformation and synthesize it. The way it's structured creates a \n\nfield where you don't have to be the quickest talker in order to \n\ncontribute. You can draw connections to other people's pictures, \n\nor you can create your own pictures and images. There's also a \n\nchange in the dynamics of how you contribute with different \n\npeople as you move between tables. . . . So because of the way \n\nthe Café operates there are lots of different strengths that people \n\nget to bring." \n\nIn reflecting on his first Café experience, hosted by Meg \n\nWheatley at the Institute of Noetic Sciences, Hans Kuendig was \n\nsurprised to discover that "you're encouraged to contribute, but \n\nyou're not pushed to contribute in a verbal way. Café conversa-\n\ntions are respectful of the fact that any one of us might just be in \n\nthe listener/observer mode. That's very exciting to me because \n\nsomeone who is sitting back and observing often helps others see \n\nthe deeper patterns. Maybe that person will offer only one or two \n\nsentences, but that could be a very powerful contribution--they \n\ncan see the forest for the trees." The World Café principle of \n\nencouraging everyone's contribution and the variety of media \n\nthrough which this is encouraged brings a broader range of learn-\n\ning styles to the table. When people can offer their diverse gifts in \n\na welcoming and supportive environment around questions they \n\ncare about, actionable knowledge is a natural outcome. \n\nSusan Skjei, in discussing a Café she hosted at Naropa Uni-\n\nversity for the development of Naropa's Marpa Center for Busi-\n\nness and Economics, shares that "when people feel they can \n\ncontribute in a way that creates new knowledge, that's when they \n\nget excited. They can feel that creative energy building. Then they \n\n102 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\ngo, 'Oh great, I have resources to bring to this because it's my \n\nbaby too. Suddenly I can see that this thing we're creating \n\ntogether has a life and I want it to grow!'" \n\nIn addition to its capacity for fostering knowledge creation, \n\nan emphasis on contribution also fosters a sense of community. \n\nAs people begin to contribute together, create \n\ntogether, and learn together, a feeling of connec-\n\ntion--to each other and to a larger whole--begins \n\nto emerge. Sherry Immediato, managing director of \n\nthe Society for Organizational Learning (SoL), \n\npoints to this relationship between contribution, \n\nco-creativity, and community building. In Cafés \n\nhosted at SoL's annual meetings, she's noticed that \n\n"through their mutual contributions during the Café conversa-\n\ntions, our members live the experience of truly co-creating \n\ntogether as a whole community." \n\nWhere inclusion and community are already an integral part \n\nof the cultural fabric, the World Café taps into that spirit of mutual \n\ncontribution. Alexander Schieffer, who, with Barbara Nussbaum, \n\nhosted a Leadership Café at a university in South Africa, was sur-\n\nprised to see how readily African students adopted the World Café \n\nprocess. He points out that "because of how closely African princi-\n\nples of group participation and inclusion in decision making are \n\nlinked to the World Café approach, the students did not regard the \n\nWorld Café as 'one of those Western tools.'" \n\nThe Culture of Connection \n\nA fascinating article by sociologist Philip Slater in the Utne Reader, \n\n"Connected We Stand," offers some insight into the larger social \n\nimplications of the World Café's focus on contribution (2003). \n\nSlater identifies two cultures that are now becoming more visible \n\nthroughout the world: the culture of division and the culture of con-\n\nnection. These two cultures can be found in national, ethnic, and \n\nother traditional demarcations. They are part of both the tradi-\n\ntional left and right. \n\nEncourage Everyone's Contribution 103 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nAn emphasis on \n\ncontribution also fosters \n\na sense of community\n\nHe points out that the culture of division, wherever it is \n\nfound, is based on creating and maintaining clear boundaries \n\nbetween people and ideas. The perception of difference and other-\n\nness infuses the divider culture, whatever its persuasion. In the \n\ndivider culture, it is clear who is "we" and who is "them." The \n\nconnector culture, in contrast, is characterized by a focus on linking \n\npeople, perspectives, and worldviews. People in this culture focus \n\non discovering the common ground and collective wisdom that can \n\nbe accessed by moving beyond the political, social, economic, and \n\norganizational categories that so often narrow our field of vision \n\nand action. They thrive on diversity. He adds that the rise of the \n\nconnector culture is based in part on what we are learning about \n\nthe connectedness of all life from research in the new sciences and \n\nliving systems (see Capra, 1996; Waldrop, 1992; Wheatley, 1992; \n\nZohar and Marshall, 1994). Slater points out that the question of \n\nwhether we choose to live our lives as part of the culture of division \n\nor the culture of connection "is the defining social issue today and \n\nprobably for decades to come" (2003, p. 63). \n\nWe believe that enlivening the culture of connection is one of \n\nthe unique contributions of the World Café to our common \n\nfuture. Café conversations inten-\n\ntionally encourage members to \n\noffer their diverse contributions \n\nwhile simultaneously increasing \n\nthe density of connections among \n\npeople and ideas as the conversa-\n\ntions iterate and self-amplify \n\nbetween rounds. By intentionally \n\nengaging the network dynamics of \n\nliving systems with a focus on \n\nquestions that matter, Café dia-\n\nlogues enrich the web of personal \n\nrelationships and foster the experi-\n\nence of community across tradi-\n\ntional boundaries. As the sense of \n\n104 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nEnlivening the Culture of Connection\n\nconnectedness and community grows, committed action for the \n\ncommon good is often a natural outcome. \n\nYvon Bastien, of Sanofi-Synthelabo, Canada, has used the \n\nCafé process extensively in this pharmaceutical company. He sees \n\nthe World Café as "an act of creation of a community--both \n\namong our employees themselves and in relation to the larger \n\nsociety. People realized that we don't work for ourselves alone, \n\nbut also for our patients and our communities. Actively \n\nconnecting with the larger society was an outcome of our Café \n\nconversations." \n\nAs more people have the opportunity to experience the \n\nmutual contribution, connectedness, community, and commit-\n\nment that the World Café fosters, it is my deepest hope that many \n\nmore of us--whatever our national identities or political persua-\n\nsions--will value and embrace the culture of connection as one \n\npath to a life-affirming future for us all. \n\nEncourage Everyone's Contribution 105 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nQuestions for Reflection \n\nThink of a gathering you are planning and then ask yourself \n\nthese questions: \n\nHow many interesting and practical approaches can you imagine that would invite and enable each person to contribute his or her best thinking? \n\nHow might you help people evolve from thinking of them-selves simply as meeting participants to thinking of them-selves as active contributors to something larger than themselves? \n\nHow might you personally encourage everyone's contribution in the next conversational gathering that you are plan-ning to attend or host?\n\n106 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nS E V E N \n\nWhenever knowledge connects with knowledge, new \n\ncombinations spontaneously take place. Ideas spark ideas, \n\nwhich synthesize with each other until more knowledge \n\nresults. It is completely natural. . . . Sharing knowledge \n\nmeans bringing people into the conversation. \n\n--Verna Allee, The Knowledge Evolution \n\nPRINCIPLE 5 \n\nCross-Pollinate and Connect Diverse Perspectives\n\n107 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat i f inte l l igence emerges \n\nas the sys tem connects to i t se l f in \n\ndiverse and creat ive ways?\n\n108 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nTHE PEACE CAFÉ: UNIVERSITY OF VICTORIA LAW SCHOOL \n\nAs Told By \n\nClaudia Chender \n\nMy first day of law school was September 8, 2001. September 11 \n\nfell during our orientation week. I was profoundly disap-\n\npointed that although we're an innovative law school with a very diverse student population, there were few places to gather as a community to talk about what had happened. We heard whis-pers about it in the hallways, and a few of us who were more invested in the news made some hasty phone calls to learn what was going on. I remember standing outside of the campus pub, staring through the glass door at the TV screen, waiting for the pub to open. There was no opportunity for our law school com-munity to get together and talk about it. No one who said, "If you feel like you want to go and find out what's going on, or take a moment, go ahead." Other gatherings and organizing were happening on campus, but at the law school, that day, nothing. \n\nThis need for community led a group of us to start a Human Rights Collective on campus. One of our key missions was to provide an alternative setting for learning conversations that would encompass as much diversity as possible, both of view-points and participants. I helped take on the responsibility for \n\ndesigning our monthly public forums. In the summer of 2002, I attended a strategic conversation seminar at the Shambhala \n\nInstitute for Authentic Leadership, where I experienced the World Café firsthand. When I returned to school, my classmates and I decided to give the Café format a shot in our pub-lic forums. \n\nWe hosted our first World Café to mark the first anniversary of September 11. People really appreciated Café conversations as a way to find their voice on this type of critical con-temporary issue. We hosted several other Cafés that school year, and they were all very suc-cessful, but the one I really want to tell you about happened during the run-up to the war in Iraq in the spring of 2003. \n\nWe have an online forum at school that had been dormant, but suddenly in the period before the war began people started posting their perspectives about what was happening. Within ten days there were more than two hundred posts. Very quickly, many of the posts began to degenerate into insults, and an intense positionality became apparent. In the mid-dle of all this, I posted an offer to host a Café dialogue so that we could diffuse the situation \n\nIn the period leading up to \n\nthe war in Iraq, law school \n\nstudents from the University \n\nof Victoria, British Columbia, \n\nCanada, became involved in \n\nan online discussion forum \n\ndebating the merits of the \n\nwar. The postings quickly \n\nbecame sharp and divisive, \n\nbut students had no other \n\nforum for discussing these \n\nissues in the school. Claudia \n\nChender, law student, tells \n\nthe story of how a group of \n\nstudents used a Café dia-\n\nlogue to connect diverse \n\nperspectives across tradi-\n\ntional boundaries. \n\nS T O RY\n\nCross-Pollinate and Connect Diverse Perspectives 109 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nand meet each other face-to-face. Although the idea initially met with hesitation, it eventu-ally took hold. With the backing of the Human Rights Collective and the help of the Student Union president, among many others, we hosted our largest Café ever. \n\nIt was scheduled for a Friday afternoon, and about one-quarter of the law school student body showed up--an amazing response! We had participants from twenty to sixty years of age, including a military officer, an international human rights activist, Middle Eastern stu-dents (some with family in Iraq), retired persons, and students with family members who were foreign correspondents in the region. In addition to a diverse cross section of the stu-dent body, a number of professors were in attendance, which was very unusual for a student gathering. Many of the people were clearly skeptical about the process. And most came with very entrenched opinions. \n\nI was really nervous about what would happen, and the hosting team we brought together was crucial in providing mutual support. Leading up to the Café, we invited anyone who was interested to come together and help design the event, including the question we would pose and the agreements we would honor. In our other Cafés we'd used reading mate-rial to stimulate the participants' thinking in advance. This time, we decided to issue a gen-eral invitation to everyone who was coming to let us know if they felt inspired to share a very brief personal story about how the current specter of war was affecting them. Two professors (an immigration scholar and a constitutional expert), the human rights activist, and the mil-itary officer volunteered. \n\nThe military officer had gotten a lot of hostile responses to his postings in the online forum. He was actually the one who inspired the context we later set for the Café. He said in one of his online postings, "Look, regardless of all of our opinions on this, what we can agree on is that ultimately what we want is peace. We just have different views on how to get there." When I heard that I thought, "Aha! If we come at it from that point of view, it's not like 'You're a warmonger who just wants to wreck everything whereas I love peace and therefore I have some kind of moral high ground.'" It broke down the moral rightness and wrongness people had around their views, and opened up the space for dialogue. \n\nWhen people arrived, we set the tone. First, I asked people to remember a time when they'd had a really good conversation--a conversation that made them think, or made them curious, or caused a good laugh or cry. I asked them to share it with a neighbor if they liked-- and then to share with the whole table what had helped that good conversation happen. Then I said that I hoped that in this Café dialogue we could move beyond the normal adver-sarial law school way of talking. As the hosting team we asked the group if they would be willing to honor a few simple agreements--confidentiality, making sure there was space for each person to speak, and trying to listen with respect. Students who had participated in our earlier Cafés took responsibility for sharing these agreements and modeling them at their tables. I also described basic World Café etiquette. Doing these things all helped set different expectations for the conversation.\n\n110 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nBefore we began, someone spoke up and suggested that we take a moment of silence, because people's lives were at stake in this whole conflict. When we did that a real shift occurred in the whole room. The silence, and the small bell that we used to mark the tran-sitions during the Café, lent the event a natural gentleness and pacing, giving people a chance to take a breath and listen more deeply. \n\nThe military officer was the first of the four volunteers to share his story. He shared how hurt he'd been when people attacked him in the online forum, and told of a friend who'd been a U.N. peacekeeper and seen people dying and not been able to stop it. He had faith that if the military, like the U.N. peacekeepers, were used properly in the service of stopping bloodshed and producing peace, there could be real hope. He had tears in his eyes. All the other presentations were like that--very personal and real. We just naturally took a moment of silence after each person spoke to really take in what they were saying, whether or not we agreed with them--simply to appreciate their contribution. By the end, many people in the room were also in tears. A big discovery for me was how powerful the sharing of personal stories can be for deepening the conversation. \n\nWe began the first of the three Café rounds by continuing the personal storytelling. We asked people to share their responses to the question, "How have you been personally affected by this war?" Afterward everyone had a chance to continue by reflecting on these questions: "Where does peace come into your questions around this conflict? What does peace look like? Where is the law in the process of peace?" In the second round we focused on this: "What societal and individual ideals are reflected in your roads to or visions of peace?" In the third round we brought it back home by asking, "What can you do in your life to work toward peace and to create further dialogue and understanding?" \n\nThere is something in the World Café process that makes being self-righteous and posi-tional really false. Perhaps it's that there's no space for high horses and grandstanding when you're sitting talking together with four people at a small table with flowers and candles. Could that be it? Or maybe it's the moving between tables that keeps you from getting stuck. There's a chink in the armor, and then as you move to another table and connect with new people and ideas you realize that it's happening with everyone else, too. It's almost like a col-lective sigh of relief happens in that second round of conversation, when people realize that everyone in that room is actually having a genuine conversation, too. \n\nThere were, of course, some challenges. At some tables people would start to raise their voices or not allow others to get a word in edgewise. . . . I mean, it wasn't perfect. But one friend told me she felt that overall there was a genuine softness that came into the room--a feminine quality to the gathering that she really appreciated, especially in the context of a conversation about war. \n\nWhen we did the large-group sharing at the end, I'd hoped to hear many great insights about the nature of peace. Instead, most of the conversation focused on acknowledging how disrespectful people had been toward each other in the Web forum and how good it was to\n\nCross-Pollinate and Connect Diverse Perspectives 111 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nhave an opportunity to come together in this new way. Our student body president, Justin, even wrote me a letter and said we should use Café conversations for all of our major student issues. \n\nI was genuinely inspired by what came out of that Café. It shifted my ideas about how you take action, particularly in polarized situations. I think that shifting the way you are with others is a powerful way of taking action. In the end, the legacy of the Café was that the diverse people in that conversation had a much deeper understanding of and respect for why others felt the way they did. That day, as we talked together in the World Café, people began to come alive to how we want to communicate and live in community together, especially when we have contact over time as we do in the law school. It felt to me like we were really modeling the kind of world I want to live in. That's action, isn't it? \n\n. . . \n\nPerspectives & Observations \n\nF \n\nrom our very earliest café experiences, World Café hosts \n\nhave commented on the excitement and energy that spirals \n\nupward as people and ideas move from one round of Café \n\nconversation to the next, developing new connections and rela-\n\ntionships. At times it feels as if the evolving rounds of conversa-\n\ntion are sparking new synapses in the larger mind of the group as \n\na whole. In addition, as Claudia's story illustrates, when members \n\nare charged with carrying not only their own but also others' key \n\nideas into the next conversation, it seems to unfreeze fixed posi-\n\ntions and create a more open and exploratory climate for the \n\nemergence of new insight. Emmett Miller, M.D., who hosted a \n\nCafé for diverse community members in Nevada City, California, \n\ndescribes how this happens: "Usually in a group we get into our \n\nroles. We polarize or focus on our little corner of the whole topic \n\nor question. But in a Café conversation, when you move to the \n\nnext table, even if you come in with your own 'position,' you can't \n\nget stuck there because you're asked to bring your whole table's \n\nkey ideas about the question to the next conversation. You're \n\nsaying, 'This is what came up at our table,' and discovering how \n\nanother group makes sense of it. Then, together with others,\n\nyou're taking it to the next level of thinking. You're having inti-\n\nmate conversations with a few people at a time, but as you move \n\naround the room you suddenly feel like you're having a common \n\nconversation with ten, or twenty, or thirty, or a hundred people-- \n\nthe whole room! There is a kind of magic in having this unex-\n\npected experience together." \n\nThe Way Life Works \n\nIt wasn't until I was introduced to Mitchell Waldrop's spellbind-\n\ning book Complexity: The Emerging Science at the Edge of Order and \n\nChaos (1992) that I began to consider more deeply the way the \n\nWorld Café process engages new levels of collaborative thinking \n\nand supports the development of unexpected insights, particularly \n\nin large-group settings. Waldrop brings scientific ideas to life as he \n\ndescribes the adventures of the multidisciplinary scientists at the \n\nSanta Fe Institute who did groundbreaking work in the field of \n\ncomplex adaptive systems. \n\nOur best Café experiences seem to embody the discoveries \n\nabout learning and change that have been illuminated by a number \n\nof the Santa Fe Institute researchers, as well as by authors from other \n\nfields who have been exploring the dynamics of living systems and \n\ntheir implications for human organizations and communities (Alex-\n\nander, 1979; Allee, 2002; Capra, 1996, 2002; Hegelson, 1995; \n\nMcMaster, 1995; Stacey, 2001; Wheatley and Kellner-Rogers, 1996). \n\nJohn Holland, one of the Santa Fe Institute pioneers chroni-\n\ncled in Waldrop's intellectual adventure story, focuses his research \n\non fundamental processes of learning and adapta-\n\ntion from cells to social systems. He emphasizes \n\nthat optimum learning and development occur in \n\nsystems in which there is a rich web of interac-\n\ntions, along with an environment of novelty \n\nwhere new opportunities and spaces of possibility \n\ncan be explored. Holland's discoveries are com-\n\nplemented by Doyne Farmer's theory that surpris-\n\ning new possibilities in any system emerge not \n\nsolely from the individual parts or nodes of the network but \n\nrather from the connections among them. \n\n112 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nOptimum learning and \n\ndevelopment occur in sys-\n\ntems in which there is a \n\nrich web of interactions\n\nIn The Embodied Mind (1992), cognitive scientists Varela, \n\nThompson, and Rosch point to the intriguing possibility that the \n\ndynamics of new learning and development in the brain involve \n\nthe same type of relational networks as in other living systems, all \n\nof which exhibit self-organizing and emergent properties when \n\nthere are simple components that have dynamic connections to \n\neach other. They add that when an "attractor" is present to focus \n\nattention, even a simple network has rich self-organizing capacities. \n\nAnd, as we shared in chapter 1, Francisco Varela and Humberto \n\nMaturana made the key insight that in human systems we "bring \n\nforth" the worlds we experience through our "social coupling \n\nthrough language in the network of conversations" in which we \n\nparticipate as part of the ongoing flow of our lives (1992, p. 232). \n\nTaken together, I found these diverse insights very intriguing. \n\nMight we be able to engage these discoveries about the way life \n\nworks to enable groups in conversation to access greater mutual \n\nintelligence? Could the World Café process enable us to make the \n\nrole of networks of conversation in co-evolving our future together \n\nmore visible and actionable? By intentionally inviting diverse partic-\n\nipants and encouraging each person's unique contribution, we bring \n\nmore variety to the "ecology" of conversation. As participants then \n\nmove from table to table, carrying seed ideas from one Café table to \n\nanother, they link and connect their thoughts, ideas, and questions, \n\nmirroring Maturana and Varela's iterating networks of conversation. \n\nThe introduction of powerful questions seems to function as \n\nan attractor that focuses attention in the "synapses of the group \n\nmind," thus activating the conversational network's self-organiz-\n\ning capacity. The new connections simultaneously create a space \n\nof novelty, such as John Holland described, in which new \n\nopportunities and spaces of possibility can be explored with the \n\nopportunity to "bring forth a world" together. \n\nIn addition, the World Café intentionally connects the parts \n\nto the whole by combining the intimacy of a four- to five-person \n\ndialogue with the cross-pollination of ideas that occurs through \n\nradiating rounds of conversation. By encouraging people to carry \n\nforward the essential or most exciting ideas from their earlier \n\nCross-Pollinate and Connect Diverse Perspectives 113 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nA Living Network \n\nQuestion as Attractor \n\nDiverse Perspectives \n\nCross-Pollination \n\nEmergence \n\nconversations, the essence of the whole tends to become more \n\nvisible as key ideas and insights travel rapidly through the con-\n\nversational web. Café participants have described this experience \n\nas a "resonance of thought," "lighting up the system in the \n\nroom," or "an accelerated evolutionary development of ideas." \n\nThe Emergence of Wholeness \n\nWe're intrigued by the lines of inquiry that the new sciences are \n\nrevealing and the questions they raise for the theory and practice \n\nof dialogue. World Café conversations hold the promise of pro-\n\nviding one intentional way not only to engage the fascinating net-\n\nwork dynamics of emergence but also to access--in their best \n\nmoments--the unique relationship between the individual and \n\nthe collective that enables a special type of mutual intelligence to \n\nemerge, the type of intelligence that the physicist David Bohm \n\nsaw as the great promise of dialogue for our common future. \n\nBohm described the type of awareness and holistic intelligence \n\n114 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nIn each conversation, individual contributions are focused on questions that matter. \n\nPeople build on one another's ideas-- everyone contributes from their own perspective to create new understanding. \n\nAs people make new connections, sparks of insight begin to emerge that no one would have alone. \n\nThe whole continues to evolve into greater coherence--the discovery of collective intelligence. \n\nAs people share insights between tables, the "magic in the middle" and a sense of the whole become more accessible.\n\nthat emerges in authentic dialogue as occurring not only at the \n\nindividual but simultaneously at the collective level. "It's a \n\nharmony of the individual and the collective," he said, "in which \n\nthe whole constantly moves toward coherence" (1996, p. 27). \n\nOur colleague, Tom Atlee (2003), describes the type of creative \n\nintegration and higher-order thinking that occurs when diverse \n\nperspectives are engaged in dialogue as "co-intelligence." Co-\n\nintelligence is an apt description of the magic that World Café \n\nhosts and participants often describe when they reflect on their \n\nmost productive Café dialogues. Mark Gerzon, president of the \n\nMediators Foundation, provides a poignant example of "the \n\nmagic." While hosting a very challenging dialogue between Israeli \n\nArabs and Jews, he recalls that "at the crucial midpoint, when the \n\ngroup seemed at an impasse, I suggested that we shift into a World \n\nCafé process over dinner. The question was: 'What story can you \n\ntell that will help the others at your table understand your perspec-\n\ntive on the conflict in Israel between Jews and Palestinians?' A \n\nmathematics whiz who was participating in the meeting developed \n\nan innovative method for ensuring that everyone rotated to 100 \n\npercent different partners. The stories were incredibly powerful, \n\nand the experience of consecutive storytelling with different part-\n\nners across the various fault lines fertilized the hard soil. The next \n\nmorning, when the breakthrough happened, I knew in my heart \n\nthat the fertilizing process of Café storytelling among all of the \n\nmembers was a key factor in making that breakthrough possible." \n\nKenoli Oleari, a community development specialist, describes \n\nthe moment he had a similar experience in a large-group Café \n\nconversation. "Something clicked for me about the World Café," \n\nhe says. "I developed a visceral sense of what could come from \n\nthe 'voice in the center of the room.' As the conversations wove \n\nthemselves through the Café, shifting between various configura-\n\ntions of people and chemistries of interaction, I could feel how a \n\nsense of the whole--of something more than the assembled indi-\n\nviduals--could grow. I was a bit awestruck by this epiphany." \n\nCarolyn Baldwin, the former assistant area superintendent of \n\nschools in Polk County, Florida, adds that the \n\nCross-Pollinate and Connect Diverse Perspectives 115 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n(continued on p. 117)\n\n116 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nS T O RY \n\nSUDDENLY, IT CRYSTALLIZED: THE FIELDING GRADUATE INSTITUTE \n\nAs Told By \n\nBo Gyllenpalm and Barclay Hudson \n\nWe had been struggling for more than two years to explain \n\nhow our program was different from other online pro-\n\ngrams, but we just couldn't find it. To help further our think-ing, we invited our faculty counterparts at Golden Gate University, which offers online programs through both its tra-ditional departments and its own "cybercampus," to partici-pate in a joint World Café conversation focusing on the question: "What can we learn from each other regarding online delivery of courses?" \n\nWhen our Golden Gate colleagues left, we continued with our own Reflection Café. During several Café rotations, we moved among tables, connecting our ideas about what was stimulated for us by listening to each other and to the perspec-tives of the Golden Gate participants in the earlier Café. Sud-denly, toward the end of our large-group conversation, a core \n\nidea crystallized as if from nowhere: a clear vision of the unique contribution Fielding offers both in program purpose and design. Although it was one person who spoke it out, she voiced our collective understanding of the deep intellectual purpose that brought us together in the first place. \n\nThe idea was simple, but for us, profound. Maybe that's how insight is. It was the idea of "metalearning." Suddenly, we all saw our mutual commitment to what lies behind and above the online skills and professional content learning: the personal and collective self-awareness and critical thinking skills that carry over well beyond curricula and formal schooling to lifelong learning and life values. We are now using specific cri-teria related to metalearning in designing our curriculum and in sharing our program's unique identity with students and other institutions. The unexpected collective "sur-prise" that emerged that day from our faculty Café was a turning point in our strategic direction for the future. \n\n. . . \n\nBo Gyllenpalm, Ph.D., and \n\nBarclay Hudson, Ph.D., are \n\nfounding core faculty mem-\n\nbers of the online master's \n\nprogram in organizational \n\nmanagement and organiza-\n\ntional development at the \n\nFielding Graduate Institute. \n\nThis is the story of the unex-\n\npected discovery, through a \n\nWorld Café dialogue, of a \n\ncore strategic insight that \n\nshaped the future of their \n\nprogram.\n\nnetworked structure of the World Café enables the group "to have \n\nmultiple eyes focused from different parts of a system on the same set \n\nof questions. Those eyes are literally moving around the questions \n\nwith all their perspectives." "The wholeness," she explains, "comes \n\nfrom being able to see the system from many different angles." Con-\n\nnecting people and perspectives around core questions in ways that \n\nmake seeing the whole more likely is what World Café learning is all \n\nabout. \n\nDesigning for Emergence \n\nThe World Café process is not simply an interesting vehicle for the \n\nrandom emergence of collective intelligence. Rather, it embodies a \n\nsimple but intentional architecture of engagement--creating the \n\nconditions for the arrival of serendipitous discoveries, new patterns \n\nof meaning, and the "voice in the center of the room"--especially \n\nin groups that are larger than most traditional dialogue circles. \n\nBut how does this actually work? Our conversations with \n\nphysicist Fritjof Capra have shed light on this question. He points \n\nout that there's a natural tension between designed structures, like \n\nformal organizational charts, and emergent structures, like the \n\ninformal ways work actually gets done in most organizations. \n\nDesigned structures have predetermined specifications; emergent \n\nstructures often self-organize in ways that cannot be predicted. \n\nWorld Café conversations simultaneously engage both the inten-\n\ntional process of design and the natural process of emergence in \n\norder to encourage coherence without control. \n\nAll seven of the Café design principles work together as a \n\ndesign for emergence in order to increase the likelihood (but \n\nnever the certainty) of enlivening a generative and focused field of \n\ninquiry, where the magic of collective understanding and insight \n\ncan be revealed. However, it is the creative cross-pollination of peo-\n\nple and ideas combined with the disciplined use of questions as \n\nattractors that is perhaps the World Café's defining contribution to \n\ndialogic learning and collective intelligence. David Marsing, former \n\nsenior executive at Intel, points out that carefully framed \n\nquestions operate as attractors around which the web of cross-\n\npollinating ideas evolves to create coherent patterns of meaning. \n\nCross-Pollinate and Connect Diverse Perspectives 117 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nIn reflecting on how he believes this works, Marsing says, "You \n\nhave the question sitting on the table as a starting point, but as \n\npeople move in the rounds of dialogue, each person orients to the \n\nquestion in a different way. The connections grow fast with each \n\nrotation. You can imagine a three-dimensional network forming, \n\nboth in depth and breadth, around the original question. I would \n\ncall it the development of a higher fusion of thinking. It's more \n\ndirectional than random evolution. It is more like co-emergence." \n\nFritjof Capra adds that when a breakthrough or emergence to \n\na new state of understanding occurs in this type of networked \n\nexchange, it "involves an experience of creativity that often feels like \n\nmagic" (2002, p. 117). In fact, Danish World Café pioneer Finn \n\nVoldtofte describes the experience of new insight that emerges in \n\nthe midst of many Café dialogues as "the magic in the middle." \n\nCafé Variations: Mixing People and Ideas Creatively \n\nThe variety of ways to increase the richness and intensity of inter-\n\nactions in a World Café conversation in order to reveal unexpected \n\npatterns of meaning is limited only by the goals of the gathering \n\nand your imagination as host. This is a domain where the art of \n\nhosting is especially important, and where your creativity as a host \n\ncan make the difference between an interesting conversation and \n\nthe magic of experiencing collective intelligence in action. \n\nThe most prominent and consistent World Café pattern for \n\nconnecting people and ideas is for participants physically to move \n\nin iterative rounds of conversation that are usually twenty to thirty \n\nminutes in length, although rounds have gone significantly longer \n\ndepending on the intention of the gathering, the time you have \n\navailable, and the kind of information you are generating around \n\nthe questions being explored. A round of conversation usually \n\nincludes one host staying with his or her table to welcome guests \n\nand to offer them the highlights of their Café table's earlier conver-\n\nsation while the remaining members travel as "ambassadors of \n\nmeaning" to other tables to share and gather collective insights. \n\nSometimes one host stays for all rounds, particularly when you \n\nneed the host to serve as a "content steward" for data that may be \n\n118 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nused in later action planning, or when you want members to travel \n\nduring a second round and then return to their home table to \n\ndeepen their exploration. At other times the host changes after the \n\nsecond round so that each host can also experience being a trav-\n\neler. This can be especially useful when the goal is building a web \n\nof new relationships and fostering a sense of community. \n\nAnother possibility, developed by our colleague Finn \n\nVoldtofte, is for the host to stay at the table at the end of the first \n\nround, while the three or four others at the table "travel for the \n\nlistening," each going to a new table for brief ten-minute learning \n\nvisits. Their job is to collect one or two gems or seed ideas from \n\nthe stories shared with them by the hosts of other conversations, \n\nand bring these key ideas back for consideration during the final \n\nround of synthesis at their home table. \n\nCharles Savage, who first introduced us to the idea of cross-pol-\n\nlinating ideas as a part of the World Café pattern, uses a learning \n\nvisit variation that he calls "dynamic teaming and knowledge net-\n\nworking" to help people develop new project ideas. After an initial \n\nround of conversation in tables of four, two members from Table 1, \n\nfor example, will move to Table 2 and Table 3 while two will \n\nremain at Table 1 to share with their two new visitors the project \n\nideas they've developed. The job of the visitors is to help enhance \n\nthese ideas and offer additional creative possibilities. For the third \n\nround, people return to their home table, sharing the ways their \n\noriginal thinking has been enriched by their learning visits and con-\n\nsidering the linkages between their ideas and those of other teams. \n\nAnother common variation can be used when there are multi-\n\nple threads of inquiry on a common theme. In this scenario, each \n\ntable tackles a separate, but related, question. For example, a \n\nStrategy Café that Verna Allee hosted for a computer firm in \n\nAustralia had six overarching strategy questions. She divided the \n\ntables throughout the room into sectors, with tables in each sec-\n\ntor addressing one of the big questions. Table hosts stayed as the \n\n"question stewards," while others in the group rotated to tables in \n\nother sectors to learn about and make contributions to additional \n\nparts of the strategy puzzle that interested them. They then \n\nCross-Pollinate and Connect Diverse Perspectives 119 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nCross-Pollinating Ideas\n\nreturned to their home table to share the relevance of what they'd \n\nlearned to the question they began with. Finally, all table stewards \n\ncame together to share their tables' collective discoveries in rela-\n\ntion to the strategy as a whole. \n\nThis approach can also be used when people generate their own \n\nquestions on the topic being explored. In the first round, each table \n\ngenerates its own core question that is then posted on the table. In \n\nsubsequent rounds, while one question steward stays, the other peo-\n\nple move to other tables to explore the questions raised by their col-\n\nleagues. These question stewards are not facilitators in the traditional \n\nsense but simply hosts, who welcome arriving members and share \n\nthe essence of the evolving conversation as they understand it so all \n\ncan continue to contribute their perspectives to what's emerging. \n\nMarimba Giam, a Café host in Singapore, has experimented with \n\ncreative ways to cross-pollinate ideas with teachers and others in a \n\nvariety of settings. She shares this: "One might think that people \n\nwould be confused with the movement, and the number of connec-\n\ntions involved. On the contrary, as people move from table to table \n\nsharing the ideas that really struck them, the common themes or key \n\ninsights that have practical applications tend to emerge naturally." \n\nPeter Senge of MIT adds that he believes the commonality and \n\npatterns of meaning that can emerge in a World Café conversation \n\nby intentionally cross-pollinating perspectives and moving people \n\nbetween rounds of conversation is "not the commonality of homo-\n\ngeneity. In fact, it's virtually the opposite. The commonality emerges \n\nfrom internalizing a richer and richer diversity and then recognizing \n\nthis richer and richer web that connects." \n\nSometimes it's not the people but the ideas that move. For \n\nexample, at an Institute of Noetic Sciences conference, the room was \n\ntoo crowded to have Café tables or to have people rotate between \n\ngroups. People sat in small conversational clusters. In that instance, \n\neach participant was given a card on which to write what he or she \n\nconsidered to be an essential idea or key insight from the first round \n\nof conversation. Each person then stood and faced outward from \n\ntheir cluster and exchanged the cards as an idea gift with a person \n\nfrom another group. Each small group continued by reading the \n\n120 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nideas they'd received as gifts from members of other clusters, using \n\nthe new connections these suggested for their ongoing dialogue. \n\nMembers can also use cards or large sticky notes to synthesize core \n\nquestions or key insights from the table as a whole, which can be \n\nsent forward from one table or conversation cluster to another to \n\nseed the inquiry as the conversation progresses. \n\nWe're continuing to discover new ways to connect diverse per-\n\nspectives as Café hosts around the world experiment with myriad \n\nvariations. The examples abound. A Café on the future of the Dan-\n\nish Constitution was hosted with participants in canoes on a lake. \n\nOn a learning journey that explored Mexico's social development, \n\nmembers moved periodically between minivans at rest stops, shar-\n\ning their emerging perspectives as they continued to travel. One \n\ninnovative host had members move between Café groups located \n\nat nature's "tables," each of which was a large redwood tree. \n\nAnother used different colored mugs to facilitate the mixing and \n\nmoving of people from different functional areas in a single busi-\n\nness unit. The colored mugs enabled a "max-mix" for creative \n\nexchange, and at the same time, allowed people from the same \n\narea to find each other easily during the action planning phases of \n\nthe Café dialogue. Experiments like these are described regularly at \n\nthe World Café Web site. We'd welcome your experiences as well. \n\nCross-Pollinate and Connect Diverse Perspectives 121 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nQuestions for Reflection \n\nTake a moment to reflect on a time when diverse perspec-tives helped shed new light on a challenging situation. What were the conditions that enabled that diversity of thought to make its contribution? \n\nConsider an upcoming meeting on an issue or concern that you care about. What diverse perspectives and voices could contribute to a more creative exploration of that question? How can you help those perspectives be included? \n\nWhat are the various ways you might encourage greater cross-pollination of ideas during the meeting in order to gain greater collective insight?\n\n122 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nE I G H T \n\nWe listened, and through that listening a dynamic of its \n\nown developed. The end result . . . was a group spirit and \n\ngroup coherence stronger than any I have ever known, yet it \n\nwas a "dance with many dancers," a group of individuals \n\nwho had found an emergent reality drawing our differences \n\ninto a meaningful whole. \n\n--Danah Zohar and Ian Marshall, The Quantum Society \n\nPRINCIPLE 6 \n\nListen Together for Patterns, Insights, and Deeper Questions\n\n123 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat i f l i s tening together \n\nreveals a deeper inte l l igence?\n\n124 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWHAT'S IN THE MIDDLE OF THE TABLE? SCANDINAVIAN SUSTAINABILITY FORUM \n\nAs Told By \n\nChristina Carlmark \n\nIn my previous position as vice president of environmental \n\naffairs at the largest telecommunications company in Scandi-\n\nnavia, I had the opportunity to explore the role of the informa-tion technology and communication (infocom) sector in creating a sustainable future. The position was newly created, and I was happy to take on this challenge since I have been passion-ate about environmental and sustainability questions for most of my life. \n\nInformation and communication services play a key role in everyday life. For example, videoconferencing and other virtual meeting tools enable people to work together across long distances without requiring travel and the use of nonrenewable fuels. I felt that we could better harness the power of this sector to benefit the environment if we were able to create open dialogue in a global community of key stakeholders, so I decided to sponsor a strategic \n\ndialogue on the topic using a World Café approach. In the first phase, we brought together global thought leaders who had an interest in \n\ninfocom and a sustainable future. We invited out-of-the-box thinkers, environmentalists, sci-entists, academics, futurists, infocom specialists, young people, politicians, and corporate representatives. Their job at what we called the Focus Search Café was to help discover the areas of inquiry that would be critical for key stakeholders to explore in the next four to eight years. At this Café, the thought leaders came to a consensus that a key issue was to under-stand how the infocom services industry might help or hinder the development of a more sustainable future in relation to transportation. For example, trucking companies, by invest-ing in computerized ordering and dispatching systems, can save huge amounts of mileage. \n\nAn internal/external research group then embarked on a five-month period of discovery on the critical questions related to the topic of infocom and transportation. Their synthesis, which they called the "World Insighters Report," posed key dilemmas and decision points for the next phase of the conversation. We called this second phase the Roundtable Dialogue, and we decided to hold it too in a Café format. \n\nWe took a big risk in the Roundtable Café. We really didn't know what would happen! We invited influential people from important cross-sector groups with very divergent per-\n\nChristina Carlmark, former \n\nvice president of environ-\n\nmental affairs at the Swedish \n\ntelecom corporation, Telia, \n\npresently serves as the mar-\n\nketing director at the merged \n\nSwedish/Finnish telecom, \n\nTeliaSonora. This is the story \n\nof a unique multi-stakeholder \n\nCafé on the role of the info-\n\ncom industry in creating sus-\n\ntainable futures--one that \n\nnurtured the capacity for lis-\n\ntening together in new ways. \n\nS T O RY\n\nListen Together for Patterns, Insights, and Deeper Questions 125 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nspectives: senior leaders from major transportation companies including DHL, UPS, and the Swedish railroads; CEOs from companies that use their transport services; key players from infocom companies who are developing transportation technologies; a member of the Euro-pean Parliament; a key leader from Greenpeace; and the head of the traffic division of a large Swedish city. \n\nAs you can imagine, these are not people who often, if ever, sit down to think together. If they do, it may be in a heated negotiation, not a Café dialogue! These power players are used to meetings that are conducted as very formal discussions, usually around big confer-ence tables, with structured agendas and tight control to make sure that nothing goes wrong. \n\nAs a sponsor of this event, I was somewhat concerned about what would happen when the people came to the meeting. We needed a natural way for people to really listen to each other's viewpoints and not get polarized. The Café environment helped set the tone. When people came in the door, some were a bit surprised by the friendly atmosphere and the small tables, but they joined in, got seated, and actually seemed curious to see what would happen next. \n\nThen came our next innovation. Right in the beginning, after making introductions, we had a young, newly married woman in her twenties share her thoughts with the group. She talked about wanting to raise a family. She said she hoped that this group, who had the power to change the direction we were headed, could find ideas together that could help-- not only for today but for future generations, including their own children and grandchil-dren. There was a very pensive atmosphere in the room when she finished sharing her story. \n\nWe then did something even more daring, but that worked amazingly well. We didn't do any teaching about dialogue. Instead, we simply introduced a "dialogue stone" as a way to encourage better listening and avoid arguments and defensive positions. We brought in beautiful stones that were thousands of years old from a pebble beach on an island not far from the outskirts of Stockholm. They symbolized our land and the history of the natural world. We placed one stone in the middle of each table, along with a small vase of fresh flow-ers and colored pens. \n\nWe then shared the basic idea behind the dialogue stones. We said that usually in meet-ings like this the discussion speeds up so fast that people have a hard time listening because they want to make sure their own ideas get heard. This is especially true when the participants come from very different camps. We asked the group to experiment with the dialogue stone as a practical tool for listening together and finding what was in the middle of the table. Only the person holding the stone would speak. As long as he or she held the stone, others would simply listen without interrupting. This would allow the individual who was holding the stone to stop and take a breath while thinking about what she really wanted to say instead of having to keep babbling so someone else wouldn't cut in while she finished her thought. \n\nWe also asked people to imagine themselves asking their inner judge to step aside and take a rest period, just for a little while, from determining whether someone else was right\n\n126 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nor wrong. The goal at this stage was just to listen together to see how everyone viewed the topic and what contribution each perspective could bring. We encouraged people to speak in the first person, for themselves, and not with their formal organizational hat on--another departure from traditional meetings. We also asked the members if they would be willing to hold off presenting solutions, because this initial phase was just meant to find the deeper themes and questions and not the answers. \n\nAfter this short introduction, participants began the first round of the Café. Each mem-ber took the stone and shared the key insights, thoughts, or deeper questions that were trig-gered for them by the World Insighters Report. The task of the other three participants at each Café table was simply to listen, but in a special way. We asked them to keep track of the ideas that emerged, and to draw in the middle of the tablecloth any interesting connections they could see among the ideas. When all had shared their first ideas and thoughts, the stone was placed back in the middle of the table. Anyone could then pick up the stone and add any comments or thoughts that had bubbled up while they were listening to their colleagues. \n\nUsing the dialogue stones made a big difference, even though some people, of course, still had the urge to just jump in with their own solutions. We found out, however, that peo-ple basically already know how to listen well when they are really interested in what's going on. We just helped slow things down and focus their attention on what to listen for. Instead of standing opposite each other they were, in a way, standing next to each other, all looking and listening in the same direction--the center. \n\nIn the second Café round, we added something new. We asked everyone to begin listen-ing together as a group for the deeper assumptions and patterns of meaning underlying their varied perspectives and write them on the tablecloth as well. People were still using the dis-cipline of the dialogue stone. We encouraged them to ask questions to help each other clar-ify the different as well as common assumptions or mindsets. \n\nWe changed tables again and started the third round of Café dialogue, continuing to see what was being revealed in the center of each table. By this time people were really into it. Each time we asked them to listen together for the next deeper layer of understanding they actually seemed to become even more interested in each other's ideas and in the issues. That was a big surprise to me. \n\nWhen the third round was over, we opened the conversation to the whole group to pool their collective knowledge and insights. We had hired a professional graphics specialist to create a large wall mural of people's reflections--which was like having a big tablecloth right in front of the whole group. We could see the themes, the assumptions, the connections between ideas, and the aha's coming out on the mural right in front of us. There was a spe-cial energy in the room that afternoon--very different than when everyone first entered with their individual mindsets. The participants were excited at this stage because they'd found something together that wasn't there when they started, or maybe it was there all the time but they just had not had common ways to see it together before.\n\nListen Together for Patterns, Insights, and Deeper Questions 127 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhen we had the checkout at the end, people said that this was the first time they had sat down with others who might be considered competitors--or even opponents--and had been able to arrive at a better understanding of each other's deeper beliefs. They found that they had more in common than they had originally thought and realized that none of them alone had the power to push the others into accepting their own solutions. \n\nI believe this way of having strategic conversations may make it easier to get coordinated action, but it's not in the same way we think about it most of the time, with voting and long lists of action steps. Much of the time nothing comes out of those lists anyway. Instead, with the special Café way of talking and listening together, people become interested in going back into their own situations with their new understandings to make positive things hap-pen, in large part because of the new personal relationships they've built with each other. People with very different perspectives and experience begin to see one another's common humanity. And that's a good outcome, whatever final decisions get implemented. \n\n. . . \n\nPerspectives & Observations \n\nT \n\nhere's a word I love in Spanish--el meollo (pronounced \n\nel may-oy-yo). El meollo means the essential nature or fun-\n\ndamental substance of a thing. It also means understand-\n\ning. As World Café hosts, we're searching for ways to access the \n\nhuman capacity to discover el meollo--not alone, but through \n\nweaving connected strands of meaning throughout the entire con-\n\nversation, even when that includes dozens or hundreds of people. \n\nListening together for patterns, insights, and deeper questions-- \n\nboth at an individual Café table and between tables as the conver-\n\nsations radiate outward--enables us to develop this collective \n\ncapacity to sense the heart of the matter. \n\nListening To, Listening With, and Listening For \n\nIt was Anne Dosher, the elder, mentor, and "guardian of the soul" \n\nof the World Café, who first introduced us to the special kind of \n\ncollaborative listening that has become a hallmark of World Café \n\nconversations. A former member of the core faculty and the board \n\nof trustees of the California School of Professional Psychology, \n\nGetting to the Heart of the Matter\n\nAnne has spent a lifetime thinking about how we can discover \n\ninnovative solutions to complex issues in communities and other \n\ncollective settings. \n\nIn one of our many conversations during the early days of the \n\nWorld Café, Anne spoke to us about the importance of what she \n\ncalls "gathered attention"--the capacity both at the individual \n\nand the collective level to engage in the type of listening that \n\nenables new patterns of meaning and innovative possibilities to \n\nbe called forth in conversational exchange. Anne drew upon \n\ninsights from the late theologian and author Nelle Morton \n\n(1985), who spoke of the special quality of listening to another \n\nperson with one's full attention as being a creative force that can \n\nevoke ideas that actually didn't exist until the other was "heard \n\ninto speech." \n\nBut gathered attention doesn't stop here. At the collective \n\nlevel, it moves beyond listening to other people speaking, and \n\nsimultaneously engages our ability to listen with each other for \n\nconnections and patterns of meaning as well as listen for new \n\ninsights or deeper questions that emerge in the space between \n\ndifferent perspectives. In fact, the word intelligence originally \n\ncomes from the Latin inter and legere, which means "to gather \n\nunderstanding in between." That's what we mean in Café settings \n\nwhen we talk about the magic in the middle. Gathered attention \n\ninvolves a more holistic type of listening, one that pays attention \n\nto the intelligence or deeper meaning that is moving among us \n\ncollectively. It focuses on the cluster of related meanings that \n\nemerge both within and between conversations, thus focusing \n\nthe attention of the group in a different way. \n\nHans Kuendig, in describing his first Café, tries to capture \n\nwhat this type of listening feels like in real time: "You're in this \n\nexciting round of conversation, and you're hearing more and \n\nmore of the threads coming out, and you're wanting to just jump \n\nin more, but everybody's also listening and thinking at the same \n\ntime. Everyone helps to uncover the patterns. That's part of the \n\nenergy that's created." In one of our seminars on strategic conver-\n\nsation, another Café member shared his own vivid description of \n\n128 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nListening Together\n\nthe interplay between the individual and the group that results \n\nfrom this type of shared listening. "It's not that it's common \n\nthinking or groupthink," he explained, "and it's not condensing. \n\nIt's both individual and group learning in a larger context of col-\n\nlective relatedness of thought." \n\nRelating to the Larger Whole \n\nOne of the dilemmas that most of us experience when coming to \n\ndialogue for the first time is the difficulty of moving away from \n\nour existing judgments and personal positions. Yet we've discov-\n\nered that something quite unexpected happens in World Café \n\nconversations when we ask everyone in the room to become \n\nambassadors of meaning--listening together and carrying forward \n\nthe essential ideas or aha insights into progressive \n\nrounds of conversation. Rigid positions seem to \n\ndrop away as people listen together in order to \n\ndiscover creative connections among the multiple \n\nperspectives that others contribute. That's when the \n\n"magic" often reveals itself in Café dialogues. \n\nHow does this work? Web site designer Amy \n\nLenzo shares a beautiful image that came to her \n\nafter a large World Café with several hundred people who were \n\nlistening together for the deeper questions in their midst: "It was \n\nlike making and refining clay pottery together. The pot wasn't \n\nbeing shaped by an individual's questions anymore. It became the \n\ngroup's questions that were being refined by the process of each \n\nindividual speaking. Their essential form was being smoothed, \n\nrefined, and made clearer by each other's words. The essence was \n\nbeing drawn out." \n\nThis type of gathered attention not only reveals the essence of \n\nthe whole but can also reveal its broad expanse, as Claudia Chen-\n\nder, law student from British Columbia, points out. "There's \n\nsomething about the World Café that's hard to articulate," she \n\nexplains. "It's like you come upon a landscape and you are stand-\n\ning on a hilltop with this wide vista spread out below you. You \n\ncan't travel all of it, but you get a sense of and an appreciation for \n\nthe whole." As Café hosts, we're continuing to discover new ways \n\nListen Together for Patterns, Insights, and Deeper Questions 129 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nRigid positions seem to drop \n\naway as people listen \n\ntogether in order to discover \n\ncreative connections\n\nto nurture that type of holistic appreciation. And what we have \n\ndiscovered is that using visual language may be one of the keys. \n\nVisual Language, Visual Listening \n\nIn our conversation about hospitable space in chapter 4, I shared \n\nwith you my excitement when we were first introduced to the dis-\n\ncoveries of Michael Schrage, a visiting scholar at MIT's media lab, \n\non the importance of informal environments for collaborative \n\nlearning. However, just as important for understanding how Café \n\nconversations work is Schrage's reflection that shared visual \n\nspaces--from paper napkins in restaurants to computer white-\n\nboards and other visual meeting tools--are critical elements for \n\ncollaboration and co-creativity. According to Schrage, for real col-\n\nlaboration to flourish "the images, maps, and perceptions bounc-\n\ning around in people's brains must be given a form that other \n\npeople's images, maps, and perceptions can shape, alter, or other-\n\nwise add value to. . . . It takes shared space to create shared under-\n\nstanding" (1990, p. 99; italics in original). \n\nSchrage's image of networks of shared spaces serving as a \n\n"conceptual and technical playground for the collaborators" clari-\n\nfied what World Café hosts have witnessed in Café conversations \n\n(1990, p. 156). The act of writing, drawing, and playing on the \n\ntablecloths at multiple Café tables seems be one creative example \n\nof the kind of lively network of shared spaces that Schrage is talk-\n\ning about. Could the simple process of intentionally engaging in \n\nshared listening, and then connecting the evolving ideas from \n\nmultiple conversations visually on the Café tablecloths, be key \n\nfactors in the kind of large-scale collaborative learning that we see \n\nin Café dialogues at their best? \n\nI was intrigued and wanted to learn more. I invited Jennifer \n\nHammond Landau and Susan Kelly, two pioneers in graphic \n\nrecording who have participated in at least a hundred Cafés col-\n\nlectively, to share their experience of the relationship between \n\nshared space, visual language, and a group's capacity to listen \n\ntogether for collaborative insight. The conversation that follows \n\nis a synthesis of our musings together. \n\n130 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nJuanita: I want us to explore the place where shared listening, collab-orative learning, and innovation happen--where people begin to see in new ways, and where the connections and the knowledge base expand. \n\nJennifer: One thing occurs to me. It's important to listen for the connections without assuming that you know what they are. There's a fine line between the two. You can listen for connections and cap-ture ideas in multiple ways and at different levels of scale--drawing on a cocktail napkin, on a Café tablecloth, on a billboard-sized wall, and electronically as well. What's important is that you're all looking at the same thing together and making the connections. \n\nJuanita: What helps listening together to happen in the first place? \n\nSusan: Color helps. Color actually connects people to a deeper real-ity. Black-and-white is abstract and linear is abstract. But the world exists in color, and it's not linear. So having color in the center of the table, whether it's the flowers or the colored pens, helps you come closer to your real world, together. And color opens up a wider range of expression. \n\nJennifer: What I've seen is that having the colored pens in the cen-ter gives a "visual voice" to those present--especially those who tend to be quieter in groups. The pens are a powerful reminder that "my voice can be seen and heard if I write on the tablecloth." Humans tend to think in symbols and images, so being able to see your own and others' scribbles and pictures can also help bring forth ideas that you're not even conscious of. That might be called visual listening. \n\nJuanita: Maybe one of the interesting things about finding the magic in the middle has to do with people literally "noting" their voice by whatever scribbles, notes, or pictures they're putting on the tablecloth. \n\nListen Together for Patterns, Insights, and Deeper Questions 131 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nMaking Conversation Visible\n\nSusan: That relates directly to the size of the group at the Café tables. Once you get past four or five at a table, the ideas don't get placed into the center as easily. You might be doodling in your cor-ner, but you're not talking and listening together with others focused on the ideas in the middle of the table. \n\nJuanita: Let's imagine people have done whatever listening and connecting they can at their individual tables. Three new people have just arrived at my table. There are little diagrams in the center of the tablecloth, a few key words, and a bold colorful figure that some-body drew. As the host, I'm sharing what I understand from listening to the conversation during the first round and how that is reflected in the drawings on my tablecloth. Then what happens? \n\nSusan: Everybody who comes to your table says, "It's amazing, because that's some of what we were talking about over there, and over there, and over there, too." \n\nJennifer: I always love that. \n\nSusan: The conversation seems to build because all of the tables have been working with other pieces of the whole puzzle simultane-ously. You begin to sense that you're part of a bigger whole--in real time. You see how it happens, how it works, how you deepen it by interchanging and listening to multiple perspectives around the same topic. \n\nJennifer: It's like concentric rings. The middle starts to expand, and paradoxically, at the same time, it gets more focused. People begin naming patterns and themes. \n\nSusan: People get excited because things are getting illuminated, especially when they really care about the question or if they are interdependent in some way. \n\nJuanita: What about people pressing their own agendas? Are there times when people have not contributed or not felt heard, and then they work their own individual agendas in the larger group? \n\nJennifer: That seems to happen less in Café settings than in other groups. By the time you get to the mural-size tablecloth or some other method through which the sense of the whole group is being reflected, most everyone has had multiple opportunities to be lis-tened to and other people have built on or carried their ideas into other conversations. So they're not generally pressing their own indi-vidual agendas. That's quite rare. \n\nJuanita: Now, let's imagine we're at the stage of hosting the conversa-tion of the whole in a town meeting style. Instead of traditional report-backs, we're asking people to link and connect their ideas at the level of the whole room from their listening together in the earlier rounds. \n\n132 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nJennifer: If innovation is going to happen in the whole group, this is the place where it sparks. Sometimes an image or symbol that appeared at one of the tables just pops out into the whole-group con-versation and crystallizes the whole thing. \n\nSusan: Then the group can acknowledge and bless their collective insight. \n\nJuanita: That's a wonderful observation. Maybe through all this focused messiness and searching together--the visual listening that appears in the images, the symbols, and shared space of the table-cloths--people begin to sense what's really central, not only in terms of intellectual content but also discovering together the heart of what the community really cares about. \n\nJennifer: The magic is experiencing our own and other people's humanity around whatever the content is. That's what the World Café is about for me, and what having shared space and shared lis-tening allows us to discover. \n\nListen Together for Patterns, Insights, and Deeper Questions 133 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nGraphic Recording in Action\n\nReflection: A Different Type of Listening \n\nReflection is a core practice in Café conversations. The combina-\n\ntion of gathered attention and collective reflection on what's at \n\nthe heart of the matter--el meollo--is what enables the group to \n\nlisten to and for the whole in order to sense patterns, themes, and \n\ndeeper questions. Discovering the optimum balance between \n\ndynamic engagement and mutual reflection is one of our key \n\nlearning edges as World Café hosts. Excitement builds as people \n\nmove between tables, but depth can be lost if Café members \n\naren't encouraged to slow down intentionally in order to reflect \n\non their discoveries. \n\nAs the host, you can encourage more reflective listening and \n\nthoughtful consideration of ideas during the conversation by \n\nintroducing a talking piece, like the dialogue stone that Christina \n\nCarlmark introduced to the Café participants in her opening \n\nstory. In addition, many hosts use musical interludes or poetry \n\nrelated to the topic, coupled with individual journaling at appro-\n\npriate times, to create a reflective tone. Samantha Tan of Singa-\n\npore points out that the use of music and poetry has the unique \n\ncapacity to "shift people into a different state . . . opening a space \n\nfor true reflection." Using these approaches, or simply asking \n\nmembers to take a couple of minutes of quiet time to make notes \n\non cards about what struck them in the conversation, all serve as \n\npowerful preludes to collective reflection on learnings, insights, \n\nand deeper questions, either toward the end of a round of conver-\n\nsation or prior to the large-group sharing of discoveries. \n\nYou may find that the simple act of encouraging moments of \n\nsilent reflection--even without music or other introduction--can \n\nyield insights beyond your (or others') imagination. Silence pro-\n\nvides an easily available yet seldom used opportunity to listen for \n\nand access the wisdom that often lies just beneath the surface of \n\nour mutual deliberations. I sometimes think of silence as a way to \n\naccess a deep well in the center of the group. Silence is the pulley, \n\nsimilar to the rope in a well, that enables members to draw a \n\ndeeper wisdom up from the common well of mutual exploration \n\nand experience. Jenni Dunn of Australia, who experienced her first \n\n134 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nRipples of Silence\n\nWorld Café at a Systems Thinking Conference, found that the peri-\n\nodic moments of silent reflection "enabled something dramatically \n\ndifferent to happen. It [the silence] assisted the group by helping \n\nus have the space to understand what was happening and not let-\n\nting it rush away in the immediacy of conversation at the table." \n\nAs a host, you can evoke deeper insights by simply asking \n\nmembers to take a couple of minutes of silence to consider or \n\nmake a few notes about what key insight, idea, or discovery they \n\nare taking from the conversation so far. Other reflection questions \n\ninclude these: \n\n◗ What did you most appreciate about this conversation? \n\n◗ What's had real meaning for you from what you've heard? What sur-\n\nprised you? What challenged you? \n\n◗ What's missing from the picture so far? What is it we're not seeing? \n\nWhat do we need more clarity about? \n\n◗ If there was one thing that hasn't yet been explored but is necessary in \n\norder to reach a deeper level of understanding/clarity, what would that be? \n\nAdditional reflection questions for focusing collective attention, \n\nfinding deeper insight, and creating forward movement are \n\nincluded in chapter 10, "Guiding the Café Process." If you're host-\n\ning a conversation in a natural environment, you can also encour-\n\nage people to spend some time alone outside to reflect on what has \n\nemerged in the conversation, prior to continuing their exploration. \n\nWe are discovering that thinking together in conversation \n\nrequires spaces between the notes in order to hear the music of \n\ncollective wisdom. As a conversation host, give yourself permis-\n\nsion to experiment with multiple approaches to creating the space \n\nfor reflection--for listening individually and collectively to the \n\ndeeper currents beneath the surface. Time for refection is a gift \n\nthat we too rarely give ourselves in the frenetic life of most organ-\n\nizations and communities. Yet World Café and other dialogue \n\nhosts are finding that creating time for reflection is essential to \n\naccessing the often surprising strategic insights that lie at the heart \n\nof effective action. \n\nListen Together for Patterns, Insights, and Deeper Questions 135 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThink of a time when you felt heard and listened to.
