What were the conditions that enabled that to happen? \n\nConsider an upcoming conversation that you will be part of hosting. How might you support the group not only in listening to one another but also in listening with one another for patterns, insights, or deeper questions? \n\nWhat questions for reflection might you offer to deepen mutual inquiry and discovery in an upcoming conversa-tion that's important to you? How and when might these be introduced? \n\nIn what ways might visual listening approaches (that is, graphic recording, drawing or sketching ideas together, or other visual displays) improve the quality of an upcom-ing conversation that you are hosting? \n\n136 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nQuestions for Reflection\n\nThis page intentionally left blank \n\n138 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nN I N E \n\nThere's another aspect to fostering mutual intelligence . . . \n\nwhat I call creating the "collective mindscreen." How can \n\nwe help people sense the whole, together? Harvesting and \n\nsharing key ideas and insights is like putting up tent poles. \n\nIt's what's in the circle revealed by putting up those poles, \n\nnot any of the individual poles, where the meaning of the \n\nconversation as a whole lies. \n\n--Finn Voldtofte, BDO ScanFutura \n\nPRINCIPLE 7 \n\nHarvest and Share Collective Discoveries\n\n139 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat i f the harves t \n\nprovides nourishment for fur ther \n\nexplorat ion and act ion?\n\n140 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nHARVESTING AND PLANTING SEEDS OF CHANGE: UNIVERSITY OF TEXAS AT SAN ANTONIO, EXECUTIVE MBA PROGRAM \n\nAs Told By \n\nRobert H. Lengel \n\nConvening productive conversations is a fundamental leader-\n\nship skill in a knowledge economy. Because of this, the \n\nEMBA program at UTSA includes a focus on developing the capacities to host effective strategic conversations--conversations that catalyze deep learning and forward movement on issues of importance to leaders. We begin by teaching the basic structure and principles of World Café conversations and other dialogue approaches, as well as Appreciative Inquiry and strategic futuring. But to really understand their power to create change and move learning forward, you have to practice. The particular story I'd like to relate is about how one executive MBA class worked with the student affairs organization at UTSA as their practice field. \n\nUTSA is one of the fastest-growing universities in the country. Over my twenty-year tenure, enrollment has grown from about five thousand students to twenty-five thousand. During this time we have also transformed from primarily an undergraduate school to a full-service research institution offering an expansive array of graduate programs. Under the inspired leadership of Dr. Rosalie Ambrosino, our student affairs organization has made significant progress in addressing student service issues related to our growth and changing academic mission. \n\nOur EMBA class took on the challenge of helping Dr. Ambrosino and her organization support both graduate and undergraduate student services by designing and hosting a series of strategic conversations with cross sections of this four-\n\nhundred-person organization. The student affairs organization has six reporting units, so our class divided into six Café study teams, one team per unit. The initial objective was for each of the teams to do "temperature checks" in their unit by asking the following ques-tions: "What is going on now in this organization? What are the key issues, challenges, concerns, and core questions?" \n\nThe study teams used a variety of World Café approaches. We wanted to give them that freedom so they could come back and compare Café approaches and talk about what they'd \n\nBob Lengel, Ph.D., is the \n\nassociate dean for executive \n\neducation and director of \n\nthe Center for Professional \n\nExcellence at the University \n\nof Texas, San Antonio \n\n(UTSA), College of Busi-\n\nness. Through the center he \n\nhas been a pioneer in intro-\n\nducing strategic conversa-\n\ntion and World Café \n\nlearning to organizational \n\nleaders as a way to foster \n\ncreativity, synergy, and \n\nstrategic innovation. This \n\nstory, about a project done \n\nby a recent executive MBA \n\n(EMBA) class, demonstrates \n\nhow insights from one Café \n\ncan be harvested and used \n\nto seed future Cafés, creat-\n\ning an ongoing exploration \n\nthat breaks down organiza-\n\ntional barriers and initiates \n\ncultural change. \n\nS T O RY\n\nHarvest and Share Collective Discoveries 141 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nlearned. I'll give you one example of an innovative approach used by one of the teams to har-vest and share their Café discoveries. \n\nThe team wanted to uncover what they called "the state of the conversation" in their assigned unit. After opening the conversation with personal introductions in a large dialogue circle, the team invited the student affairs folks to join Café tables. \n\nAt each table the team gave participants four different piles of colored paper. They asked the members to first take the yellow paper and write one phrase or sentence per sheet that reflected what people were saying publicly during meetings in their unit. They collected the yellow sheets in a manner that preserved the anonymity of the author. Then they asked the group to take the blue paper and write on it what people were saying in the restroom after the meeting, or at lunch when they were with friends. What was that conversation like? The blue papers were then collected in a similar manner. On the pink sheets, participants were asked to write what they thought other people in the university were saying about stu-dent affairs. Finally, the green sheets captured the answers to the question, "What are you not saying that you wish you could?" \n\nFrom these sheets, the Café team created a monster sticky wall with the colors clustered together from all the tables . . . all the yellows, greens, pinks, and blues. The idea was to har-vest the group's collective thinking and make their ideas visible so people could see new con-nections. They asked the group to take a gallery walk and notice the common themes and unique perspectives that stood out. \n\nThen the student affairs folks returned to their next round of Café conversations around this question: "What questions should we ask you in order to understand what all this is telling us about your organization?" Seeing all their previous thinking harvested and organ-ized on the wall enabled the group to identify the most relevant questions for their situation in student affairs. It was a stimulating experience for the participants to have to think about their own questions as opposed to answers. The questions also triggered thoughtful story-telling about their current reality. And everyone had a lot of fun! \n\nUTSA Monster Sticky Wall\n\n142 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nOnce all six EMBA teams had finished their Temperature Check Cafés in the individual student affairs units, we came back together. Each team shared their Café design, the insights and questions they harvested, a synthesis of their findings about their unit, and their reflec-tions about their own experience. To pull it all together, we held a fairly long Integration Café and had a graphic recorder there to harvest their collective learnings. From the Integration Café, the students discovered, after much deliberation, that what was needed next was not more bits and pieces of information from the individual units. Rather, in order to illuminate the larger picture it was going to be important to work in the space between the units. \n\nSo, in the second semester, we introduced the concept of the Future Search conference. Future Search is a structured step-by-step approach that brings diverse stakeholders through-out a system together to understand their past and present, seek common ground, search for innovative strategies, and build mutual commitment to their shared future (Weisbord and Janoff, 2000). We got permission to take two days with about sixty people from student affairs who had been carefully selected to represent the diversity of voices from all of the indi-vidual organizational units. Our goal here was to have a whole-system conversation, as opposed to a parts conversation. The Future Search conference made it possible to have the organization talk together and see their conversation of the whole made visible on the walls in the form of graphics and stickies, which we could then harvest and use for the next steps. \n\nI think when people can see graphically what they hold in common, they can begin to connect the dots. What emerged from the Future Search event was a common sense of what they, as a whole, wanted student affairs to be in the future. One could say that they discov-ered their "commons sense." The student affairs group generated six initiative thrusts--areas where they wanted to act together--and we agreed to develop the four highest priority ones in subsequent Action Cafés. These Action Cafés would involve members from all of student affairs, not just the people who attended the two-day Future Search event. \n\nAs you can see, World Café conversations and Future Search are very compatible modes of working. So the next step was to figure out how to share the insights from the Future Search conference in a way that enabled those who hadn't been there to actively participate in the upcoming Action Cafés. Fortunately, to take on this challenge the EMBA class had assigned its most creative and technologically savvy class members to a special Future Search documentation team. This team had carefully planned in advance how to harvest the dis-coveries from the Future Search conference so the highlights could be shared with others who hadn't been there. In addition to graphic illustrations, the documentation team made audio and video recordings of all open conversations and kept journals capturing their impressions. From this material, the team developed a creative, multimedia synthesis in the form of a DVD that other EMBA teams could use as they delivered two follow-up briefings that were open to all of student affairs. At the conclusion of each briefing, we posted sign-up sheets, inviting anyone who was interested to participate in one or more of the upcoming student affairs Action Cafés.\n\nHarvest and Share Collective Discoveries 143 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nFor the Action Cafés, the class divided into four Café teams, one for each major ini-tiative that had been chosen from the original six. We wanted this work to be sustainable, so each team worked in close partnership with someone from student affairs. This gave the people in student affairs the design and implementation experience to continue the Cafés on their own once our project was finished. At the end of the project we gave them writ-ten reports from the initial Temperature Check Cafés and the Action Cafés, as well as a final DVD containing audio, video, and other content harvested and synthesized from the entire process. \n\nWe've found that although the raw data like tablecloths and wall graphics are extremely useful for members who have participated in Café dialogues, they are rarely in a form that can easily engage focused conversations in other parts of an organization. This is a real chal-lenge in Cafés as well as other types of strategic dialogue. But when the outputs are inten-tionally blended into a coherent story, then this story can travel and engage others. For example, the DVD stories produced for student affairs have been widely shared across the university and have stimulated conversations about key questions with groups far removed from the original participants. \n\nI would say that we're learning two key things about harvesting and sharing collective discoveries from our Café experiences. First, intentionally harvesting the insights is impor-tant. This is one essential way that everyone who participates can contribute to weaving together the bits and pieces of their emerging collective intelligence into a coherent whole. This is true whether you are doing an individual World Café event or seeing Café conversa-tions as part of a broader process of engagement, as we did with student affairs. \n\nSecond, effective strategic conversations--conversations that can move learning forward in a large system--are made up of a repeating cycle of planting seeds, harvesting the fruits, refining the new seeds, and replanting them in new soil. I believe that the role of the leader is to tend the garden of strategic conversations and make sure that this type of planting and harvesting happens throughout their unit or their organization. At the Center for Profes-sional Excellence, we've found that Café conversations provide key ingredients that leaders can use for tending the soil, sharing food for thought, and discovering common ground. \n\n. . .\n\nO \n\nne of the most exciting personal experiences I've had \n\nof seeing a group harvest and share its own collective \n\nknowledge occurred in a rustic lodge high in the moun-\n\ntains above Denver, Colorado. I was with the advanced technolo-\n\ngies R&D group of a major global communications corporation. \n\nThey had gathered in this remote place to spend three days in a \n\nknowledge exchange. The goal? One of the scientists put it this \n\nway: "None of us knows what all of us knows. But we need to \n\nknow enough to be able to represent the whole of our R&D \n\nexpertise to any client we are working with in the system." \n\nThe three days we spent together were a real turning point for my \n\nown learning about how to make collective intelligence visible at \n\nincreasing levels of scale. We did a number of Café cycles over the first \n\ntwo days in which the scientists learned about client needs, shared \n\nknowledge about the variety of projects they were working on, and \n\nexplored future opportunities for leveraging their collective expertise. \n\nOn the last day, the group found ladders and scaffolding and \n\ncreated a huge matrix on the wall, with boxes for client groups \n\nacross the top and their various areas of R&D down the side. They \n\nbegan to fill in each of the matrix boxes with easel-size sheets that \n\nsummarized the understandings they had gleaned from the previ-\n\nous days' Café conversations. I could see new connections emerg-\n\ning and evolving as they built the cells of the matrix, helping each \n\nother fill in the gaps. It was like watching a conscious, living \n\norganism evolve and grow. \n\nI was deeply moved as I bore witness to the unfolding of their \n\neffort to make their own collective knowledge visible. Through \n\ntheir personal storytelling and multiple Café conversations, these \n\nscientists, engineers, and wizards in fiber optics had been able to \n\nco-evolve a mutual understanding of their shared professional \n\nworlds. They had demonstrated this understanding by literally \n\nconstructing and displaying it together in shared visual space. \n\nThere was a long silence as the sixty members took in the whole \n\npicture. Their heartfelt appreciation for each other and for their \n\n144 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nPerspectives & Observations\n\ncommon work was palpable as people acknowledged the wisdom \n\nand experience they had assembled. \n\nCafé Variations: Documenting and Displaying Collective Knowledge \n\nWorld Café hosts around the globe are discovering many innovative \n\nways to make visible the essence of their learnings from Café \n\ndialogues. In these efforts, we're building on the pioneering work \n\nof Bob Horn (1998), Michael Schrage (1990), David Sibbet (2003), \n\nand others who have recognized that spoken conversation is often \n\nephemeral. The absence of a verbal or visual memory means that cre-\n\native ideas, images, and insights that are generated in conversations \n\nare often distorted or lost. Therefore, harvesting and sharing discover-\n\nies in multiple mediums that can both create and reveal people's \n\nindividual and collective insights is an important part of being able \n\nto think and act together, especially in large-group settings. \n\nAs we saw in Bob Lengel's story and in my own experience with \n\nthe R&D scientists, depending on your purpose and desired out-\n\ncomes there are many ways to harvest and share discoveries as they \n\nunfold in World Café dialogues. Finn Voldtofte and his colleagues in \n\nDenmark have perhaps the most experience using different alterna-\n\ntives for harvesting and sharing discoveries. He shared with us some \n\nof the approaches he has tried. Here's a synthesis of his reflections: \n\nWe've done many things, of course. You can display the tablecloths and \n\nhave people gather around those. It works particularly well if people from \n\ndifferent tables give each other tours of their tablecloths. Then, later, the \n\ngroup can explore what's in the center of the conversation as a whole. \n\nIf there are a lot of tables, we may have each table turn in a large card \n\nor sticky note with one key idea that expresses the essence of what was \n\nimportant from their conversations. If the group size is smaller, each \n\nmember can contribute a card with the idea he or she feels is most essen-\n\ntial. Then we can post these on a wall or cluster related cards and make \n\na gallery tour from that. Other times we do a quick computer turnaround \n\nand publish a newspaper right on the spot, so participants can read the \n\nheadlines and build on them for further conversation or action planning. \n\nWe've even used video to create news stories about what people have learned. \n\nHarvest and Share Collective Discoveries 145 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .\n\nAt times, we have people find a partner and just walk around the room \n\ntalking about what they learned that day, or what idea had the most life \n\nfor them. Then they link up with two others and then the four link with \n\nfour more. With each linking up you have to get briefer and more focused \n\non the deeper themes. Very quickly you can get a sense of what everybody \n\nis hearing, especially if everyone is listening for the collective wisdom in \n\nthe larger group. \n\nAnother option is to have each Café table create an exhibition. You tell \n\npeople, "In one hour you will have the opening show for the exhibition. \n\nWe'll serve drinks and everyone can come to the gallery and see what \n\neach Café table or group of tables created in terms of the essence of what's \n\nbeen explored, what people learned, and possible action steps" (or what-\n\never you want as output). After they have each created their own exhibi-\n\ntion, people can take a tour of the other contributions in the gallery--but \n\nthey can also add their insights and put actual comments on each other's \n\ncontributions. It's like growing a living picture of the whole. \n\nYou can never actually capture the whole of what has occurred in a Café \n\ndialogue because each individual makes his or her own sense of it. How-\n\never, by inviting multiple perspectives on the essence of what's been dis-\n\ncovered, the group, together, can point toward the deeper knowledge of \n\nwhat's most important. \n\nOur colleague Ken Homer, who is fostering the development \n\nof the World Café community globally, shares another harvesting \n\napproach he's found useful in a wide variety of situations. At the \n\nUniversity of California, Santa Cruz, he and campus ombudsman \n\nLaurie McCann co-hosted a World Café for the IT services division \n\nto coordinate efforts to consolidate the operations of twenty-four \n\ndifferent departments into a single entity. The final Café round \n\nasked members: What is the most important question each of you has \n\nwhich, if explored, would allow you to move forward with your role in \n\nthe project? \n\nAs Ken recounts the day, "The final question generated several \n\ndozen areas for exploration. To make the collective wisdom in the \n\nroom visible to the group as a whole we gave everyone four self-\n\nadhesive dots. One had an X on it and the rest were plain. The \n\n146 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\ninstructions were to place the dot with the X on the one question \n\nthey most needed to explore before moving forward and use the \n\nremaining dots to identify the next most important ones. What \n\nemerged was a clear visual map of both the conversations needing \n\nthe most attention and the order in which they needed to be \n\nengaged to move forward." \n\nAt Saudi Aramco, Dan Walters used art and symbols in a \n\nfuturing session with company doctors, encouraging them to \n\nleave an empty oval in the middle of their Café tablecloths. At the \n\nend of their conversations, Dan asked each Café group to draw a \n\nsimple picture or image in the oval to express their best hopes for \n\nthe future. These core images became important data for setting \n\npriorities and determining future direction. \n\nIn Poland, the American Society for Quality was hosting a \n\nCafé at a conference on quality sponsored by the United Nations, \n\na situation where formality and hierarchy had often affected the \n\nfree sharing of ideas. In this case, Paul Borawski, the executive \n\ndirector of ASQ, used keypad polling technology to harvest \n\ninsights and stimulate further exploration. "We'd pose a Café \n\nquestion . . . for example, 'What do you think will be the future of \n\nquality in Poland?' While the groups were in their Café dialogues \n\nI put up several options along a continuum--such as 'It's strong,' \n\n'It's at risk,' and so on. Then, based on their sense of the discus-\n\nsion at their Café tables, we asked people to respond to the ques-\n\ntions and we posted the aggregated results of the anonymous poll \n\nfor the whole group to see. The moment they saw the varied \n\nresults it completely opened the conversation in the large group. \n\nPeople asked questions, listened to insights from others, and got \n\nthe benefit of seeing how their answer fit with the sense of the \n\nmajority of people in the room." \n\nDiscovering creative ways to synthesize discoveries and dis-\n\nplay Café insights in order to help participants notice the deeper \n\nconnections between the parts and the whole is a learning edge in \n\nour work. We believe this is an important arena to consider for all \n\nof us who host conversations that matter, whether or not they \n\ntake place in a Café format. \n\nHarvest and Share Collective Discoveries 147 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n(continued on p. 149)\n\nS T O RY \n\nCONNECTING THE PARTS AND THE WHOLE: THE FINANCIAL PLANNING ASSOCIATION \n\nAs Told By \n\nKim Porto and Sean Walters \n\nOne of the more challenging aspects of the World Café is \n\nthe whole-group synthesis and conversation that occurs \n\nafter the Café rounds. We stumbled onto what we feel is one of the best ways of keeping people's energy and contribution level high when we invented our own version of the Gallery Walks. Here's how we do it. We create a three- or four-round Café on a particular topic. In round one we ask tables to come up with their own overarching question, which, if answered, could make a difference to the situation under consideration. That question gets written on a large piece of heavy paper that is folded in half as an easily visible "tent card" and becomes the \n\ncore question for the next two or three rounds of Café dialogue at that table. One host stays throughout all rounds as the steward for his or her particular table \n\nquestion. At the end of round one the other three people travel to new tables, choosing tables with a new question that interests them. In each round, people write personal insights and thoughts about their table's question on large individual sticky notes that they keep with them as they move to subsequent rounds. \n\nThe table host/question steward continues to make notes or drawings related to that table's question on the Café tablecloth. During round two or three, the overall Café host or hosts walk around the room and write down the question that's on each table's tent card. We create a flip chart page for each one of these questions. If several of the table questions are the same, we put them on a single flip chart page, and if several are simi-lar or related we post them near each other. By posting the questions around the room, everyone can begin to see the emerging outlines of the larger patterns of inquiry. \n\nAt the conclusion of the last round, rather than giving a formal break (which tends to disperse the energy of the group) we give folks ten minutes to place their individual sticky notes next to the questions posted on the flip charts around the room. This gives people the opportunity to add their insights, ideas, inspirations, and what-if's to the questions they were part of exploring as well as any of the others posted around the room. People get pretty excited as they look at each other's contributions. It starts a whole new round of little "buzz groups"--people in two's or three's commenting on the ideas they are seeing posted during the Gallery Walk. \n\n148 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nKim Porto and Sean Walters \n\nof the Financial Planning \n\nAssociation have discovered \n\nan extremely useful approach \n\nfor harvesting and sharing \n\nindividual and collective dis-\n\ncoveries. It's one that can be \n\neffective in a wide variety of \n\nsituations.\n\nHosting the Whole-Group Conversation \n\nThe World Café is designed to foster collective knowledge-sharing \n\nand knowledge creation; no individual is as likely to make these \n\ndiscoveries alone. Yet paradoxically, emerging insights can only be \n\nvoiced through individual expression. How then can members \n\nshare collective insights without resorting to traditional report-\n\nouts that rarely reflect the liveliness or depth of the smaller group \n\nconversations? \n\nThe conversation of the whole--which comes at the end of a \n\nsequence of Café rounds--is a key element in the harvesting and \n\nsharing of collective discoveries. Designing and facilitating this \n\nconversation requires special artistry and care. The goal is to \n\ncontinue to nurture a spirit of authentic dialogue while creating \n\nthe opportunity to evoke collective insight in the larger group. \n\nThe approach you choose for designing the whole-group \n\nconversation depends on the Café's purpose and the ways that \n\nHarvest and Share Collective Discoveries 149 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWe continue by hosting the whole-group conversation, focusing on key ideas or insights. We start with one table question from the flip charts that intuitively seems to have the most energy and interest, or where there were a number of tables with the same or related questions. We continue by asking people to look among the questions for themes and patterns, new opportunities for action, or areas that need further discussion. Sometimes we ask people to share what they still want to explore that didn't get addressed, in order to ensure that all voices are heard. \n\nOne of the aspects that we think makes this particular approach to whole-group synthesis so engaging and useful is that people have control over what they contribute. They are not relying on the table host to summarize what they said, nor are they relying on a scribe to capture their words. Each person gets the opportunity (and has the respon-sibility) to contribute exactly what is most meaningful to him or her, and put it exactly where he or she feels it best fits in the overall synthesis. It also creates a tangible record of the conversation, which can be used for follow-up, action planning, priority setting, and other tasks, ensuring that nothing important will be lost. \n\n. . .\n\nsharing discoveries can be most useful to your desired outcomes. \n\nWe've found it generally helpful to pose catalytic questions that \n\ncan focus the whole-group synthesis. You'll find examples of such \n\nquestions in chapter 10. In addition, to help people connect into \n\nthe larger field of collective thought, you can have \n\nthem imagine that the emerging conversation is a \n\nball of yarn that is being passed from one person \n\nto the next as people around the room focus on \n\nthe essence of the dialogue, share personal reflec-\n\ntions, and bring closure to the conversation. Ask \n\nmembers to listen closely to each person's contri-\n\nbution, rising to share their reflection if they \n\nsense it is connected to the person's before them \n\nin some way. This helps to weave a web of connected thought \n\nthroughout the room. When one thread is complete, invite others \n\nto offer a new thread into the weave of ideas. \n\nAt other times, you might begin by asking everyone to con-\n\nsider what's most essential from the previous conversations. Ask \n\nthem to imagine themselves as a system thinking together-- \n\nlistening for deeper wisdom before beginning to share insights \n\nand discoveries. These two simple approaches tend to bring more \n\ncoherence to the large-group exploration. People begin to sense \n\nthemes, patterns, and key ideas unfolding in the larger group, \n\nwhile still being free to offer to offer divergent views that spark \n\nunexpected possibilities. \n\nUsing a graphic recorder can also make a big difference in \n\nhelping the group gain a greater sense of the whole as it unfolds \n\nfrom people's individual comments. After taking a gallery tour of \n\nthe tablecloths or using another approach to display key ideas, \n\nthis visual specialist records people's reflections in words and pic-\n\ntures on large wall murals or rolling blackboards. This "tablecloth \n\nin the center of the room" enables members to see the larger \n\npicture. Using this type of visual recording reveals how key ideas \n\nconnect with one another, and enables people to think more \n\nsystemically. See "Resources and Connections" and "Illustration \n\n150 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWe've found it generally \n\nhelpful to pose catalytic \n\nquestions that can focus \n\nthe whole-group synthesis\n\nCredits," at the back of the book, to learn how to contact special-\n\nists in visual language. \n\nWhether you use a graphics professional or not, we've found \n\nthat it's important for the host to allow a few minutes of silence \n\nfor individual reflection and note-taking prior to opening the \n\nwhole-group conversation. In addition, ask people to speak \n\npersonally (and briefly) about what's at the center of the collective \n\nconversations of which they've been a part. This enables what \n\nSwedish Café host Bo Gyllenpalm calls a "shared understanding \n\nof individual meanings" to emerge, revealing key facets of the \n\nconversational web while not requiring agreement on a single \n\nshared meaning in order for multiple perspectives to be included \n\nin later priority setting or action planning. In addition, encourag-\n\ning the contribution of key personal meanings enables not only \n\n"head knowledge" but also "heart wisdom" to be revealed. This \n\nsimple shift sidesteps the traditional roles of group reporter or \n\nofficial representative that are so prevalent (and often deadly) in \n\nother modes of large-group report-backs. \n\nHelping Insight Travel \n\nYvon Bastien poses an important and provocative question related \n\nto this exploration: How does insight travel? Bo Gyl-\n\nlenpalm has been especially innovative in consider-\n\ning interesting ways to help insight travel to those \n\nwho are not part of the original dialogue. He tells one story of sev-\n\neral large-scale strategy conversations with a major Swedish bank. \n\nThe regional director told his local managers they would receive \n\noverhead copies of the colorful murals created throughout their \n\nCafé sessions. "Then something very interesting occurred that we \n\nhadn't anticipated," Bo says. "The local managers told us that after \n\ntraditional strategy meetings they usually receive pre-formatted \n\nPowerPoint slides with bullet points from the formal strategy pre-\n\nsentations. They were required to show them to their employees. \n\nThese are often seen as obligatory presentations, with little engage-\n\nment and enthusiasm." Bo smiled as he added, "But when they \n\nreceived the colorful overheads with the murals from their own \n\nHarvest and Share Collective Discoveries 151 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nHow does insight travel?\n\nknowledge harvesting, they simply couldn't make a dry presenta-\n\ntion like before. They had to tell a story! When local managers used \n\nthe storytelling approach to share what had happened at the meet-\n\ning, people became immediately engaged in a lively dialogue about \n\nwhat the different images and key words meant and what the \n\nimplications were for their own work." \n\nStorybooks, visual reports, CDs, or DVDs that share the core \n\nquestions, flow of conversation, and outcomes from a Café \n\ndialogue can also be used as conversational "memory joggers" \n\nfor follow-up and implementation planning. In addition, these \n\ncreative syntheses of key insights can also be used to spread the \n\nword and engender larger networks of conversation. Although \n\nthese approaches to helping insight travel rarely convey the full \n\nexcitement and discoveries of the original Café dialogue, they can \n\nbe used effectively as process tools to stimulate new rounds of \n\nconversation and action--whether in a Café format or not. \n\nOur Learning Edges \n\nDiscovering the best ways to reveal and utilize the collective \n\ninsights and stories that emerge in a World Café dialogue has \n\nunearthed a deeper set of challenges and questions we're still in \n\nthe process of exploring. For example, Western learning and \n\neducational approaches have been shaped by an individualistic \n\nmindset. In both the United States and abroad, we're just begin-\n\nning to employ what Michael Schrage (1990) calls "convivial \n\ntools"--as simple as Café tablecloths and graphic recording or as \n\nsophisticated as groupware and other collaborative technology \n\ntools--that enable us to make collective knowledge visible. \n\nIt's essential to discover innovative ways to link individual \n\nand collective intelligence in order to foster more holistic and \n\nsystemic understandings of critical issues in our organizations \n\nand communities. On this subject, Finn Voldtofte has raised \n\nprovocative questions for leaders to consider: "How does a collec-\n\ntive actually learn to think together? Are there different principles \n\nunderlying the development of collective insight versus the development \n\nof individual insight alone?" \n\n152 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nOther Café hosts have built on this inquiry by asking: "What \n\napproaches can we use to make collective insight both visible and \n\nactionable at increasing levels of scale?" It does appear that to be \n\neffective we need to consider alternatives to the linear reporting \n\nmodalities--the analytical reports and PowerPoint presentations \n\nthat we're all too familiar with in traditional organizational and \n\ncommunity settings. We're discovering that innovative method-\n\nologies like interactive graphics and other forms of visual \n\nlanguage, theater, poetry, artistic expression, and interactive tech-\n\nnology tools can play powerful roles in enhancing our capacities \n\nfor thinking together about complex issues. \n\nWe're making great progress in learning how best to access, \n\nharvest, and share insights and stories from World Café conversa-\n\ntions, with their focus on evoking collaborative versus solely indi-\n\nvidual intelligence. We are excited by the creative experiments that \n\nare happening around the world and the unique contribution the \n\nWorld Café is making to this inquiry. We'd love to learn from your \n\nexperiences as you begin to experiment with hosting conversations \n\nthat matter in your own organizations and communities as well. \n\nHarvest and Share Collective Discoveries 153 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nQuestions for Reflection \n\nWhat are some of the most effective or innovative ways you've seen to enhance collective learning and discovery by making collective knowledge more visible to the group as a whole? \n\nFrom your perspective and experience, how does insight travel and spread most effectively? What practical role can harvesting and sharing collective discoveries play in enhancing mutual intelligence and carrying key discover-ies forward? \n\nConsider an upcoming gathering or conversation you will be a part of. What creative ways for harvesting, docu-menting, and sharing discoveries can be most useful for (a) the group at the event and (b) carrying the insights forward to other constituencies?\n\n154 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nT E N \n\nHosting is an attitude as much as an activity. \n\n--Carlos Mota Margain, World Café host \n\nGuiding the Café Process: The Art of Hosting\n\n155 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat i f host ing means \n\nwelcoming a l l that ar r ives?\n\nWORKING ON BEHALF OF THEIR SISTERS: AFRICAN WOMEN WITH DISABILITIES \n\nAs Told By \n\nMarianne "Mille" Böjer \n\nThat was my excited response when I heard that thirty-five to forty women from across Africa were coming to my town, Johan-nesburg, to inspire each other, give each other ideas and tools for their work, and set up a new African network of women with disabilities focused on reproductive health and HIV/AIDS. I got the news from my colleague Marianne Knuth that the Danish Council of Associations of Disabled People was seeking a World Café host in South Africa to facilitate the meeting. The group was not interested in creating a new formal organization or interna-tional institution. Instead, they wanted to create a network that could work through their existing organizations and would get its energy from the group's clarity of purpose, creative ideas, and strong personal relationships. \n\nSoon I was corresponding with Lena Nielsen in Denmark and Kirsten Nielsen in Uganda, the two Danish partners involved in the project, as well as representatives from their South African counterpart, Disabled People of South Africa (DPSA), to design the program. As I learned more, it became apparent that this group would present unique challenges in adapting the Café \n\nprocess to their particular needs. The group would be composed entirely of women with disabilities. A good number were blind or visually impaired, an equally high number were physically disabled (dependent on crutches or wheelchairs), and two were deaf. \n\nI'd never in my life worked in this kind of situation! I e-mailed Lena and asked if she was sure they wanted to use the World Café for this gathering. Café conversations involve a lot of moving around and I usually hang things all around the room as the meeting progresses to visualize the group's journey as it unfolds. Would all this be possible with this group? \n\nAlthough Lena hadn't experienced the World Café herself, she knew the members wanted to use a process that "put the participants' ideas in the center" and this was what she had heard was powerful about the World Café. Although still a bit nervous, I started getting excited by the challenge. I was also inspired by the possibilities of what I might learn personally about hosting and being creative in figuring out how to apply the World \n\nS T O RY \n\n156 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nMille Böjer is one of the \n\nfounders of the Pioneers of \n\nChange, a global learning \n\ncommunity of committed \n\nchangemakers in their mid-\n\ntwenties to early thirties. \n\nThese young leaders are \n\nbringing leading-edge orga-\n\nnizational, community, and \n\nleadership development pro-\n\ncesses to over seventy coun-\n\ntries where the network has \n\nmembers. The art of hosting \n\nlies at the heart of what \n\nmakes the World Café \n\nprocess successful. This is \n\nthe story of Mille's discover-\n\nies about herself as a host in \n\na unique Café situation in \n\nSouth Africa. \n\n"I'm in!"\n\nGuiding the Café Process 157 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nCafé principles in this unique situation. Sure enough, as the weeklong workshop unfolded, it turned out to be one of the most profound experiences of my life. \n\nSince this gathering would be our first time together as a network of women with dis-abilities, we agreed at the outset to try as much as possible to practice network approaches in how we organized and hosted the meeting. For example, although participants had pre-pared a number of presentations, these were used to stimulate more interactive conversations in multiple small groups. We also invited each woman to share a question that mattered to her during her personal introduction, so the participants' own web of concerns could be at the center of the meeting. \n\nEach afternoon we held a World Café around the topic of the day. We explored the strengths and weaknesses of various disabilities movements in the participants' countries, explored their unique African context, and came up with the vision for their network. One day was devoted to country reports. But instead of listening to fifteen presentations in a ple-nary session, we discussed them at small Café tables and drew out the common trends, pat-terns, and questions that cut across them all. \n\nThe women embraced the Café process. I noticed that their disabilities gave us all a rea-son for physical contact, which helped build relationships. I was constantly touching the women--touching the blind to let them know I was there, touching the physically disabled to aid their mobility. For the deaf, we had to work a bit more slowly so that the translators could keep up. And to accommodate everyone's pace of movement, we took longer to move between tables. \n\nAs the Café host, whenever someone would enter the room, I would acknowledge her presence and have her introduce herself so those who were blind could all know who was there and could hear where the newcomer was located in the room. I looked each person in the eye. I learned their names from day one. When I wrote something or drew some-thing on the wall, I would describe what was there. I described the room setup every time it changed, and let them know if they should expect to come back to a room that was arranged differently. \n\nAfter three days of sharing their struggles and learning about the alarming reality of reproductive health among African women with disabilities, we moved into visioning the future of their network and designing its structure--the purpose and principles, roles and relationships, and communication flows. Since we had been working with a networked process in the Café conversations throughout the week, it was easier for them to envision the kind of organization that could bring the fundamental network idea to life on a larger scale, while not losing the personal bonds and connectedness that the World Café had helped to establish. \n\nIt was very gratifying to spend five days with real, sincere women--disabled women working on behalf of their sisters who are also disabled. The feeling was so different from typical conferences, such as the one I had facilitated the month before, where the more the\n\n158 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nparticipants talked about poverty, the further away the actual poor seemed. Here, the more the women shared their personal stories and experiences within the weave of the conversa-tions, the more present others who shared their situation seemed to be. Although I haven't seen many of the Café members recently, they are still in my heart, and from e-mail contacts I know the network continues. \n\nMy main lessons from this experience as a Café host were at the level of my "being," in contrast to specific tools. It seems that hosting is really about being yourself rather than just playing a formal role. The participants had a sense of humor and I had the feeling that they were being themselves. As I felt that, it created a space for me, as the host, to be more of myself, which I think encouraged them to be more of themselves. In a way, I was helping to host a mutual hosting! \n\nI also learned how to be attentive in ways I hadn't experienced before. The World Café process creates a web of connections among the participants that generates collective wis-dom. But beyond that, I discovered that the host can also play a key role in connecting peo-ple at many levels as the meeting unfolds. This can make a big difference in the group's ability to get in touch with its own wisdom. \n\nOne way this happens is through constant inclusion. I paid close attention to everyone's needs because they were so explicit, watching to see if somebody was outside the process or somehow not included, and then inviting her in and encouraging her contribution. This an important job for the host, even in more conventional situations. \n\nI also think that it helps when the host introduces the Café with a lot of confidence. It can be challenging when people aren't familiar with what's going to happen and they start questioning the framework and assumptions, so you need to be clear when you introduce the principles and the process. It's also important that the host be available for people in a spirit of service. Don't assume that it's easy and that the conversations are all going to flow immediately, but move around and check to see if your help is needed. \n\nIt might seem as if the hosting practices I learned at this meeting are not directly applicable to a typical group of people who don't have disabilities. But at a deeper level I believe they apply just as much--it's about us as hosts having the courage and attention to practice them. \n\n. . .\n\nI \n\n've often wondered what it is about the World Café process \n\nand principles that enables those who have even a modicum \n\nof leadership, facilitation, or group skills to be successful in \n\nhosting Café-style dialogues, often with very large groups. We're \n\ndiscovering from our "research in action" over the last decade that \n\ncreatively employing the seven World Café design principles, \n\ncoupled with introducing Café etiquette and assumptions as the \n\ndialogue begins (see the following section, "Hosting a World \n\nCafé"), tends to foster the enabling conditions for collaborative \n\nconversation while mitigating many of the dysfunctional behav-\n\niors often found in group settings. \n\nHowever, it is important to emphasize that the "magic" of \n\nCafé conversations at their best doesn't always occur. Determining \n\nthat a World Café conversation is the appropriate mode for your \n\ngroup (see the following section, "Decide If a World Café Gather-\n\ning Is Appropriate for Your Situation"), planning enough time for \n\nthe conversation, and working with the seven Café design princi-\n\nples as an integrated set makes accessing collective intelligence \n\nmore likely. \n\nHosting great conversations--whether Café dialogues or \n\nnot--is an art that requires personal awareness and attention. \n\nToke Møller, who has introduced hundreds of people to the art of \n\nhosting both in his native Denmark and internationally, observes \n\nthat "you cannot host a Café or any deep learning space without \n\nbeing fully present yourself. It's about real-life practice, not theory. \n\nSooner or later, something will happen that will take you past \n\nyour limits of understanding and create a new possibility. But \n\nentering into that new space requires going beyond your fear and \n\ncoming into a calm place where you, as the host, can see what \n\nyou need to do in the present moment." \n\nFinn Voldtofte, another Danish World Café pioneer, points \n\nout, "As the host, I'm putting my own attention on the wholeness \n\nthat is emerging in the spaces in between all the participants. I'm \n\nalways seeking creative ways for the members to make visible \n\nGuiding the Café Process 159 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nPerspectives & Observations\n\nwhat's emerging in the middle of their own table and what's \n\nemerging as their conversations connect and weave together." \n\nToke adds that hosting involves an understanding of the mul-\n\ntiple systems embedded in World Café conversations. "The system \n\nof knowledge-sharing, the system of working with the questions, \n\nthe system of relationships--all need to unfold so that they can \n\ncome alive. When you can be open with great curiosity to how \n\nthings are actually unfolding, you gain access to your best contri-\n\nbution. Good hosting is a privilege, not a job. It's very important \n\nleadership work." \n\nJan Hein Nielsen, a friend and colleague of Toke's, echoes \n\nthese ideas but also stresses the importance of trust: "The host \n\nmust trust herself and her capacity to open up space for everyone \n\nto give their best. She needs to trust that the participants have the \n\ncapability and willingness to enter a learning field together. The \n\nparticipants also need to trust that they will be hosted in such a \n\nway that learning can enter, that action can enter, that anything \n\nthat they really want to happen can enter the room. If trust is not \n\non the menu, hosting cannot take place. It will be something else, \n\nbut it will not be hosting." \n\nAs a host, one invites people into a creative space--a space of \n\n"not knowing." It must be open, but not intrusive, so that people \n\nfeel comfortable and able to unfold their best thinking. As \n\nMonica Nissén, another Danish colleague, describes it, "If the \n\nfield of discovery is open, emergence can happen and you need to \n\nbe prepared to be surprised. You cannot know exactly what will \n\ncome out of it. You are sometimes standing on \n\nthe edge right there with everyone else." \n\nMost of us are not aware of how a good host \n\ncultivates the open field that Monica describes \n\nunless we stop to ask ourselves, What does a host \n\ndo that makes me feel welcome? And that question \n\nis perhaps the doorway to discovering the essence of Café hosting. \n\nWhen we speak of hosting, we're not referring to traditional facili-\n\ntation or leading. Instead, by engaging the Café principles you are \n\ncreating the conditions within which members can self-organize \n\n160 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat does a host do that \n\nmakes me feel welcome?\n\nto host each other at their individual tables around questions \n\nthey care about. \n\nAs a conversation host, ask yourself: What can I do to make \n\nwhomever I am with feel physically comfortable, emotionally safe, and \n\nintellectually challenged? How can I support members in discovering a \n\ndeeper understanding and appreciation--for each other and for the \n\nquestions we're exploring? How can I engage the Café participants \n\nthemselves in hosting each other and in discovering the magic in the \n\nmiddle of their conversations? Whatever answers you get and how-\n\never they reveal themselves, begin to experiment with putting \n\nthem into practice. Leave aside the techniques that don't work \n\nand refine the ones that seem most effective. Above all, keep ask-\n\ning yourself the questions and integrate your answers into your \n\nhosting practice. You'll soon discover the way of hosting that is \n\nmost natural for you. \n\nThe remainder of this chapter is designed to be used, with \n\nacknowledgment, as a World Café hosting guide independently of \n\nthe rest of the book. This hosting guide is not a recipe. It simply \n\nprovides the initial ingredients to cook up creative conversations, \n\nbased on your unique needs and situation. You will find many \n\nother practical examples and tips in other chapters of this book as \n\nwell as on the World Café Web site. \n\nGuiding the Café Process 161 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThe World Café Hosting Guide \n\nAdvance Preparation: Convening a World Café \n\nWhen you convene and host a Café conversation, you can use your \n\npersonal imagination and have great fun! Although the traditional \n\nsetup of café-like tables and flowers creates a special ambiance, it is \n\nby no means the only way to go. We know of exciting World Café \n\ndesigns in which people have moved among conversation clusters \n\nin living rooms, rotated between large trees in a forest, or shifted \n\nplaces among several minivans on a learning journey. Whatever \n\nspecific form you choose, engaging the seven Café principles in \n\ncombination increases the likelihood of active engagement, authen-\n\ntic dialogue, and constructive possibilities for action. Use whatever \n\nspace you have available, be creative with the setup and supplies, \n\npay attention to the seven design principles, and use your imagina-\n\ntion to help the World Café achieve your purpose. Be the best host \n\nyou can, and then trust the process. \n\nDecide If a World Café Gathering Is Appropriate for Your Situation \n\nDesigning with the World Café principles in mind (see p. 174) \n\nallows you to adapt and engage in conversations that matter in \n\nalmost any circumstance. Cafés have been designed for sessions as \n\nshort as ninety minutes as well as for conferences lasting several \n\ndays. A Café can stand alone or serve as part of a larger meeting. \n\nWorld Café conversations are especially useful for these purposes and in these circumstances: \n\n◗ For sharing knowledge, stimulating innovative thinking, build-ing community, and exploring possibilities around real-life issues and questions. \n\n◗ For conducting an in-depth exploration of key challenges and opportunities. \n\n◗ For engaging people who are meeting for the first time in authentic conversation. \n\n◗ For deepening relationships and mutual ownership of outcomes in an existing group. \n\n◗ For creating a meaningful interaction between a speaker and the audience. \n\n162 The World Café Hosting Guide \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThe World Café Hosting Guide 163 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\n◗ When the group is larger than twelve (we've hosted as many as twelve hundred) and you want each person to have the opportu-nity to contribute. The World Café is especially suitable for con-necting the intimacy of small-group dialogue with the excitement and fun of larger-group participation and learning. \n\n◗ When you have a minimum of one and a half hours for the Café (two hours is much better). Some Cafés have spanned several days or become part of a regular meeting infrastructure. \n\nThe World Café is not an optimal choice under these circumstances: \n\n◗ You are driving toward an already determined solution or answer. \n\n◗ You want to convey only one-way information. \n\n◗ You are making detailed implementation plans and assignments. \n\n◗ You have less than one and a half hours for the Café. \n\n◗ You are working with a highly polarized, explosive situation. (Hosting a World Café in this setting requires highly skilled facilitation.) \n\n◗ You have a group smaller than twelve. In that case, consider a traditional dialogue circle, council, or other approach to foster-ing authentic conversation. \n\nSet the Context \n\nOnce you've determined that a Café conversation is appropriate \n\nfor your circumstances, you'll need to clarify the context. This \n\nmeans paying attention to the three Ps: purpose, participants, and \n\nparameters. \n\n◗ Determine clearly your purpose for bringing people together, along with the best possibilities or outcomes you can see emerg-ing from your Café. Once you know your Café's purpose, name it in a way that reflects that purpose. For example: Leadership Café, Knowledge Café, Community Café, Discovery Café, Anniversary Café, and so on. \n\n◗ Identify the participants who need to be included. Diversity of thought yields richer insights and discoveries. \n\n◗ Take into account the parameters with which you are working (time, money, venue, and so on). See if you need to stretch the parameters in order to achieve your purpose.\n\nCreate Hospitable Space \n\nCafé hosts around the world emphasize the power and impor-\n\ntance of creating a welcoming environment--one that feels com-\n\nfortable, safe, and inviting for people to be themselves. In \n\nparticular, consider how your invitation and your physical setup \n\ncontribute to creating a welcoming atmosphere. \n\nIn your invitation, pose an initial question or theme that you \n\nbelieve is important to those you've invited. Choose a question \n\nthat arouses curiosity and opens the way for more conversation. \n\nCraft your invitation to convey that members can expect to have \n\nfun, be engaged, and learn new things. When sending a written \n\ninvitation, find ways to make it stand out from the usual e-mail \n\nor written correspondence by making it informal, creative, per-\n\nsonal, and visually interesting. \n\nWhether you are convening several dozen or several hundred \n\npeople, your guests should notice immediately that this is no \n\nordinary meeting. Arrange \n\nthe room to be as \n\nwelcoming as possible. \n\nHave music playing when \n\npeople arrive. Natural \n\nlight and an outdoor view \n\nare always inviting. If \n\nyour room doesn't have \n\nwindows, bring in plants \n\nand greenery to enliven it. \n\nYou can quickly trans-\n\nform a dull meeting room \n\ninto a welcoming space \n\nby hanging pictures or posters on the walls. Hospitality and com-\n\nmunity thrive on food and refreshments; have snacks and bever-\n\nages available throughout the gathering. \n\nPlease don't limit yourself with these suggestions. Look at the \n\nroom setup and supplies list near the end of this chapter. Then \n\nuse your own imagination and creativity to create a setting that par-\n\nticipants will enjoy. \n\n164 The World Café Hosting Guide \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nCreating a Café Environment\n\nExplore Questions That Matter \n\nFinding and framing relevant questions that open the way for \n\ngreat conversations is an area where careful thought and attention \n\ncan produce profound results. Your Café may focus on exploring \n\na single question, or you may develop several lines of inquiry to \n\nsupport an emergent discovery process through successive rounds \n\nof dialogue. In many cases, Café conversations are as much about \n\ndiscovering and exploring powerful questions as they are about \n\nfinding immediate solutions. The question or questions you \n\nchoose or that participants discover during a Café conversation \n\nare critical to its success. As Eric Vogt of the International Corpo-\n\nrate Learning Association points out, a powerful question has \n\nthese characteristics: \n\n◗ It is simple and clear. \n\n◗ It is thought-provoking. \n\n◗ It generates energy. \n\n◗ It focuses inquiry. \n\n◗ It surfaces assumptions. \n\n◗ It opens new possibilities. \n\nExperienced Café hosts recommend posing open-ended \n\nquestions--the kind that invite exploration. Good questions \n\nneed not imply immediate action steps or problem solving. They \n\ninvite inquiry and discovery rather than advocacy and advantage. \n\nWe've included a list of generative questions near the end of this \n\nchapter (see p. 173) that can help you craft your own questions \n\nfor exploration. You'll know you have a good question when it \n\ncontinues to surface new ideas and possibilities. Test questions \n\nbefore using them. Bounce them off trusted friends or colleagues \n\nwho will be participating in the Café to see if they sustain inter-\n\nest and energy. If you are featuring a speaker, involve that person \n\nin helping to create questions that, if explored, could make a dif-\n\nference to the real-life concerns of those attending. \n\nThe World Café Hosting Guide 165 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nAt the Event: Hosting a World Café \n\nA World Café generally consists of three rounds of progressive \n\nconversation lasting approximately twenty to thirty minutes each, \n\nfollowed by a dialogue among the whole group. Rounds have \n\ngone longer, but people often feel rushed in less than a twenty-\n\nminute round. The number and length of rounds prior to the \n\nwhole-group dialogue will depend on your focus and intent. Feel \n\nfree to experiment. \n\nThe job of the overall Café host (or the hosting team at larger \n\ngatherings) begins with welcoming people as they arrive, directing \n\nthem to the refreshments, inviting them to their seats, and \n\nanswering any logistical questions before the first round starts. \n\nOnce everyone is seated, explain the purpose and the logistics of \n\nthe Café. Let people know that they will be moving from table to \n\ntable and that the end of a round may come when they are in the \n\nmiddle of an intense conversation, just as happens in life. It is \n\nnatural to feel some resistance to the interruption, but they can \n\npick up the conversation again at the next table. Explain that \n\nwhen a round ends one person will remain behind as the host \n\nof that table, and the other people will travel to new tables to sit \n\nwith a different mix of people. \n\nThe job of the individual table host is to engage in the con-\n\nversation as a participant and steward, not as a formal facilitator. \n\nThe added role of this person is to share the essence of the con-\n\nversation for the guests who arrive for the next round. Everyone \n\nat the table is responsible for supporting the host in taking notes, \n\nsummarizing key ideas, and if so moved, making drawings that \n\nreflect interesting thoughts and insights as they unfold. This \n\n"table recording" helps the host do the best job possible in con-\n\nveying to new members the key ideas that have emerged. Be sure \n\nto encourage people to write, draw, or doodle on the tablecloths in the \n\nmidst of their conversations. Often these tablecloth drawings will \n\ncontain remarkable notes, and they help visual learners link ideas. \n\nMembers who will be traveling to a new table should bring with \n\nthem the key ideas, themes, and questions from their last round \n\nto seed their upcoming conversation. \n\n166 The World Café Hosting Guide \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nBefore beginning the first round, it is helpful \n\nto introduce the Café assumptions and Café eti-\n\nquette. You can display our graphics, shown here, \n\non overheads, post them on easel sheets, or dis-\n\ntribute them on cards at each table. Or use your \n\nown illustration style. Being clear about basic Café \n\nassumptions and Café etiquette orients members \n\nto underlying beliefs and personal behaviors that \n\nare useful in supporting constructive dialogue \n\nwithout being heavy-handed about "shoulds" and \n\n"shouldn'ts." Then allow time to answer any final \n\nquestions prior to commencing the Café dialogue. \n\nYou are now ready to pose the question for the \n\nfirst round of conversation. Write the question-- \n\neven if it's a question that asks people to discover \n\ntheir own question or questions--on flip charts or \n\noverheads and if necessary make extra copies to \n\npost around the room or distribute on cards to \n\neach table. Very often what people think they \n\nheard is different from what you thought you said. \n\nA visual reference can avoid a lot of confusion. \n\nHelp clarify the question, if requested, but avoid \n\nproviding answers or directing the conversation. \n\nEncourage everyone to share their ideas and \n\nperspectives freely, and acknowledge that some \n\npeople's special contribution may be their pres-\n\nence as attentive listeners. Some Café hosts prefer \n\nto ask people to identify the table host at the start \n\nof the round, while some opt for doing it at the \n\nend. Whichever one you choose, make sure that a \n\nhost is chosen before people change tables. \n\nUpon completing each round of conversation, let people \n\nknow in a gentle way that it's time to move for the next round. \n\nMany hosts use a raised hand that signals others in the room to \n\nfall silent and raise theirs as well, marking the end of a round. \n\nEncourage the departing travelers from each table to find new \n\nThe World Café Hosting Guide 167 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .\n\ntables and different people to sit with. Ask hosts to welcome their \n\nnew guests. Remind everyone that when they arrive at their new \n\ntable, they should briefly introduce themselves before the table \n\nhost shares the essence of the conversation from the previous \n\nround. Then the travelers add connections and ideas from the \n\nconversations at their previous tables. Ask everyone to listen care-\n\nfully and build on each other's contributions. Let people know if \n\nthere is a new question for this round, and make sure it is posted \n\nwhere it can easily be seen. \n\nAt times people will participate in the first round, go traveling \n\nfor the second, and then return to their home table for a final \n\nround of synthesis. At other times, members will continue travel-\n\ning for several rounds while the host stays as the ongoing steward \n\nof the evolving conversation and insights at their table. On occa-\n\nsion, people will simply travel for a very brief listening tour to hear \n\nwhat's being explored at other tables prior to returning to their \n\nhome table to both connect common threads and introduce \n\ndiverse perspectives. The variations depend on your purpose, as the \n\nmany stories and examples throughout this book demonstrate. \n\nEncourage Everyone's Contribution \n\nOne of the reasons for having only four or five people per table is \n\nto enable each voice to be heard. People who are hesitant to speak \n\nin a large group often offer rich and exciting insights in a more \n\nintimate Café setting. At most Café gatherings, once the question is \n\nposed people are encouraged simply to jump into the conversation \n\nand begin to explore ideas. Most of the time this works well. \n\nHowever, we have found it often helpful to have a talking \n\nobject on the tables to help ensure that no single participant takes \n\nover the group's airtime. Originally used by indigenous peoples, a \n\ntalking object can be a stick, a stone, a marker, a saltshaker-- \n\nalmost any object that can be passed among the people at the \n\ntable. Ask people to pick up the talking piece when they are ready \n\nto speak, and return it to the center of the table when they are \n\ndone. The talking piece can also be passed around the circle, or \n\nthe person who begins can offer it as a gift to whomever he \n\nchooses, though people have the opportunity to pass if they wish. \n\n168 The World Café Hosting Guide \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nAs the host, you can introduce the use of the talking piece as the \n\nCafé begins or at any appropriate point in the process where you \n\nsense that deep listening and "slowing down the action" for more \n\nthoughtful engagement may be needed. There are two aspects to \n\nthe talking object that encourage helpful member participation. \n\nWhoever is holding it is encouraged express his or her thoughts as \n\nclearly and briefly as possible. Whoever is not holding the talking \n\npiece is asked to listen with respect, appreciating the other's per-\n\nspective as a part of the larger picture. \n\nAs host, you can assess what combination of free-flowing \n\nexchange and reflective listening to other members using the talk-\n\ning piece will work best. If you anticipate intense emotions or dif-\n\nferences of opinion, it's often helpful to begin with a talking piece \n\nand then move to a more free-flowing dialogue. \n\nCross-Pollinate and Connect Diverse Perspectives \n\nMoving among tables, talking with new people, contributing your \n\nthinking, and linking the essence of your discoveries to ever-\n\nwidening circles of thought are hallmarks of the World Café. \n\nPatterns emerge, additional perspectives surface, and surprising \n\nThe World Café Hosting Guide 169 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nKen Homer, World Café host and Webmaster \n\nCafés often surface differences of opinion and understanding; this is part of their ability to generate new insights. However, differences can foster either energy and excitement or anxiety and dissen-sion. If you anticipate difficulties or discover that a conversation seems to be getting really stuck, then in an upcoming round you might encourage participants to use the following three statements as their dialogue unfolds. \n\n◗ What I heard you say that I appreciated is . . . \n\n◗ What I heard that challenged my thinking is . . . \n\n◗ To better understand your perspective I'd like to ask you . . . \n\nUsing this simple discipline appropriately can make the difference between a conversation that is ineffective and divisive and one in which everyone takes advantage of the diversity of thought and opinion for their mutual learning. \n\nConflicted Café? How to Deal with Differences and Tension\n\ncombinations of insight and creativity reveal themselves in ways \n\npeople had not previously imagined. The physical movement and \n\ncross-pollination of ideas in Café dialogues also reduces the com-\n\nmon tendency of participants to "hang onto" their initial posi-\n\ntions and opinions. \n\nSometimes it is not practical for people to move, but this \n\ndoes not mean people can't cross-pollinate ideas. As the Café \n\nhost, you can ask all the participants to write one key insight, \n\nidea, or theme from their table conversation on a large index \n\ncard. Each member then turns in a different direction and \n\nexchanges the card with a person at a nearby table, thus randomly \n\ncross-pollinating insights among table conversations. Members \n\nread aloud the "gifts" they've received to provide creative input \n\nfor a deepening round of conversation. \n\nListen Together for Patterns, Insights, and Deeper Questions \n\nNoticing patterns and connections lies at the heart of knowledge \n\ncreation. Dynamic listening plays a key role in realizing such \n\nbreakthrough discoveries. As the Café host, you can encourage the \n\nkinds of listening that will make insight, innovation, and action \n\nmore likely to occur. At the start of the Café, ask members to \n\nenter the conversation with the goal of learning from each person \n\nat their table. Encourage people to view different perspectives and \n\nassumptions as gifts: even when they make us uncomfortable, \n\nthey offer fertile ground for discovering new possibilities. \n\nBreakthrough thinking occurs most often when people \n\nencourage one another to take their thinking further. Ask partici-\n\npants to give each other their full attention by linking and build-\n\ning on shared ideas rather than going off in random directions or \n\npersonal tangents. Remind folks to listen together for the insights, \n\npatterns, or core questions that underlie the various emerging per-\n\nspectives that no individual member of the group might access \n\nalone. Suggest that they watch for times when it might be helpful \n\nto pause between comments, allowing time for new ideas to sur-\n\nface. Finally, encourage each table to take some time to reflect \n\nduring their inquiry together by asking, What is at the center of \n\nour conversation? \n\n170 The World Café Hosting Guide \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nHarvest and Share Collective Discoveries \n\nAfter several Café rounds, it is helpful to engage in a whole-group \n\nconversation. These town meeting-style conversations are not for-\n\nmal reports or analytical summaries, but a time for mutual reflec-\n\ntion. Give people a few moments of silence to reflect on or jot \n\ndown what they have learned in their travels, what has heart and \n\nmeaning, or what is present now as a result of their conversations. \n\nAsk anyone in the room to share briefly a key idea, theme, or core \n\nquestion that holds real meaning for them personally. Encourage \n\neveryone to notice what discoveries from their own conversations \n\nlink to this initial sharing. \n\nSolicit additional ideas and insights, making sure to balance \n\nnew discoveries with moments of silent reflection--for it is often \n\nin silence that a deeper intelligence, intuitive flash of new knowl-\n\nedge, or a new action possibility is revealed. Make sure key insights \n\nare recorded visually or gathered and posted, if possible. If you \n\nwant to capture the specifics, ask everyone to contribute by writing \n\na core idea or insight on stickies or index cards, which can then be \n\nposted and consolidated for action planning or other purposes. \n\nThe World Café Hosting Guide 171 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nIn most World Café gatherings, participants write or draw ideas on paper tablecloths, enabling other Café participants literally to "see" what they mean. Here are some additional ways ideas from the Café can be harvested and uti-lized in practical ways. \n\n◗ Have a graphic recorder capture the whole-group conversation by drawing the group's ideas on flip charts or a wall mural. These colorful murals act like a big tablecloth for the whole group, enabling people to notice key insights and action opportunities. \n\n◗ Take a gallery tour of the tablecloths. They can be hung on a wall so that members can see the group's ideas on a break as a prelude to posting key insights. \n\n◗ Post your insights. Each participant can write one key insight on a large sticky note and place it on the wall so that everyone can review the ideas during a break. They can be used at the end of a Café for consolidating key themes or action items. \n\n◗ Create idea clusters. Have volunteers group the insights into affinity clusters so that related ideas are visible. This can help a group plan its next steps. \n\n◗ Make a story. Some Cafés create a newspaper or storybook to share the results of their work with larger audiences after the event. Or a graphic recorder will create a picture book, often with digital photos, along with text as documentation for future use. \n\nMaking Collective Knowledge Visible and Identifying Action Priorities\n\n172 The World Café Hosting Guide \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThe room setup described here is an ideal situa-tion. Your venue may not fit this model exactly. However, by using your imagination, you can improvise and design a World Café dialogue process, with or without café tables, that reflects your unique situation and embodies the seven basic operating principles. Be creative! For exam-ple, if tables aren't available, you can arrange chairs in small U-shaped clusters and ask people to form a circle when the conversation begins. Place index cards or pads of paper on the chairs along with felt-tipped pens for noting key ideas. \n\nRoom Setup \n\n◗ A room with natural light and view of outside foliage. If this isn't possible, place plants or flowers around the room to give the Café a natural feeling. \n\n◗ Small round or square tables (approximately 36 to 42 inches in diameter) that can seat four or five. Card tables work as well, although round tables tend to create more of a Café ambiance. Fewer than four at a table may not provide enough diversity of perspective, while more than five limits the amount of personal interaction. \n\n◗ A large enough room so that people can move comfortably among tables and Café hosts can mingle without disturbing seated participants. \n\n◗ Tables spread in a slightly chaotic fashion, not in rows. Create a random distribution around the room. \n\n◗ Checkered or other informal-colored tablecloths. If none exist, then white tablecloths will work. Even just putting pieces of easel or flip chart paper on the table alone will work. \n\nSupplies \n\n◗ Two pieces of white flip chart paper on each table (similar to in cafés, where people often write on the tablecloths). Use more layers if you'll be removing and posting these sheets. Since peo-ple record ideas on the tablecloths, flip charts at individual tables are generally not needed. \n\n◗ Mural or flip chart paper for harvesting and posting collective insights. \n\n◗ Flat wall space for mural paper or two large rolling whiteboards for the work of a graphic recorder. Wall space is also useful for posting the sheets of paper from tables. \n\n◗ A mug or wineglass at each table filled with a variety of colored markers (preferably water-based and nontoxic).
