You can use Crayola watercolor markers, Mr. Sketch, or other pointed felt-tipped pens or markers in darker colors such as red, green, blue, black, and purple. \n\n◗ One small vase with sprigs of fresh flowers on each table. Flowers should be small so they don't obscure the view. Add a small candle as well, if the venue permits it. \n\n◗ One additional Café table set up in the front of the room for the host's and presenters' materials. \n\n◗ A side table for coffee, tea, water, and refresh-ments for participants. \n\n◗ Name tags and chairs for all participants and presenters (with capacity to remove extra chairs). \n\nOptional Equipment \n\n◗ An overhead projector, screen, a table for over-heads, and a digital camera. \n\n◗ A sound system with good speakers that can play both tapes and CDs. \n\n◗ CDs or tapes of mellow jazz or other upbeat music to play as people enter. \n\n◗ Microphones with speakers for Café hosts. Two wireless lavalieres, if needed, and two handheld wireless mikes for town meeting-style sessions. \n\n◗ Two to four flip charts with blank white paper. \n\n◗ Two or more 4 ft. × 6 ft. or 4 ft. × 8 ft. rolling whiteboards or blackboards. \n\n◗ A box containing basic supplies: stapler, paper clips, rubber bands, markers, masking tape, extra pens, pushpins, pencils, and sticky note pads. \n\n◗ Colored 4 in. × 6 in. or 5 in. × 8 in. cards, in colors other than white, if possible--enough cards for each participant to have several for personal note-taking or sharing insights across tables during the gathering. \n\n◗ Bright-colored 4 in. × 6 in. large sticky notes, divided into small packs of twenty-five sheets, one package for each table. You will need these if you are going to ask people to write ideas and post them. \n\nRoom Setup and Supplies\n\nThe World Café Hosting Guide 173 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nHere are generative questions that we and other colleagues have found useful to stimulate new knowledge and creative thinking in a wide vari-ety of situations around the world. Look at these questions to jump-start your own creative thinking about the most appropriate ones for your specific situation. \n\nQuestions for Focusing \n\nCollective Attention \n\n◗ What question, if answered, could make the greatest difference to the future of the situation we're exploring here? \n\n◗ What's important to you about this situation, and why do you care? \n\n◗ What draws you/us to this inquiry? \n\n◗ What's our intention here? What's the deeper purpose--the "big why"--that is worthy of our best effort? \n\n◗ What opportunities can we see in this situation? \n\n◗ What do we know so far/still need to learn about this situation? \n\n◗ What are the dilemmas/opportunities in this situation? \n\n◗ What assumptions do we need to test or challenge in thinking about this situation? \n\n◗ What would someone who had a very differ-ent set of beliefs than we do say about this situation? \n\nQuestions for Connecting Ideas \n\nand Finding Deeper Insight \n\n◗ What's taking shape here? What are we hearing underneath the variety of opinions being expressed? What is in the center of our listening? \n\n◗ What's emerging that is new for you? What new connections are you making? \n\n◗ What have you heard that had real meaning for you? What surprised you? What puzzled or challenged you? What question would you like to ask now? \n\n◗ What is missing from the picture so far? What are we not seeing? Where do we need more clarity? \n\n◗ What has been your major learning or insight so far? \n\n◗ What's the next level of thinking we need to address? \n\n◗ If there was one thing that hasn't yet been said but is needed in order to reach a deeper level of understanding/clarity, what would that be? \n\nQuestions That Create \n\nForward Movement \n\n◗ What would it take to create change on this issue? \n\n◗ What could happen that would enable you/us to feel fully engaged and energized in this situation? \n\n◗ What's possible here and who cares about it? \n\n◗ What needs our immediate attention going forward? \n\n◗ If our success was completely guaranteed, what bold steps might we choose? \n\n◗ How can we support each other in taking the next steps? What unique contribution can we each make? \n\n◗ What challenges might come our way, and how might we meet them? \n\n◗ What conversation, if begun today, could rip-ple out in a way that created new possibilities for the future of [our situation . . . ]? \n\n◗ What seed might we plant together today that could make the most difference to the future of [our situation . . . ]? \n\nQuestions for All Seasons\n\n174 The World Café Hosting Guide \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThe following set of seven integrated World Café design principles, when used in combination, can help to intentionally engage the power of conversation for business and social value. \n\nPrinciples for Hosting \n\nListen Together for \n\nPatterns, Insights, \n\nand Deeper \n\nQuestions \n\nFocus shared attention in ways that nurture coherence of thought without losing individ-ual contribution. \n\nHarvest and Share \n\nCollective Discoveries \n\nMake collective knowledge and insight visible and actionable. \n\nSet the Context \n\nClarify the purpose and broad parameters within which the dialogue will unfold. \n\nCreate Hospitable Space \n\nAssure the welcoming envi-ronment and psychological safety that nurtures personal comfort and mutual respect. \n\nEncourage Everyone's \n\nContribution \n\nEnliven the relationship between the "me" and the "we" by inviting full participation and mutual giving. \n\nCross-Pollinate and \n\nConnect Diverse Perspectives \n\nUse the living system dynamics of emergence through intentionally increasing the diversity of perspec-tives and density of connections while retaining a common focus on core questions. \n\nExplore \n\nQuestions \n\nThat Matter \n\nFocus collective attention on power-ful questions that attract collaborative engagement.\n\nGuiding the Café Process 175 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nQuestions for Reflection \n\nWhat are some of the creative ways you might apply the World Café principles and process to an upcoming conversation that matters in your own life or work? \n\nConsider an upcoming meeting or conversation that you are hosting or helping to host. What are some of the specific things you can do to create a welcoming environment, set the context, and frame key questions for the conversation in a way that opens the possibility for greater collabora-tion, learning, and discovery? \n\nIf you are going to host your conversation as a World Café dialogue, what logistical considerations, personal prepa-ration, or other support do you need to feel comfortable guiding the process? \n\nWhat challenges might you encounter and how can you deal with them? \n\nWhat difference might your becoming more skilled in the art of hosting make to situations you care about in your life, your work, or your community? \n\nGo Forth and Multiply \n\nThe World Café is a continuously evolving practice, refined by \n\nhosts taking the seven Café principles and utilizing them in inno-\n\nvative ways to create exciting breakthroughs in collaborative \n\nthinking and effective action. When dealing with choices or situa-\n\ntions that are not covered in the hosting guide or in other parts \n\nof the book, use your own best experiences with groups and your \n\nintuition about appropriate paths forward to guide you. Conver-\n\nsational leadership is an art, not a science. Use your own creativ-\n\nity not only to design formal Café experiences but to enhance \n\nyour conversational leadership in other aspects of your life and \n\nwork where authentic dialogue makes a difference to positive \n\noutcomes. Try it. . . . You'll like it!\n\n176 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nE L E V E N \n\nA life-affirming leader is one who knows how to rely on \n\nand use the intelligence that exists everywhere in the com-\n\nmunity, the company, the school, or the organization. A \n\nleader these days needs to be a host--one who convenes \n\npeople, who convenes diversity, who convenes all viewpoints \n\nin creative processes where our intelligence can come forth. \n\n--Margaret Wheatley, The Berkana Institute \n\nConversational Leadership: Cultivating Collective Intelligence\n\n177 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat i f conversat ional leadership \n\ni s l ike tending a f ie ld?\n\nIn this chapter, we include four short stories that reveal varied ways \n\nthat the World Café process and principles can serve conversational \n\nleaders in creating valued outcomes. These stories help to highlight key \n\nconversational leadership skills and organizational infrastructures for \n\ncultivating collective intelligence in twenty-first-century organizations \n\nand communities. \n\n178 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nFOSTERING CONVERSATIONAL LEADERSHIP IN EDUCATION: POLK COUNTY SCHOOLS \n\nAs Told By \n\nCarolyn Baldwin \n\nDr. Ray Jorgensen, a respected organizational learning con-\n\nsultant, first introduced the concept of the World Café to \n\nour thirty-person Executive Leadership Team. Sue Miller Hurst, an early member of the MIT Dialogue Project, which did pio-neering research in the dialogue field, later hosted a three-day program with all of our principals at which we experienced the power of dialogue circles. Over a period of several years, we developed learning conversations as a core leadership practice and way of working in our school system--first with the princi-pals, then with the teachers and staff of individual schools. It was a demanding but exhilarating experience! \n\nThe first Café with our Executive Leadership Team was really wonderful. Sitting in circles talking to one another is not a format that has been used much in school bureaucracies, but we found that Café conversations provided a nonthreatening way for people to think together and explore ideas. The Executive Leadership Team met monthly, and over the period of our initial year we started each meeting with a Café dialogue. By the end of the school year we had experienced several Cafés and we became very familiar with the process. Café dialogues became our primary means for talking about significant issues and creating consensus as well as for build-\n\ning trusting relationships among our very diverse leadership team. Based on this experience, the five area superintendents began to take this conversational \n\nway of leading back to our school principals. For example, early in our use of the World Café \n\nCarolyn Baldwin is a former \n\narea superintendent in \n\nFlorida's Polk County public \n\nschools. Over a five-year \n\nperiod, she and her col-\n\nleagues used Café conversa-\n\ntions and dialogue circles to \n\nengage the knowledge and \n\nexperience of the district's \n\n138 school principals and five \n\nthousand teachers in order to \n\nimprove school performance \n\namong the county's eighty-\n\nfour thousand elementary \n\nand secondary school chil-\n\ndren. Carolyn coined the term \n\n"conversational leadership" \n\nto describe their approach to \n\nlarge-scale change. This is \n\nthe story of how it unfolded. \n\nS T O RY\n\nConversational Leadership 179 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\napproach, the principals in my geographic area used Café conversations to develop a long-term plan and a key strategic initiative for improving student achievement. It worked! At that time, the state of Florida had started to "grade" schools. My particular geographic area had many Title 1 schools, meaning schools with a large percentage of low-income families. When we began we had no A schools, three B schools, some C schools, some D schools, and one F school. In the year after my principals began using the World Café to establish learning communities among their teachers, my area schools had no F-grade schools. All of the schools improved their grades by at least one category, and some by more than one. I don't think we'd have achieved these results without the key ideas and actions for improvement that came out of our Café dialogues. \n\nUsing the World Café as part of our conversational leadership approach created other unexpected outcomes. When I began using these conversational methods, the prevailing atti-tude was "I don't want to share my ideas because then other schools will know my secrets for success and my school might lose its competitive position." But through our Café con-versations, the principals began to realize that they were more effective together as a team and as a system than they were alone. I knew that internal competitiveness had truly dimin-ished when a representative from a local shopping mall came to one of our meetings to pitch a competition among our schools during a holiday sales drive--complete with financial incentives for the winning school. When the mall representative left, the principals said, "We don't want to do that this year because it undermines our whole philosophy. We're not just individual schools, we're a system of schools and members of the same team." And I thought to myself, "Wow, this is pretty powerful stuff!" \n\nAfter that breakthrough moment, the principals began to have really deep conversations about the problems in their schools, and to use one another's wisdom to seek positive change. One of the principals had been at the same elementary school for twenty-nine years, and so he had a wealth of experience to share. During his tenure he had seen many initia-tives come and go. Most of his contemporaries had either died or retired. During one of the Café conversations he said, "At first I thought this Café stuff was really stupid--this moving around the room having conversations and sitting in circles for dialogues. I thought, 'I'll just outlast it.' But you know what? I have really found friends again." He became one of the strongest advocates for conversational leadership. \n\nOnce the principals were comfortable with the Café and dialogue circle formats, they brought conversational leadership to their own schools. The teachers discovered that they didn't need a whole lot of training or new materials in order to have productive dialogue; they just needed the focused time to talk together and discover what they already knew about their kids and about what needed to be different. And what they did as a result made a real difference! For example, the kids improved their standardized test scores in both math and reading. There were consistent achievement gains each year of the five-year\n\nTHE RESULTS ARE IN THE RELATIONSHIPS: HEWLETT-PACKARD \n\nAs Told By \n\nBob Veazie \n\nThe first time I experienced a World Café was at a program on \n\nliving systems at the Berkana Institute. At the time, I was an HP \n\nmanufacturing manager, leading about three hundred people. Something profound but disturbing happened to me during those Café conversations. I realized that the boxes on my organization chart might be more accurately depicted as webs of conversations. This realization carried with it the feeling that "managing" these conversations might not be the best way to achieve results. Each day we are engaged in conversations about different questions, just like those table conversations--and we move between the "tables" as we do our work in the company. Seeing the World Café in action hit me with laser beam clarity. This is how life actually works! \n\nSensing the power and potential for networks of conversations and the connections among them to produce real value was dis-concerting to me as a leader. I began to wonder: If our conversations and personal relationships are at the heart of our work, then how am I, \n\nas a leader, contributing to or taking energy away from this natural process? Are we using the intel-ligence of just a few people when we could gain the intelligence of hundreds or thousands by focus-ing on key questions and including people more intentionally? These questions haunted me. \n\nBob Veazie, a senior engineer \n\nwith Hewlett-Packard, focuses \n\non organizational perform-\n\nance. He has led system-wide \n\nshifts in safety and quality, \n\nand is now focusing his \n\nefforts on the customer expe-\n\nrience. Here Bob talks about \n\nhow he applied the World \n\nCafé pattern and principles in \n\nhis role as the conversational \n\nleader of a major safety \n\nimprovement effort affecting \n\nfifty thousand employees \n\nover a four-year period. \n\n180 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nproject. And discipline problems dramatically decreased in the area schools, as demon-strated by a reduction in school suspensions and disciplinary measures. \n\nI think what really happens in Café conversations is that professional titles and other things that usually separate people fall away. The Café is almost like a time-out, where every-one can step back from themselves as actors in the system and reflect on it together. People begin to see that things can be improved and changed by the combined wisdom and per-spectives of all the people who work there, whatever their formal roles. Through this process, we enhanced our capacity to co-create together. That's what I found most rewarding about my conversational leadership experience in the Polk County public schools. \n\n. . . \n\nS T O RY\n\nConversational Leadership 181 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nAbout a year and a half after that first Café, I became the safety leader for a small produc-tion area of Hewlett-Packard's inkjet operations in Corvallis, Oregon. The job quickly evolved to include HP's worldwide inkjet manufacturing business--about fifteen thousand employees at five sites around the world, including Ireland and Puerto Rico. Later, as I collaborated with the corporate-wide safety group, our approach expanded to other manufacturing sites and HP business units, a total of about fifty thousand people throughout the company. \n\nWhen we started, there was a high accident rate. In Corvalis, for example, 6.2 per hun-dred members of our workforce were hurt every year. In Puerto Rico the percentage was 4.1 percent. In Ireland it was 2.5 to 3 percent. Those are big numbers! The first year I worked on safety, we used Du Pont's program called STOP, an approach where people give each other feedback about how well they are doing relative to a predetermined set of risks. Our test group loved it for the first couple of months, and then they began hating it because it was somebody else's list of risks--they had not generated their own ideas about what their own risks really were. In other words, we didn't start with a question that evoked people's own curiosity and creativity. We started with someone else's answer. \n\nIn my second year, we dropped Du Pont's program and put together a small group of full-time internal safety experts, called "safety change agents," who defined our own set of risks for the whole organization. Here's where we made our second mistake. I told myself that by doing the work internally I was congruent with the principles of the World Café, since one assumption of the World Café is that much of the wisdom lies with the people them-selves. But in reality, we had created a small group that still functioned like outside experts or a management committee. We weren't being mindful of yet another a key principle of the World Café: the importance of encouraging everyone's contribution. \n\nSo we changed our approach. We began to ask ourselves, What are the few key questions that would improve safety results if we were to ask them to people already in conversations about their daily work? We didn't present those questions in the form of World Café events. In fact, we've never actually designed or hosted a formal World Café event. Instead, it dawned on us that what we needed to do was pose key safety questions to people in the already-existing but invisible "HP Café"--the current web of relationships--so they could integrate the ques-tions into the conversations they were already having. \n\nWe began by meeting with people where they normally gathered--in staff meetings, in worker assemblies, and on the shop floor. We'd start by sharing their local facility's safety record with them. Then we'd show them the funnel diagram that Juanita had shared with us at that first World Café I attended. Thousands of people saw that funnel slide! It helped peo-ple visualize more clearly the powerful pattern of the World Café in action and opened the door to talking about how change actually happens in large systems (see next page). It also helped employees grasp that we were making a shift; rather than using predetermined train-ing programs that focused only on solutions, we were trusting that their own conversations, relationships, and mutual intelligence were the way to deal with critical safety questions.\n\n182 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nSo, first we asked, "If you were to get hurt, how would that happen?" People began answer-ing the question with risks that they identified from their own work situations. Then we'd ask the second question: "Do you want to manage these risks before people get hurt or after?" And of course they'd say, "Before." Then we'd ask the final question: "Great! What do you want to do about it?" At that point, we didn't have to facilitate any-more. We had invited them into in a meaningful conversation called "I don't want to get hurt at work." We'd talk together about methodologies-- their own creative approaches for managing those risks. And finally we'd say, "Try out the answers you've come up with. Keep asking the questions, and revisit your answers as you learn more." \n\nThroughout the whole development of the safety effort, I held a guiding image of the World Café as the deeper pattern for what was happening in the organization. Each of the employee meetings I attended was like a table in this large, ongoing \n\nSafety Café--this network of conversations. The tables all over the company were connected by the key questions, just as they are at an actual World Café event. \n\nOur group of full-time safety folks became the nucleus of hosts. Four or five of us began "table hopping" between key sites around the world, some on multiple occasions. We began to share the stories of our travels with the employees at the different sites. We also brought people together from product lines across the company to learn with and from each other. As we were leading the safety effort we were simultaneously learning about how conversa-tion as a core business process really works to enhance performance. \n\nAnd talk about performance! For example, during our effort the recordable injury rate at our Corvallis, Oregon, site was dramatically reduced--from 6.2 to 1.2 per hundred. Puerto Rico went from 4.1 percent to .2 percent, among the world's best. The company as a whole was able to reduce the overall accident rate by approximately 33 percent. Today there isn't a week that goes by without a problem being discussed and people asking, "How can we approach this like we did with safety?" \n\nNonetheless, even with these impressive results, I still have a key question I'd love oth-ers' thoughts about. In the several years since the group of safety hosts and I were reassigned to other roles because we'd achieved such outstanding results, the safety rate has begun inch-ing back up, particularly at the original Corvallis site. It's now about 2.5 per hundred--still\n\nS T O RY \n\nConversational Leadership 183 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nLIVING STRATEGY: DISCOVERING THE FUTURE, TOGETHER: AMERICAN SOCIETY FOR QUALITY \n\nAs Told By \n\nArian Ward, with Paul Borawski and Ken Case \n\nWho are we? \n\nWhy are we here? \n\nHow can we make the biggest difference? \n\nThese seem to be universal questions that matter to us at the \n\npersonal level. But what would it mean if these were also the \n\nquestions that mattered most to an organization and its stake-holders? That's what we embarked on a journey to find out as we developed a "living strategy" approach to discovering ASQ's future. At the heart of our work was a commitment to creating a dynamic story of the shared aspirations, future direction, and strategic outcomes for ASQ and the community it supports. We wanted to create a process through which this story could con-tinuously evolve, through expanding the network of ongoing strategic conversations among all members of the community. We hoped that, over time, the story would become woven into the fabric of the organization through a continuous process of reflection and sense-making. \n\nAn exciting experiment using \n\nWorld Café principles to \n\ndesign an inquiring system \n\nhas been underway at the \n\nAmerican Society for Quality \n\n(ASQ), led by Paul Borawski, \n\nexecutive director, and Ken \n\nCase, volunteer president. \n\nASQ is the largest quality \n\nassociation in the world, with \n\nover 130,000 members in fifty \n\ncountries on six continents. \n\nArian Ward, of Community \n\nFrontiers, is a pioneer in \n\nengaging the World Café with \n\nmembership associations and \n\ncommunity groups. \n\nan improvement of more than 50 percent from the original 6.2 per hundred, but not as out-standing as Puerto Rico, which remains a world leader at .2 percent years later. \n\nWhat's different about the two sites? In Puerto Rico they continued their own internal system of hosting conversations on safety issues, whereas in Corvallis continuous hosting of safety conversations became less intensive. This leaves me wondering: In a world that is always dynamic and changing, what kind and level of conversational leadership is needed to create long-term sustainable performance on any key issue of systemic importance, whether it be safety, qual-ity, new product development, or on a large scale, the sustainability of life on our fragile planet? . . .\n\n184 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat prompted this new way of thinking was a belief that continued success would not come simply from doing traditional planning better. As Paul Borawski, ASQ's executive direc-tor, explains, "I was personally looking for a planning approach that could help make the connection between the mindset and the 'heartset' of our board and members. I was seeking a way to unleash the spirit of our members around the deeper meaning of quality, not only in the workplace but in the larger society as well." \n\nThe first meeting that employed the World Café methodology--conducted with the board's Strategic Planning Committee--was definitely a risk! Unlike past sessions, this two-day meeting was not designed to produce a traditional one-page plan. Instead, the goal was to develop key strategic questions that called for further exploration. "I think if anybody who knew us had listened to what we planned to do with Cafés they would have laughed and said that it wouldn't work," Paul recalls. "But you should have seen the room come to life with the first set of Café questions! This session turned ASQ strategically inside out. The commit-tee began to ask questions that they concluded weren't theirs to answer. Some needed to go to the full board; others needed the input of the full membership. The whole thing started to blossom into a realization that many more voices were needed to discover the answers to the key strategic questions raised at that initial session." \n\nAs a result of this and subsequent strategic dialogues, ASQ's strategic planning process has evolved into an ongoing inquiry spreading out from a small group of people (the Strategic Planning Committee), to a larger group (the board), to many other stakeholders. Members of the ASQ board and staff have now actively hosted Cafés on key strategic questions for ASQ members, former members, non-quality professionals who are using quality tools in their work, executives who need to leverage quality in their organizations, and people from the service sector where formal quality tools haven't traditionally been employed. Strategic ques-tions and storytelling based on the outcomes from previous Cafés serve as the connectors for linking the evolving collective intelligence from these growing networks of conversation. \n\nSupported by ASQ staff, who help coordinate Café events and organize the feedback and collective intelligence gathered from the Cafés, this approach also involves creating "sense and respond" opportunities throughout ASQ's far-flung membership. A growing number of mem-bers of the global quality community use Cafés and other forms of strategic dialogue to explore what's happening in their environment. They provide ongoing feedback to the association, which helps with collective sense-making in relation to emerging issues and opportunities. \n\nKen Case, the ASQ president, provides a rich image of this living process for creating an inquiring system: "It's an ongoing dialogue spreading in waves. We're trying to ask the right questions--which is really an art as well as a science. Our purpose is to cause people to dig deeply and to think together about who we are, what we are about, and what we want to be. Based on the key questions, you can see the ripples going out. And, if you notice, those rip-ples come back too: pearls of ideas suggested by our outer constituencies that get fed back to\n\nConversational Leadership 185 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nthe board. That's the beautiful dynamic of this approach. We hear rich discussion going on that we haven't heard in any prior year of participation on the ASQ board." \n\nASQ's ripples-in-a-pond living strategy process uses Café dialogues to engage an expand-ing web of conversations around its most important questions. We're learning that true strategic leadership is about seeding these conversations with the right questions, hosting the ongoing dialogue, and helping the organization make sense of and take action in response to the insights and opportunities that emerge. It's not easy to change traditional ways. Paul Borawski shares that: "Our transformation is young and fragile, but it has begun. What's exciting is the knowledge that living systems thinking is transforming a community deeply rooted in mechanistic traditions." Through this approach we're slowly discovering ways to create engagement environments that work in concert with the organization as a living sys-tem to co-evolve our future in ways that are worthy of our best effort. \n\n. . . \n\nPerspectives & Observations \n\nT \n\nhe first time I encountered Alan Webber in person was in \n\nJanuary 1995, when I welcomed him at the door of our \n\nhome for the strategic dialogue on intellectual capital, the \n\nevening before the World Café was born in our living room. But \n\nthe former managing editor of the Harvard Business Review and \n\nsoon to be co-founder of Fast Company magazine was no stranger \n\nto me. Unbeknownst to Alan, he was one of David's and my \n\nheroes. \n\nTwo years earlier, Alan had written an HBR article, "What's So \n\nNew About the New Economy?" (1993, p. 28) where he posited-- \n\nmay I add, somewhat before his time--that in the new economy, \n\nwhere ideas and information are key currencies of exchange, con-\n\nversation is the mother of invention. Contrary to popular opinion, \n\nhe said, it's not having new technology platforms that create busi-\n\nness value. Rather, Alan argued, it's flesh-and-blood people in con-\n\nversation, learning from each other, sharing their knowledge and \n\nexperience, who create the innovations and solutions that make or \n\nbreak the organization. Alan was unequivocal in his belief that\n\n"the most important work in the new economy is creating conver-\n\nsations". In today's world, he stressed, it becomes the leader's pri-\n\nmary responsibility to facilitate the kind of collaborative \n\nenvironment, mutual trust, and authentic conversations that \n\nenable the organization to access the collective intelligence of its \n\nmembers. It was Alan's groundbreaking article that \n\ngave David and me the confidence to believe we \n\nwere on the right track in our early musings about \n\nthe centrality of conversation as a generative force \n\nin our lives and work. \n\nWhile it was Alan who pointed to conversa-\n\ntion as the lifeblood of the knowledge era, it was \n\nFlorida educator and Café host Carolyn Baldwin \n\nwho coined the term conversational leadership to \n\ndescribe the leader's intentional use of conversa-\n\ntion as a core process to cultivate the collective \n\nintelligence needed to create business and social \n\nvalue. Yet even today, conversational leadership is \n\nnot taught in most leadership development pro-\n\ngrams. How can we begin to cultivate both the \n\norganizational infrastructures and personal lead-\n\nership capabilities that are needed to access and \n\nact on the wisdom that already exists in our \n\norganizations and communities? Although we're still at an early \n\nstage in our exploration of conversational leadership, here are \n\nsome initial areas to consider based on the stories in this book \n\nand our own research-in-action. We'd welcome your thoughts and \n\ninsights as well. \n\nEngage Strategic Questions \n\nMastering the art and architecture of powerful questions that evoke \n\nknowledge-sharing, inspire strategic dialogue, and invite commit-\n\nted action is a critical personal leadership skill (Vogt and others, \n\n2003). Strategic questions create forward movement and new \n\npossibilities for collective discovery. But they can also serve as the \n\n"glue" that holds together overlapping webs \n\n186 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nHow can we begin to \n\ncultivate both the organ-\n\nizational infrastructures \n\nand personal leadership \n\ncapabilities that are \n\nneeded to access and act \n\non the wisdom that \n\nalready exists in our organ-\n\nizations and communities? \n\n(continued on p. 189)\n\nS T O RY \n\nConversational Leadership 187 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nDESIGNING ORGANIZATIONAL INFRASTRUCTURES FOR DIALOGUE: PHILIP MORRIS USA \n\nAs Told By \n\nMike Szymanczyk \n\nI'm an architect at heart. If you want to use conversation as a \n\ncore process, then you have to be intentional about designing \n\nthe infrastructures that will evoke people's capacity for thinking together in new ways. It's one thing to have the personal leader-ship skills to host a great conversation. It's another to create an organizational architecture that channels how people collabo-rate to discover their mutual intelligence. I've found that ordi-nary people in a good infrastructure will create better results than their more brilliant counterparts operating in a poorly designed system. \n\nWe've done a number of things over the years to create infra-structures for collaborative dialogue and engagement. Some of our choices might seem "outside the box," but we've found they've made a real difference. For example, very early on we invented a strategy process, the Game Plan, based on collabora-tive dialogue and inquiry that is used organization-wide. It focuses on discovering the big questions at the heart of shaping the future and on creating initiatives that respond to those critical strategic questions. \n\nWe also regularly utilize a variety of conversational architectures and creative meet-ing formats that foster collaborative thinking and innovative solutions. As part of our large-scale change effort we've introduced World Café conversations, dialogue circles, Open Space sessions, scenario planning, outdoor experiential learning, and even dra-matic theater presentations to stimulate dialogue and breakthrough thinking around critical issues. We also use graphic recording and visual language as a key resource to help people think more systemically, connect ideas, and surface difficult concerns. \n\nIntroducing other infrastructures for learning, information sharing, and dialogue have enabled us to create organizational venues for thinking more deeply together about key strategic questions. For example, my regular full-day senior team meetings and three-times-per-year off-site senior team sessions, our mission goals conversa-tions with cross-functional groups, and our town hall-style gatherings with employ-ees all help surface the organization's collective intelligence around key questions. \n\nAs the current chairman and \n\nCEO of Philip Morris USA, \n\nMike Szymanczyk is leading \n\nhis company through the \n\nchallenges of realigning with \n\nsociety and reinventing its \n\nfuture. Collaborative dia-\n\nlogue around critical strate-\n\ngic questions lies at the heart \n\nof this evolution toward a \n\nmore positive future. Here \n\nMike talks of the conversa-\n\ntional infrastructures being \n\nused to engage the organiza-\n\ntion's best thinking.\n\n188 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nPutting younger leaders with diverse perspectives into exploration teams that reach out to key stakeholders has also brought fresh thinking to important emerging issues. \n\nNot long ago the company moved its headquarters from a skyscraper in New York City to a tree-filled campus in Richmond, Virginia. The design of the building encour-ages greater cross-fertilization of ideas and a more collaborative environment. Our sen-ior team, who helped design the space, wanted places where people could gather informally to talk together, so we put a large coffee bar and café in our lobby area. Peo-ple pass through the café area whenever they enter or leave the building. Throughout the day people will say, 'Let's meet downstairs for a coffee.'" We co-located groups that need to work closely together and created comfortable living rooms with whiteboards on each floor. There's also a library and special meeting room designed for Café conversa-tions on the ground floor with plenty of wall space for graphic recording that groups can reserve for their "think meetings." We've created special meeting spaces with double-screen technology to support conversations at a distance in ways that allow us both to see our colleagues from different sites and to work with visual materials related to the questions or projects we're exploring. And most offices also have a view to the outdoors and natural light. \n\nAs you can imagine, all of this is a big change from New York--and it's already hav-ing a significant organizational payoff. People are now making more connections across functional boundaries, and we're seeing a greater diversity of perspectives and richness of thought on key projects. Hosting regular community gatherings with local stake-holders in our new building is also bringing in fresh points of view. \n\nBy intentionally designing these conversational infrastructures, we're able to engage both our own employees and key external stakeholders in thinking more creatively about what a tobacco manufacturer needs to do to meet society's expectations of a responsible corporation. I believe that engaging in collaborative dialogue, along with the other positive steps we are taking to reinvent our future, are slowly but surely enabling us to distinguish ourselves as a leader in our industry. \n\n. . .\n\nof conversations through which diverse resources can combine and \n\nrecombine to create innovative solutions. As Bob Veazie discovered \n\nin his safety work at Hewlett-Packard: "It was the questions them-\n\nselves, coupled with the invitation to explore them, that moved \n\npeople from compliant behavior to committed performance. \n\nManagers would ask me, 'How can results come from just asking \n\nour people questions? I want answers, not questions!' But we \n\nfound that results do come from the questions. The results lie in \n\nthe personal relationships, the knowledge, and the mutual caring \n\nthat gets strengthened in people's conversations together about the \n\nquestions, along with the discovery of their own answers." \n\nEspecially in a volatile and uncertain business environment, \n\nanother important leadership opportunity lies in creating conver-\n\nsational infrastructures--like the Philip Morris USA Game Plan \n\nprocess--that encourage members at all levels to discover their \n\nown key strategic questions. For example, when leaders are willing \n\nto ask, What is the question that, if explored deeply, could give us a \n\nreal breakthrough? people's own collective wisdom often reveals a \n\npath less traveled that shows an innovative way forward. \n\nConvene and Host Learning Conversations \n\nA core aspect of the new work of leadership involves convening \n\nand hosting gatherings to engage productive dialogue around \n\nchallenging questions. As educator Linda Lambert and her col-\n\nleagues point out, "Leading the conversations is not a neutral \n\nrole: it is a role of active involvement. . . . Leaders need to pose \n\nthe questions and convene the conversations that invite others to \n\nbecome involved. . . . Developing meaningful dialogue is about \n\ncreating conceptual fields that deepen or shift thinking" (Lambert \n\nand others, 1995, pp. 101, 105). \n\nAuthentic conversation that deepens a group's thinking and \n\nevokes collaborative intelligence is less likely to occur in a climate \n\nof fear, mistrust, and hierarchical control. When the human mind \n\nand heart are fully engaged in exploring questions that matter, new \n\nknowledge often begins to surface. To succeed, leaders need to \n\nstrengthen their personal skills in hosting dialogue and other \n\napproaches that deepen mutual inquiry. \n\nConversational Leadership 189 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nThese capabilities include: \n\n◗ Creating a climate of discovery \n\n◗ Suspending premature judgment \n\n◗ Exploring underlying assumptions and beliefs \n\n◗ Listening for unexpected connections between ideas \n\n◗ Encouraging the expression of a wide range of perspectives \n\n◗ Articulating shared understandings \n\nOther aspects of effective hosting are also key, including \n\nclarifying the larger context, ensuring a welcoming environment, \n\nencouraging everyone's contribution, and managing divergent \n\nviewpoints. Your personal authenticity, integrity, and values \n\nbecome increasingly central to establishing your credibility as a \n\nlegitimate host and convener who can inspire trust and foster col-\n\nlaboration among diverse constituencies. \n\nInvite Diverse Perspectives \n\nHow often do you hear a leader pose the question, Whose voices need \n\nto be included in this conversation? Who's not here who should be? Not \n\noften. Yet cultivating conversational leadership requires leaders to \n\nbecome active connectors--of diverse people and stimulating ideas. \n\nJust as an individual World Café conversation can \n\ncreate a rich web of unexpected insights through \n\nthe cross-pollination of ideas and the creation of \n\nnew personal networks, as a conversational leader \n\nyou can foster creative insights and new collegial \n\nnetworks by convening strategic conversations \n\nacross traditional boundaries. As Gary Hamel of \n\nthe London School of Economics points out, \n\n"Strategizing depends on creating a rich and \n\ncomplex web of conversations that cuts across \n\npreviously isolated pockets of knowledge and creates new and \n\nunexpected combinations of insight" (1997, p. 9). This means \n\nengaging diverse voices inside the organization, including those of \n\nyounger people who are often not considered "ready" to be part of \n\nthe inner circle by senior leadership. It also means convening and \n\n190 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nYou can foster creative \n\ninsights and new collegial \n\nnetworks by convening \n\nstrategic conversations \n\nacross traditional boundaries\n\nhosting learning conversations with key outside constituencies, \n\nincluding customers, suppliers, nongovernmental organizations \n\n(NGOs), community members, and others who have a stake in your \n\norganization's future. \n\nSupport Appreciative Inquiry \n\nSearching for innovative possibilities requires today's leaders to \n\nshift from focusing primarily on what is not working and how to \n\nfix it to discovering and appreciating what is working and how to \n\nleverage it. Appreciative Inquiry (AI), developed by David Cooper-\n\nrider and his colleagues at Case Western University, is a powerful \n\nconversational process for valuing previously untapped sources of \n\nknowledge, vitality, and energy (Cooperrider and others, 2003; \n\nWhitney and Trosten-Bloom, 2003; Watkins and Mohr, 2001). AI \n\nstimulates lively conversations about what the organization does \n\nwell and uncovers its hidden assets. By focusing on aspects of the \n\ndesired future that are already being lived today, AI enables lead-\n\ners to foster networks of conversation that can leverage emerging \n\npossibilities rather than simply fix past mistakes. \n\nThis approach to organizational change creates a generative \n\nfield of mutual trust and excitement about new opportunities. \n\nWhile taking an AI stance is not a "normal" approach for leaders \n\nwho are paid to solve problems, we are finding that this simple \n\nshift of lens lies at the heart of the kind of conversational leader-\n\nship that can bring out the best in an organization or community. \n\nFoster Shared Understanding \n\nIn today's complex environment, leaders are \n\ndiscovering that one of their unique contributions \n\nis to provide conceptual leadership--creating shared \n\ncontexts and common frameworks in which groups \n\ncan deepen or shift their thinking together. We \n\nmake meaning of our experiences through the \n\nlanguage we use, the stories we share, and the \n\nimages we favor. For example, holding the image of \n\nyour organization as a battlefield, where members \n\ncarry out "preemptive strikes" and "decimate the \n\nConversational Leadership 191 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWe make meaning of our \n\nexperiences through the \n\nlanguage we use, the \n\nstories we share, and the \n\nimages we favor\n\ncompetition" evokes very different behavior than a shared under-\n\nstanding of the organization as a dynamic web of conversations and \n\npersonal relationships, part of a living system that includes key inter-\n\nnal and external stakeholders--at times even those you've tradition-\n\nally thought of as "the competition." Conversational leaders also put \n\ntime and attention into framing a common language and articulating \n\ncompelling scenarios--stories of the future--that can shape collective \n\npurpose and provide direction for organizational conversations. \n\nFostering shared understanding means creating conversa-\n\ntional infrastructures that carve out the time for true listening, \n\nthoughtful reflection, and mutual sense-making. Thoughtful lis-\n\ntening and disciplined collective reflection provide the founda-\n\ntion for creating productive conversations and shared \n\nunderstanding about how the organization's results are actually \n\ncreated, and how they can be improved. As Dennis Sandow and \n\nAnne Murray Allen, who have both worked with HP's Bob Veazie, \n\nexplain, "With multiple experiences of shared understanding, we \n\nbegin to see the flow of knowledge and performance in social sys-\n\ntems, much as one begins to see the flow in a successful musical, \n\ntheatrical, or sports performance" (2004, p. 9). \n\nHonor Social Networks and Nurture Communities of Practice \n\nMany of the most provocative questions and leading-edge prac-\n\ntices that are vital to a creating sustainable value are discovered by \n\naccident on the front lines, in the middle of the action, in the \n\nconversations of everyday life. But this wisdom is often lost. Most \n\nleaders rarely notice, honor, and utilize the learning and knowl-\n\nedge creation that is woven into the informal conversations, per-\n\nsonal networks, relationships, and practices that are already part \n\nof the organization's social fabric (Sandow and Allen, 2004). \n\nEtienne Wenger's pioneering work on communities of prac-\n\ntice offers many directions to take to engage the wisdom residing \n\nin these informal knowledge networks (Wenger, 1998; Wenger \n\nand others, 2002). Thoughtful leaders will pay special attention to \n\nensuring that new work processes or redesigned organizational \n\nstructures do not inadvertently destroy the ongoing conversations \n\n192 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nand knowledge that are woven into these informal webs of rela-\n\ntionships. You can also provide informal meeting space, intranet \n\nknowledge-sharing infrastructures, or knowledge stewards who \n\nhelp facilitate the development of active communities of practice \n\nand learning conversations among people with the special expert-\n\nise needed to address long-term organizational needs. \n\nRethink Training and Development \n\nMost training and development efforts have been founded on \n\ndeeply embedded assumptions about learning as an individual \n\nendeavor, and on the separation of training activities from the \n\npractical realities of action on the front line. You may remember \n\nyour own early education, where the adage was "Listen to the \n\nteacher and don't talk to your neighbor." In today's organizations \n\nand communities, where continuous learning and knowledge-\n\nsharing are important, conversational leaders are rethinking train-\n\ning and development. Recognizing the social nature of learning, \n\nthey are reallocating resources away from traditional training pro-\n\ngrams to support the evolution of communities of practice, invest-\n\ning in experientially based collaborative learning through peer \n\ndialogue, and designing interactive technology infrastructures to \n\nsupport learning conversations rather than simply store data. \n\nSupport Collaborative Technologies \n\nIntranet and groupware technologies are now making it possible \n\nfor widely dispersed work groups to participate in learning con-\n\nversations and team projects across time and space. As these \n\ntools become even more widely available, the notion of conver-\n\nsational leadership will expand to include supporting widespread \n\nonline conversations through which members can contribute \n\ntheir own questions and best thinking to critical strategic issues \n\nand ongoing learning opportunities. For example, Buckman Lab-\n\noratories has created a worldwide intranet, K'Netix, that enables \n\nits employees in more than one hundred countries around the \n\nworld to engage in ongoing learning conversations about cus-\n\ntomer needs and solutions. Users can contribute to the conversa-\n\ntion at any time, from any place, and in several different \n\nConversational Leadership 193 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .\n\nlanguages. The system updates the evolving "knowledge threads" \n\nas questions are explored and solutions discovered. Far-flung \n\nemployees who would normally never have the chance to be a \n\npart of the conversations are often the ones who provide key \n\ninsights and expertise. K'Netix has become the central nervous \n\nsystem for the company's knowledge-sharing conversations (Pra-\n\nhalad and Ramaswamy, 2004). \n\nThese types of conversational infrastructures, along with com-\n\nplementary approaches like graphic recording and visual map-\n\nping, create possibilities for individuals and groups to be in \n\nconversation in ways that were previously unimaginable. Leaders \n\nwho recognize their strategic importance and support their use \n\nwill be at a significant advantage. \n\nDesign Welcoming Places and Spaces \n\nAs we initially shared in chapter 4, famed architect Christopher \n\nAlexander points to the deep human yearning for both physical \n\nand psychological environments that embody the "quality that has \n\nno name"--a special quality of aliveness, wholeness, and comfort \n\nthat people recognize whenever they experience it, even though it \n\nis hard to describe. Designing or selecting physical and social envi-\n\nronments that are alive and comfortable as a context for thinking \n\ntogether has not been high on most executives' list of strategic pri-\n\norities. However, as our last decade of experience with the World \n\nCafé and Mike Szymanczyk's story in this chapter demonstrate, \n\ncreating physical spaces that embody the quality that has no name \n\nis important for conversational leaders to consider in stimulating \n\ninnovative thinking and cultivating collective intelligence. \n\nCo-Evolve the Future \n\nOur challenging times require thoughtful consideration of difficult \n\neconomic, social, and environmental questions. Leaders who \n\ncultivate their personal conversational skills and at the same time \n\nare able to design and implement conversational infrastructures \n\ntailored to the unique needs of their own organization or commu-\n\nnity will be in increasing demand. Seeing the ways in which conver-\n\nsation can help an organization create its future--and utilizing \n\n194 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nprocesses, principles, tools, and technologies that support this \n\nevolution--is everyone's job. We each have the opportunity to exer-\n\ncise conversational leadership in our lives and work. This is one \n\npowerful way that organizations and communities can cultivate \n\nboth the knowledge required to thrive today and the wisdom \n\nneeded to ensure a sustainable future for our children and grand-\n\nchildren. \n\nConversational Leadership 195 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nQuestions for Reflection \n\nHow well does your organization foster conversational leadership \n\nand engage conversation as a core process? What might you do to \n\nfoster a greater appreciation of its possibilities in your own set-\n\nting? Consider the following questions: \n\nTo what extent does your organization consider conversation to be the heart of "real work"? How often do the leaders and members attend to the principles and practices of good conversation as they engage with key stakeholders? \n\nHow much importance do you place on your role as convener or host for good conversations about questions that matter? \n\nHow much time do you and your colleagues spend discovering the right questions in relation to the time spent finding the right answers? \n\nWhat enabling infrastructures, processes, or tools are being systematically used to support good conversations and knowledge sharing in your organization or community? \n\nHow often do your meetings and conferences include well-designed opportunities for collaborative dialogue, peer exchange, and interactive learning? \n\nTo what extent is your physical work space or office area designed to encourage the informal interaction that sup-ports good conversation and effective learning? \n\nHow much of your training and development budget is devoted to supporting informal learning conversations and shar-ing effective practices across organizational boundaries?\n\n196 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nT W E LV E \n\nConversation is at the heart of the new inquiry. It is \n\nperhaps the core human capacity for dealing with the \n\ntremendous challenges we face. To engage in great \n\ncivilization we need to ask questions that matter. We \n\ncannot afford to spend our time on issues that can't hold \n\nour attention, that don't touch our hearts. The culture \n\nof conversation is a different culture, one that could make \n\na difference in the future of our world. \n\n--Institute for the Future, In Good Company: \n\nInnovation at the Intersection of Technology and Sustainability \n\nThe Call of Our Times: Creating a Culture of Dialogue\n\n197 \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWhat i f a conversat ion \n\nbegun today could r ipple out and \n\ncreate new poss ibi l i t ies?\n\n198 The World Café \n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . \n\nWHAT DOES IT MEAN TO CARE? CREATING A LEARNING CULTURE \n\nTHROUGH DIALOGUE: SINGAPORE \n\nAs Told By \n\nSamantha Tan \n\nNo one thought Singapore would survive once it separated \n\nfrom Malaysia in 1965. We're
